of the Banach algebra $L^\infty(E)$ onto a closed normal subalgebra $\mathcal{A}$ of $B(\mathcal{H})$, which is related to $E$ by
\[
\braket{\Psi(f)x | y} = \int_\Omega f \,\dif E_{x,y}
\]
for every $f \in L^\infty(E)$ and every $x,y \in \mathcal{H}$.
This justifies the notation $\Psi(f) = \int_\Omega f \,\dif E$.
Moreover,
\[
\|\Psi(f)x\|^2 = \int_\Omega |f|^2 \,\dif E_{x,x}
\]
for every $f \in L^\infty(E)$ and every $x \in \mathcal{H}$
and
an operator $Q \in B(\mathcal{H})$ commutes with every $E(\omega)$ if and only if $Q$ commutes with every $\Psi(f)$.

Recall that a \emph{normal} subalgebra $\mathcal{A}$ of $B(\mathcal{H})$ is a commutative one which contains $T^*$ for every $T \in \mathcal{A}$.
To say that $\Psi$ is a *-isomorphism means that $\Psi$ is one-to-one, linear, multiplicative and that $\Psi(f^*) = \Psi(f)^*$ for every $f \in L^\infty(E)$.
\end{theorem}


The principal assertion of \autoref{thm:spectral-theorem} is that every bounded normal operator $T$ on a Hilbert space induces (in a canonical way) a resolution $E$ of the identity on the Borel subsets of its spectrum $\sigma(T)$ and that $T$ can be reconstructed from $E$ by an integral of the type discussed in \autoref{thm:integration-projection-valued-measure}. 
A large part of the theory of normal operators depends on this fact.

\begin{theorem}[Spectral theorem]\label{thm:spectral-theorem}
If $T \in B(\mathcal{H})$ and $T$ is normal (\autoref{def:normal-hermitian-unitary-projection}), then there exists a unique resolution of the identity $E$ (\autoref{def:resolution-of-identity}) on the Borel subsets of $\sigma(T)$ (\autoref{def:spectrum}) such that
\begin{equation}\label{eq:spectral-theorem}
% \boxed{
T = \int_{\sigma(T)} \lambda \,\dif E(\lambda)
% }
\end{equation}
% where 
% \[
% \sigma(T) = \{\lambda \in \C : T - \lambda \id \text{ is not invertible}\}
% \]
% is the spectrum of $T$.
Moreover, every projection $E(\omega)$ commutes with every $S \in B(\mathcal{H})$ which commutes with $T$.
\end{theorem}

We call $E$ the \emph{spectral decomposition} of $T$.

Sometimes it is convenient to think of $E$  as being defined for all Borel sets in $\C$;
to achieve this put $E(\omega) = 0$ if $\omega \cap \sigma(T) = \emptyset$.



\begin{notation}[Symbolic Calculus for Normal Operators]
If $E$ is the spectral decomposition of a normal operator $T \in B(\mathcal{H})$, and if $f$ is a bounded Borel function on $\sigma(T)$, it is customary to denote the operator
\(
\Psi(f) = \int_{\sigma(T)} f \,\dif E
\)
by $f(T)$.
\end{notation}
% Using this notation, parts of \autoref{thm:integration-projection-valued-measure} and \autoref{thm:spectral-theorem} can be summarized as follows:


\begin{theorem}\label{thm:normal-bounded-operator-spectral-properties}
A normal $T \in B(\mathcal{H})$ is 
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item hermitian if and only if $\sigma(T)$ lies on the real axis
\item unitary if and only if $\sigma(T)$ lies on the unit circle
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}












\subsection{Unbounded operators}

Let $\mathcal H$ be a Hilbert space.
By an \emph{operator} in $\mathcal H$ we mean a linear map
\(
T \colon D(T) \to \mathcal H
\),
where the \emph{domain} $D(T)$ is a linear subspace of $\mathcal H$.
% Its \emph{range} is
% \(
% R(T) \coloneq \{Tx : x \in D(T)\}
% \).
If $D(T)$ is dense in $\mathcal H$, we say that $T$ is \hl{\emph{densely defined}}.

% \begin{definition}
The \emph{graph} of an operator $T$ is
\(
G(T) \coloneq \{(x,Tx) : x \in D(T)\} \subseteq \mathcal H \times \mathcal H
\).
The operator $T$ is said to be \emph{closed} if $G(T)$ is closed in $\mathcal H \times \mathcal H$.
It is said to be \emph{closable} if the closure of $G(T)$ is itself the graph of an operator.
% \end{definition}

Equivalently, $T$ is closed if whenever $x_n \in D(T)$, $x_n \to x$ in $\mathcal H$, and $Tx_n \to y$ in $\mathcal H$, then $x \in D(T)$ and $Tx = y$.

% \begin{definition}
Suppose $T$ is densely defined.
An element $y \in \mathcal H$ is said to lie in the domain of the \emph{adjoint} $T^*$ if the map
\(
x \mapsto \braket{Tx | y}
\),
\(
x \in D(T)
\),
is a bounded linear functional on $D(T)$ (with the norm inherited from $\mathcal H$).
By Hahn-Banach and the Riesz representation theorem, there is then a unique vector $T^*y \in \mathcal H$ such that
\[
\braket{Tx | y} = \braket{x | T^*y}
\]
for every $x \in D(T)$.
Thus
\[
D(T^*) \coloneq \left\{y \in \mathcal H : x \mapsto \braket{Tx | y}
\text{ is bounded on } D(T)\right\}
\]
and $T^* \colon D(T^*) \to \mathcal H$ is an operator in $\mathcal H$.
% \end{definition}
If $T \in B(\mathcal H)$, this agrees with the usual adjoint of a bounded operator.

\begin{definition}\label{def:symmetric-self-adjoint-unbounded}
A densely defined operator $T$ is called
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item 
\emph{symmetric} if
\(
\braket{Tx | y} = \braket{x | Ty}
\)
for every $x,y \in D(T)$, or,
equivalently,
\(
T \subseteq T^*
\)
\item \label{item:densely-defined-self-adjoint}
\emph{self-adjoint} if
\(
T = T^*
\)
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{definition}


Unlike for bounded operators, symmetry and self-adjointness are not the same for unbounded operators.

% \begin{definition}
If $S$ and $T$ are operators in $\mathcal H$, their sum and product are defined on the natural domains
\[
D(S+T) = D(S)\cap D(T)
\qquad
D(ST) = \{x \in D(T) : Tx \in D(S)\}
\]
% \end{definition}
With these definitions, the associative laws remain valid, but one must always watch domains carefully.

\begin{theorem}
If $T$ is densely defined, then $T^*$ is closed.
In particular, every self-adjoint operator is closed.
\end{theorem}

\begin{theorem}
If $T$ is densely defined and closed, then 
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item
$D(T^*)$ is dense in $\mathcal H$ and
\(
T^{**} = T
\).
\item
\(
I + T^*T
\)
is a one-to-one mapping of
\(
D(T^*T)=\{x\in D(T): Tx\in D(T^*)\}
\)
onto $\mathcal H$.
Its inverse
\(
(I+T^*T)^{-1}
\)
belongs to $B(\mathcal H)$.
\item
$T^*T$ is self-adjoint and positive, i.e.,
\(
\braket{T^*Tx | x} = \|Tx\|^2 \ge 0
\)
for all $x \in D(T^*T)$.
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{definition}\label{def:spectrum-unbounded-operator}
The \emph{resolvent set} of an operator $T$ is the set of all $\lambda \in \mathbb C$ such that
\(
T-\lambda I
\)
is one-to-one, maps $D(T)$ onto $\mathcal H$, and has bounded inverse in $B(\mathcal H)$.
Its complement is the \hl[6]{\emph{spectrum} $\sigma(T)$}.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}\label{def:domain-of-Psi}
Let $E$ be a resolution of the identity on a measurable space $(\Omega,\mathfrak M)$, and let
\(
f \colon \Omega \to \mathbb C
\)
be measurable.
Define
\(
D_f \coloneq \{x \in \mathcal H : \int_\Omega |f|^2 \, \dif E_{x,x} < \infty \}
\).
Then $D_f$ is a dense subspace of $\mathcal H$.
The operator $\Psi(f)$ is defined by the requirement
\[
\braket{\Psi(f)x | y}
=
\int_\Omega f \, \dif E_{x,y}
\]
for every $x \in D_f$ and every $y \in \mathcal H$.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}%[Functional calculus for measurable functions]
With the notation above, $\Psi(f)$ is a densely defined closed operator with domain $D_f$, and
\[
\|\Psi(f)x\|^2 = \int_\Omega |f|^2\, \dif E_{x,x}
\]
for every $x \in D_f$.
If $f$ and $g$ are measurable, then
\(
\Psi(f)\Psi(g) \subseteq \Psi(fg)
\),
and
\(
\Psi(f)^* = \Psi(f^*)
\).
Hence
\(
\Psi(f)^*\Psi(f)=\Psi(|f|^2)=\Psi(f)\Psi(f)^*
\).
So every operator of the form $\Psi(f)$ is normal.
\end{theorem}


\begin{definition}\label{def:normal-operator}
A (not necessarily bounded) linear operator $T$ in $\mathcal{H}$ is said to be \hl[2]{\emph{normal}} if $T$ is closed, densely defined, and $T^* T = T T^*$.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}
If $N$ is normal, then
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item $D(N)=D(N^*)$
\item $\|Nx\|=\|N^*x\|$ for every $x\in D(N)$
\item $N$ is \emph{maximally normal}: if $M$ is normal and $N\subseteq M$, then $M=N$
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}




\autoref{thm:normal-bounded-operator-spectral-properties} can be extended to unbounded normal operators as follows:


% Theorem about spectrum of normal operators (unbounded)
\begin{theorem}
A densely defined normal operator $T$ is 
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item self-adjoint if and only if $\sigma(T)$ lies on the real axis
\item unitary if and only if $\sigma(T)$ lies on the unit circle \label{item:unitary-if-spectrum-on-unit-circle}
\end{enumerate}
Note that if \ref{item:unitary-if-spectrum-on-unit-circle} holds, then necessarily $D(T)=\mathcal H$, so $T$ is bounded.
\end{theorem}

% Definition and theorem about involutions (unbounded)
\begin{definition}\label{def:involution-unbounded}
An operator $T$ in $\mathcal H$ is called an \emph{involution} if
$D(T^2)=D(T)$
and
\[T^2x=x\]
for every $x\in D(T)$.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:unitary-selfadjoint-involution}
Let $T$ be an operator in $\mathcal H$. Then any two of the following properties imply the third:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
  \item $T^* = T^{-1}$ ($T$ is unitary) \hfill \ding{43} \autoref{def:normal-hermitian-unitary-projection} \ref{item:unitary}
  \item $T^* = T$ ($T$ is self-adjoint) \hfill \ding{43} \autoref{def:symmetric-self-adjoint-unbounded} \ref{item:densely-defined-self-adjoint}
  \item $T^2 = I$ ($T$ is an involution) \hfill \ding{43} \autoref{def:involution-unbounded}
\end{enumerate}
In particular, if one of these three implications involves unitarity, then necessarily $D(T)=\mathcal H$, so $T$ is bounded.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
If $T$ is unitary and self-adjoint, then $T^{-1}=T^*=T$, hence $T^2=I$, so $T$ is an involution.
If $T$ is unitary and an involution, then $T^*=T^{-1}=T$, so $T$ is self-adjoint.
Finally, assume that $T$ is self-adjoint and an involution.
Then $T$ is closed, and for every $x\in D(T)$,
\[
\|Tx\|^2 = \braket{Tx|Tx} = \braket{x|T^2x} = \|x\|^2
\]
so $T$ is an isometry on $D(T)$.
Moreover,
\[
\|x\|_T^2 = \|x\|^2+\|Tx\|^2 = 2\|x\|^2
\]
for every $x \in D(T)$.
Hence $D(T)$ is complete in the graph norm and therefore also in the Hilbert norm.
Since a self-adjoint operator is densely defined, $D(T)$ is a dense complete subspace of $\mathcal H$, hence $D(T)=\mathcal H$.
Thus $T$ is everywhere defined, isometric, and surjective because $T^2=I$, so $T$ is unitary.
\end{proof}


\begin{notation}
If \(M \subseteq \mathcal H\) is a closed subspace, we write \(P_M\) for the orthogonal projection onto \(M\), i.e. the unique self-adjoint projection with range \(R(P_M)=M\) (see \autoref{prop:orthogonal-projection-closed-subspace}).
\end{notation}

\begin{proposition}\label{prop:spectral-projection-eigenspace}
Let \(A\) be self-adjoint with spectral measure \(E_A\). Then for every eigenvalue \(\lambda\) of \(A\),
\[
E_A(\{\lambda\}) = P_{N(A-\lambda \id)}
\]
where \(P_{N(A-\lambda \id)}\) is the orthogonal projection onto the eigenspace
\[
N(A-\lambda \id)=\{x\in\mathcal H:Ax=\lambda x\}
\]
This notation is justified by \autoref{prop:orthogonal-projection-closed-subspace}, since eigenspaces of self-adjoint operators are closed subspaces.
% In particular, if \(\lambda\) is nondegenerate with normalized eigenvector \(\varphi\), then
% \[
% E_A(\{\lambda\}) = P_{\mathrm{span}\{\varphi\}} = P_\varphi
% \qedhere
% \]
\end{proposition}


\begin{theorem}[Spectral theorem]\label{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded}
Every normal operator $T$ in $\mathcal H$ has a unique spectral decomposition\footnote{%
In the unbounded case, a resolution of the identity means exactly the same thing as before (\autoref{def:resolution-of-identity}): a projection-valued measure (PVM)
\(
E:\mathfrak M\to B(\mathcal H)
\).
What is new is that for measurable \(f\), the operator
\(
\Psi(f)
\)
need not be bounded; its domain is
\(
D_f=\{x\in\mathcal H:\int |f|^2\,\dif E_{x,x}<\infty\}
\)
(see \autoref{def:domain-of-Psi}).%
}  $E$, such that
\begin{equation}\label{eq:inner-product-spectral-representation}
% \boxed{
\braket{Tx | y} = \int_{\sigma(T)} \lambda \,\dif E_{x,y}(\lambda)
% }
\end{equation}
for every $x \in D(T) \overset{\text{\ref{def:domain-of-Psi}}}{=} \{x\in\mathcal H:\int_{\sigma(T)} |\lambda|^2\,\dif E_{x,x}(\lambda)<\infty\}$ and every $y \in \mathcal H$.
Equivalently:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:spectral-theorem-unbounded-operator}
% \boxed{
T=\int_{\sigma(T)} \lambda\, \dif E(\lambda)
% }
\end{equation}
Moreover, $E(\omega) S = S E(\omega)$ for every Borel set $\omega \subseteq \sigma(T)$ and every $S \in B(\mathcal H)$ which commutes with $T$ in the sense that $ST \subseteq TS$.
\end{theorem}



% \clearpage














\clearpage

\section{Postulates of Quantum Mechanics} \label{sec:postulates-of-quantum-mechanics}

% The postulates in this section follow standard introductory quantum-mechanics references such as \cite{delRioLaneveCarocariQuantumPhysics,zettiliQuantumMechanics}.



\subsection{The state}

To an isolated quantum system we associate a complex Hilbert space $\mathcal{H}$.
A \emph{state} of the system is represented by a normalized vector
\(
\psi \in \mathcal{H}
\)
with
\(
\|\psi\| = 1
\).
If $\mathcal{H}$ is realized as a space of wave functions, for example $\mathcal{H} = L^2(\R^3)$, then, as already introduced in \eqref{eq:inner-product}, the scalar product is
\[
\braket{\psi_1 | \psi_2}
\coloneq
\int_{\R^3} \psi_1(\vect{x}, t)^* \, \psi_2(\vect{x}, t) \,\dif \vect{x}
\]
and the normalization condition becomes
\(
\|\psi\|^2
=
\braket{\psi | \psi}
=
\int_{\R^3} |\psi(\vect{x}, t)|^2 \,\dif \vect{x}
=
1
\).
In that case, $|\psi(\vect{x}, t)|^2$ is interpreted as probability density.

The linear structure of $\mathcal{H}$ gives the \emph{superposition principle}:
if $\psi_1,\psi_2 \in \mathcal{H}$ and $\alpha,\beta \in \C$, then also
\(
\alpha \psi_1 + \beta \psi_2 \in \mathcal{H}
\).
If, in addition,
\(
\|\alpha \psi_1 + \beta \psi_2\| = 1
\),
then this superposition again represents a physical state.
Thus quantum states behave like waves: linear combinations of states are again states.






\begin{digression}[Position Basis]\label{dig:position-basis}
Physicists often describe position by a family of kets
\(
\ket{\vect{x}}
\),
\(
\vect{x}\in\R^3
\),
and write
\begin{equation}\label{eq:position-basis-expansion}
\ket{\psi}
=
\int_{\R^3} \dif \vect{x} \, \psi(\vect{x}) \ket{\vect{x}} 
\end{equation}
This is analogous to the  expansion of \(\ket{\psi}\) in a finite-dimensional orthonormal basis \(\{\ket{e_i}\}\):
\[
\ket{\psi} = \sum_i c_i \ket{e_i}
\]
Thus the wave function \(\psi(\vect{x})\) plays the role of a \emph{continuous coefficient} of the state in the position representation.

\medskip

Formally, the coefficient of \(\ket{\vect{x}}\) is obtained by projection:
\[
\psi(\vect{x})
=
\braket{\vect{x}|\psi}
\]
Thus \(\psi(\vect{x})\) is the probability amplitude for the position outcome \(\vect{x}\).
Hence the Born rule in the position representation becomes
\[
\Prob(\text{particle in }\omega)
=
\int_\omega |\psi(\vect{x})|^2\,\dif \vect{x}
\]
for every Borel set \(\omega\subseteq\R^3\).
% So it is not the coefficient \(\psi(\vect{x})\) itself that is directly measurable, but its modulus square.

\medskip

The formal vectors \(\ket{\vect{x}}\) satisfy the relations
\(
\braket{\vect{x}_1|\vect{x}_2}
=
\delta(\vect{x}_1-\vect{x}_2)
\)
and
\(
\id
=
\int_{\R^3} \ket{\vect{x}}\bra{\vect{x}} \,\dif \vect{x}
\).
The former is the continuous analogue of
\(
\braket{e_i|e_j}=\delta_{ij}
\),
while the latter is the continuous analogue of
\(
\id =\sum_i \ket{e_i}\bra{e_i}
\).

Applying that continuous resolution of the identity
to a state \(\ket{\psi}\) yields
\[
\ket{\psi}
=
\left(\int_{\R^3}\ket{\vect{x}}\bra{\vect{x}}\,\dif\vect{x}\right)\ket{\psi}
=
\int_{\R^3}\ket{\vect{x}}\braket{\vect{x}|\psi}\,\dif\vect{x}
\]
which is exactly \eqref{eq:position-basis-expansion}.

\medskip

Moreover, for two states
\(
\psi_1,\psi_2 \in L^2(\R^3)
\),
one may write formally
\[
\bra{\psi_1}
=
\int_{\R^3}\psi_1(\vect{x})^* \bra{\vect{x}}\,\dif\vect{x}
\qquad
\ket{\psi_2}
=
\int_{\R^3}\psi_2(\vect{y}) \ket{\vect{y}}\,\dif\vect{y}
\]
and therefore
\[
\begin{aligned}
\braket{\psi_1|\psi_2}
&=
\left(\int_{\R^3}\psi_1(\vect{x})^* \bra{\vect{x}}\,\dif\vect{x}\right)
\left(\int_{\R^3}\psi_2(\vect{y}) \ket{\vect{y}}\,\dif\vect{y}\right)
\\
&=
\int_{\R^3}\int_{\R^3}
\psi_1(\vect{x})^* \psi_2(\vect{y}) \braket{\vect{x}|\vect{y}}
\,\dif\vect{x}\,\dif\vect{y}
\\
&=
\int_{\R^3}\int_{\R^3}
\psi_1(\vect{x})^* \psi_2(\vect{y}) \delta(\vect{x}-\vect{y})
\,\dif\vect{x}\,\dif\vect{y}
\\
&=
\int_{\R^3}\psi_1(\vect{x})^*\psi_2(\vect{x})\,\dif\vect{x}
\end{aligned}
\]
which recovers \eqref{eq:inner-product}, the usual inner product on \(L^2(\R^3)\).

The family \(\{\ket{\vect{x}}:\vect{x}\in\R^3\}\) is often called a \emph{continuous orthonormal basis}.
This characterization is standard and useful, but not entirely accurate.
The objects \(\ket{\vect{x}}\) are not even honest vectors of \(L^2(\R^3)\), since
\(
\braket{\vect{x}|\vect{x}}
=
\delta(\vect{0})
\)
is not a finite number.
Rather, they are \emph{generalized eigenvectors} of the position operator, more precisely distribution-like objects in the sense of a \emph{rigged Hilbert space}
\cite{deLaMadridRiggedHilbertSpace}
\[
\Phi \subset \mathcal H \subset \Phi'
\]
where \(\mathcal H = L^2(\R^3)\), \(\Phi\) is a space of sufficiently nice test functions (for example Schwartz functions), and \(\Phi'\) is its dual space of continuous linear functionals (distributions).
The generalized position eigenstates are then not elements of \(\mathcal H\), but distribution-like objects in \(\Phi'\).
In this sense, the notation \(\ket{\vect{x}}\) is a formal Dirac ket corresponding to the evaluation functional at \(\vect{x}\), and the relation
\(
\braket{\vect{x}|\psi} = \psi(\vect{x})
\)
should be understood in this generalized sense.

\medskip

To see how the position-basis connects to the spectral theory from \autoref{sec:operators} and \autoref{sec:observables}, 
recall that by \autoref{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded} every self-adjoint observable \(A\) has a spectral measure \(E_A\),
a PVM on \(\sigma(A)\).
For the position observable, the corresponding PVM \(E_{\vect{X}}\) on the Borel subsets of \(\R^3\) acts by
\[
(E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)\psi)(\vect{x})
=
\ind_{\omega}(\vect{x})\,\psi(\vect{x})
\]
for each Borel set \(\omega\subseteq\R^3\),
where 
% \(\ind_{\omega}\)
%  is the characteristic function of \(\omega\), i.e. 
 \(\ind_{\omega}(\vect{x})=1\) if \(\vect{x}\in\omega\) and \(\ind_{\omega}(\vect{x})=0\) otherwise.
So \(E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)\) simply projects a wave function onto the part supported in \(\omega\).
Hence the Born rule becomes
\[
\begin{aligned}
\Prob_{\vect{X}}^{\psi}(\omega)
&=
\braket{\psi | E_{\vect{X}}(\omega) | \psi}
=
\braket{\psi | E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)\psi}
\\
&=
\int_{\R^3} \psi(\vect{x})^*\,(E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)\psi)(\vect{x})\,\dif\vect{x}
\\
&=
\int_{\R^3} \psi(\vect{x})^*\,\ind_{\omega}(\vect{x})\,\psi(\vect{x})\,\dif\vect{x}
\\
&=
\int_{\omega}|\psi(\vect{x})|^2\,\dif\vect{x}
\end{aligned}
\]
In Dirac notation, one writes the corresponding spectral projection as
\[
E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)
=
\int_{\omega}\ket{\vect{x}}\bra{\vect{x}}\,\dif\vect{x}
\]
Then one obtaines the same result as
\[
\begin{aligned}
\Prob_{\vect{X}}^{\psi}(\omega)
&=
\braket{\psi|E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)|\psi}
\\
&=
\Braket{\psi|\int_{\omega}\lvert\vect{x}\rangle \langle\vect{x}\rvert\,\dif\vect{x}|\psi}
\\
&=
\int_{\omega}\braket{\psi|\vect{x}}\braket{\vect{x}|\psi}\,\dif\vect{x}
\\
&=
\int_{\omega}|\psi(\vect{x})|^2\,\dif\vect{x}
\end{aligned}
\]
so the ``position basis'' is the Dirac-notation form of the spectral decomposition of the position observable.
% In Dirac notation, one may write this as
% We can use the position-basis language to connect to the spectral theory from \autoref{sec:operators} and \autoref{sec:observables}.
% For the position observable, one writes for each Borel set \(\omega\subseteq\R^3\)
% \[
% E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)
% =
% \int_{\omega}\ket{\vect{x}}\bra{\vect{x}}\,\dif\vect{x}.
% \]
% Applying this to a state \(\psi\) and taking the position representation gives
% \[
% \begin{aligned}
% (E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)\psi)(\vect{x})
% &=
% \braket{\vect{x}|E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)|\psi}
% \\
% &=
% \int_{\omega}\braket{\vect{x}|\vect{y}}\braket{\vect{y}|\psi}\,\dif\vect{y}
% \\
% &=
% \int_{\omega}\delta^{(3)}(\vect{x}-\vect{y})\,\psi(\vect{y})\,\dif\vect{y}
% \\
% &=
% \mathbf 1_{\omega}(\vect{x})\,\psi(\vect{x}).
% \end{aligned}
% \]
% So \(E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)\) projects the wave function onto the part supported in \(\omega\).
% Consequently,
% \[
% \begin{aligned}
% \Prob_{\vect{X}}^{\psi}(\omega)
% &=
% \braket{\psi|E_{\vect{X}}(\omega)|\psi}
% \\
% &=
% \int_{\omega}\braket{\psi|\vect{x}}\braket{\vect{x}|\psi}\,\dif\vect{x}
% \\
% &=
% \int_{\omega}|\psi(\vect{x})|^2\,\dif\vect{x}.
% \end{aligned}
% \]
% Thus the position PVM, the usual Born rule for \(|\psi(\vect{x})|^2\), and the Dirac ``position basis'' are all just different descriptions of the same object.
\end{digression}


\subsection{The time evolution}\label{sec:time-evolution}

As already introduced in \eqref{eq:schroedinger}, the time evolution of a quantum state is governed by the Schrödinger equation
\[
\boxed{
\iu \hbar  \frac{\partial}{\partial t}\psi(\vect{x}, t) = H  \psi(\vect{x}, t)
}
\]
where $H$ is the Hamiltonian operator \eqref{eq:hamiltonian}.
It is linear: any linear combination of solutions is again a solution.

We assume the Hamiltonian $H$ to be self-adjoint (see Definition \ref{def:hermitian}, \ref{def:normal-hermitian-unitary-projection} \ref{item:hermitian}, \ref{def:symmetric-self-adjoint-unbounded}).
If $H$ is time-independent, then the solutions can be written as
\[
\psi(t) = \eu^{- \iu H t / \hbar} \psi(0)
\]
Thus the time evolution operator is
\(
U(t) = \eu^{- \iu H t / \hbar}
\).
As discussed before, $\eu^{\iu A}$ is unitary whenever $A$ is Hermitian, so $U(t)$ is unitary (see Definition \ref{def:unitary}, \ref{def:normal-hermitian-unitary-projection} \ref{item:unitary}).
Hence time evolution preserves the inner product and in particular the norm:
if \(\braket{\psi(0) | \psi(0)} = 1\), then
\(
\braket{\psi(t) | \psi(t)} = 1
\)
for all $t \in \R$.







\subsection{Observables} \label{sec:observables}

A measurable physical quantity is represented by an observable, that is, by a self-adjoint operator
\(
A = A^*
\)
(see Definitions \ref{def:hermitian}, \ref{def:normal-hermitian-unitary-projection} \ref{item:hermitian}, \ref{def:symmetric-self-adjoint-unbounded}, Theorems \ref{thm:spectral-theorem}, \ref{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded}).
The expectation value of $A$ in a state $\psi$ is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:expectation-value-observable}
\braket{A}_{\psi}
=
\braket{\psi | A | \psi}
\coloneq
\braket{\psi | A\psi}
\end{equation}
provided $\psi \in D(A)$.
The possible outcomes of a measurement of $A$ are given by the spectrum (see \autoref{def:spectrum-unbounded-operator}) $\sigma(A) = \sigma_p(A) \cup \sigma_c(A)$ (for self-adjoint (wether bounded or unbounded) operators, the residual spectrum $\sigma_r(A)$ is always empty) of $A$.

% This is the calculation one would like to preserve in general Hilbert spaces.
% The difficulty is that in infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces a self-adjoint operator need not admit an orthonormal basis of eigenvectors: there may be continuous spectrum 

In a general Hilbert space $\mathcal{H}$, every self-adjoint operator $A$ admits a spectral decomposition
(see \autoref{sec:operators}, \autoref{def:spectrum-unbounded-operator} and \autoref{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded}).
Denote the spectrum of $A$ by $\sigma_A \coloneq \sigma(A)$.
By \autoref{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded}, for every self-adjoint operator there exists a unique projection-valued measure (PVM)
\[
E_A \colon \mathcal B(\sigma_A) \to B(\mathcal H) % why bounded? because each E_A(omega) is a projection, hence a bounded operator
\]
on the Borel sets of $\sigma_A$ such that
\(
A = \int_{\sigma_A} \lambda \,\dif E_A(\lambda)
\).
Here each $E_A(\omega)$ is a self-adjoint projection, and by \autoref{def:resolution-of-identity} and the remarks following it,
\[
\braket{\psi|E_A(\omega)\psi} \overset{\text{\eqref{eq:Exx-positive-measure}}}{=} \|E_A(\omega)\psi\|^2 \ge 0
\]
for every Borel set $\omega \subseteq \sigma_A$.
Moreover,
\(
E_A(\sigma_A)=\id
\) and
\(
E_A(\emptyset)=0
\),
so for normalized $\psi$ we have
\(
\braket{\psi|E_A(\sigma_A)\psi} = \braket{\psi|\psi} = 1
\).
Therefore, 
for every normalized state $\psi$,
the map
\begin{equation}\label{eq:probability-measure-from-spectral-decomposition}
\mu_\psi^A(\omega)
\coloneq
\braket{\psi|E_A(\omega)\psi}
\overset{\text{\ref{def:resolution-of-identity}.\ref{item:each-Eomega-self-adjoint-projection}}}{=}
\braket{E_A(\omega)\psi|\psi}
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:complex-measure-via-inner-product}}}{=}
E_{\psi,\psi}(\omega)
\end{equation}
for $\omega \in \mathcal{B}(\sigma_A)$, 
defines a probability measure $\Prob_A^\psi(\cdot) \coloneq \mu_\psi^A(\cdot)$ on $\sigma_A$.
This is the probability distribution of the measurement outcomes of $A$ in the state $\psi$.
This is called the \hl[2]{\emph{Born rule}}.

% So the generalization of the discrete formula
% \(
% p_i = \bigl|\braket{\varphi_i|\psi}\bigr|^2
% \)
% is not, in general, a pdf, but a probability measure
% \[
% \Prob_A^\psi(B)
% =
% \mu_\psi^A(B)
% =
% \braket{\psi|E_A(B)\psi}
% \]
% for Borel sets $B \in \mathcal B(\sigma_A)$.
% This is the precise infinite-dimensional analogue of the Born rule.

% \[
% \begin{aligned}
% \braket{A}_{\psi}
% &=
% \braket{\psi|A\psi}
% \\
% &=
% \Braket{\psi | \int_{\sigma_A} \lambda \,\dif E_A(\lambda)\, \psi}
% \\
% &=
% \int_{\sigma_A} \lambda \, \dif \braket{\psi|E_A(\lambda)\psi}
% \\
% &=
% \int_{\sigma_A} \lambda \, \dif \mu_\psi^A(\lambda)
% \end{aligned}
% \]

With this notation, the expectation value is obtained as 
\[
\begin{aligned}
\braket{A}_{\psi}
&=
\braket{\psi|A\psi}
\overset{\text{\ref{def:symmetric-self-adjoint-unbounded}.\ref{item:densely-defined-self-adjoint}}}{=}
\braket{A\psi|\psi}
\\
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:inner-product-spectral-representation}}}{=}
\int_{\sigma_A} \lambda \,\dif E_{\psi,\psi}(\lambda)
% \\
% &\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:complex-measure-via-inner-product}}}{=}
% \int_{\sigma_A} \lambda \,\dif \braket{E_A(\lambda)\psi | \psi}
\\
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:probability-measure-from-spectral-decomposition}}}{=}
\int_{\sigma_A} \lambda \,\dif \mu_\psi^A(\lambda)
\end{aligned}
\]
% where we used notational shorthand `$\dif \braket{\psi|E_A(\lambda)\psi} = \dif \braket{E_A(\,\cdot\,)\psi | \psi}(\lambda)$' in the penultimate step.
which is precisely the probabilistic expectation of the measurement outcomes with respect to the spectral probability measure.
In case the spectrum has both discrete and continuous parts, then $\mu_\psi^A$ automatically contains both.

% If the measure happens to be absolutely continuous with respect to Lebesgue measure, then one may write
% \[
% \mu_\psi^A(B) = \int_B \rho_\psi^A(\lambda)\,\dif\lambda
% \]
% for some density $\rho_\psi^A$.
% But pedagogically the fundamental object is the measure $\mu_\psi^A$, not necessarily a density.

After an ideal measurement, the state updates by projection.
If one only knows that the outcome lies in a Borel set $\omega$, then the post-measurement state is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:post-measurement-state}
\psi
\longmapsto
\frac{E_A(\omega)\psi}{\|E_A(\omega)\psi\|}
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:Exx-positive-measure}}}{=}
\frac{E_A(\omega)\psi}{\sqrt{\braket{\psi|E_A(\omega)|\psi}}}
\end{equation}
provided $E_A(\omega)\psi \neq 0$.







\begin{example}[Finite-dimensional case]\label{ex:finite-dimensional-case}
If ${A} \colon \C^n \to \C^n$ is self-adjoint, then by the (finite-dimensional) spectral theorem there exist distinct real eigenvalues.
Since this is explicitly the finite-dimensional case, all sums in this example are finite.
\(
\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_r
\)
and pairwise orthogonal eigenspaces
\[
M_j \coloneq N({A}-\lambda_j \id)
\]
for
\(
j=1,\dots,r
\),
such that
\[
\C^n = \bigoplus_{j=1}^r M_j
\]
and
\[
\sigma(A)=\{\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_r\}
\]

Let $E_A$ be the spectral measure of $A$ from \autoref{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded}. Since the spectrum is finite, the spectral integral
% \(
% A = \int_{\sigma(A)} \lambda \,\dif E_A(\lambda)
% \)
reduces to a finite sum over the atoms $\{\lambda_j\}$:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:discrete-spectrum-spectral-integral}
A
=
\int_{\sigma(A)} \lambda \,\dif E_A(\lambda)
=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j\, E_A(\{\lambda_j\})
\end{equation}
By \autoref{prop:spectral-projection-eigenspace},
\(
E_A(\{\lambda_j\}) = P_{M_j}
\),
where $P_{M_j}$ denotes the orthogonal projection onto the eigenspace $M_j$.
Hence
\begin{equation}\label{eq:discrete-spectrum-spectral-decomposition}
{A} = \sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j P_{M_j}
\end{equation}
If we choose an orthonormal basis of eigenvectors in each eigenspace \(M_j\),
\[
\{\varphi_{j,k}: 1\le j\le r,\ 1\le k\le d_j\}
\]
with
\(
d_j = \dim M_j
\),
\(
M_j = \mathrm{span}\{\varphi_{j,1},\dots,\varphi_{j,d_j}\}
\),
 the orthogonal projection onto \(M_j\) is the sum of the one-dimensional projections onto these basis vectors. Hence
\[
P_{M_j}\psi 
= % because the orthogonal projection onto a finite-dimensional subspace is the sum of the rank-one projections onto an orthonormal basis of that subspace
\sum_{k=1}^{d_j} P_{\varphi_{j,k}}\psi
= % because for a normalized vector $\varphi$ one has $P_{\varphi}\psi = \braket{\varphi|\psi}\,\varphi$
\sum_{k=1}^{d_j} \braket{\varphi_{j,k}|\psi} \varphi_{j,k}
\]
and, by orthonormality,
\begin{equation}\label{eq:projection-onto-eigenspace-squared-norm}
\|P_{M_j}\psi\|^2
=
\sum_{k=1}^{d_j} |\braket{\varphi_{j,k}|\psi}|^2
\end{equation}


Substituting \eqref{eq:discrete-spectrum-spectral-decomposition} into the expectation value gives
\[
\begin{aligned}
\braket{{A}}_{\psi}
&=
\braket{\psi|{A}\psi}
\\
&=
\left\langle \psi \middle| \sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j P_{M_j}\psi \right\rangle
\\
&=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j \, \braket{\psi|P_{M_j}\psi}
\\
&=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j \, \|P_{M_j}\psi\|^2
\end{aligned}
\]
Thus the numbers
\(
p_j \coloneq \|P_{M_j}\psi\|^2
\)
are precisely the probabilities of obtaining the outcomes $\lambda_j$.
So in the finite-dimensional case the expectation value has the familiar form
\(
\braket{{A}}_{\psi} = \sum_{j = 1}^r \lambda_j p_j
\).


The general formulas from the main text hence reduce to these familiar finite-dimensional discrete ones. In particular, \eqref{eq:discrete-spectrum-spectral-integral} shows explicitly how the spectral integral collapses to a finite sum, and \autoref{prop:spectral-projection-eigenspace} identifies the spectral projections with the orthogonal projections onto the eigenspaces.
Indeed, given a Borel set $\omega \subseteq \sigma(A)$, we have
\begin{equation}\label{eq:EAomega-discrete-spectrum}
E_A(\omega) 
=
E_A\left(\bigcup_{\smash[b]{\lambda_j} \in \omega} \{\lambda_j\}\right)
\overset{\text{\ref{def:resolution-of-identity}.\ref{item:if-disjoint-then-Eomega1cupomega2-is-sum}}}{=} 
\sum_{\lambda_j \in \omega} E_A(\{\lambda_j\})
\overset{\text{\ref{prop:spectral-projection-eigenspace}}}{=}
\sum_{\lambda_j \in \omega} P_{M_j}
\end{equation}
Here the sum is finite because $\sigma({A})=\{\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_r\}$ is finite and hence $\omega \subseteq \sigma(A)$ is so as well.
Therefore
\[
\begin{aligned}
\mu_\psi^{A}(\omega)
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:probability-measure-from-spectral-decomposition}}}{=}
\braket{\psi|E_{A}(\omega)\psi}
\\
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:EAomega-discrete-spectrum}}}{=}
\left\langle \psi \middle| \sum_{\smash[b]{\lambda_j} \in \omega} P_{M_j}\psi \right\rangle
\\
&=
\sum_{\lambda_j \in \omega} \braket{\psi|P_{M_j}\psi}
\\
&\overset{\text{\ref{thm:projection-properties}}}{=}
\sum_{\lambda_j \in \omega} \|P_{M_j}\psi\|^2 % please simplify further in terms of inner product etc, as discussed...
\\
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:projection-onto-eigenspace-squared-norm}}}{=}
\sum_{\lambda_j \in \omega}\sum_{k=1}^{d_j} |\braket{\varphi_{j,k}|\psi}|^2
\end{aligned}
\]
which recovers exactly the usual formula.
In particular,
\begin{equation}\label{eq:projection-onto-eigenspace-born-rule}
\Prob_{A}^\psi(\{\lambda_j\})
=
\|P_{M_j}\psi\|^2
=
\sum_{k=1}^{d_j} |\braket{\varphi_{j,k}|\psi}|^2
\end{equation}

If the measurement outcome is the eigenvalue $\lambda_j$, then the post-measurement state from \eqref{eq:post-measurement-state} becomes
\[
\psi
\longmapsto
\frac{P_{M_j}\psi}{\|P_{M_j}\psi\|}
\]
provided $P_{M_j}\psi \neq 0$.
Thus the state collapses to the normalized component of $\psi$ in the eigenspace $M_j$.
% If $P_{M_j}\psi$ happens to be a scalar multiple of a single normalized eigenvector $\varphi \in M_j$, then this reduces to
% \[
% \psi
% \longmapsto
% \varphi \, \eu^{\iu \theta}
% \]
% for some phase $\theta \in [0,2\pi)$.
\end{example}













\subsection{Joint systems and composition}

If two quantum systems are described by Hilbert spaces \(\mathcal H_A\) and \(\mathcal H_B\), then the composite system is described by the tensor product Hilbert space
\(
\mathcal H_A \otimes \mathcal H_B
\).

The tensor product is linear in each factor, so it is compatible with the superposition principle: superpositions in the subsystems induce corresponding superpositions in the composite system.

If the two subsystems are in pure states \(\psi_A \in \mathcal H_A\) and \(\psi_B \in \mathcal H_B\), then the corresponding product state of the joint system is
\[
\psi_A \otimes \psi_B \in \mathcal H_A \otimes \mathcal H_B
\]

More generally, the composite Hilbert space also contains superpositions of product states. Not every state in \(\mathcal H_A \otimes \mathcal H_B\) can be written in the form \(\psi_A \otimes \psi_B\). States that cannot be written in this form are called \emph{entangled}.


\pagebreak[2]


\subsection{Abstraction and simplification}

From now on, we restrict to the case where the Hilbert space is finite-dimensional.
Then
\[
\mathcal{H} \cong \C^n
\]
for some $n \in \N$, i.e. $\mathcal{H}$ is isomorphic to the standard $n$-dimensional complex vector space.  

From this point on, all basis expansions are therefore finite sums.
This allows us to represent states by coordinates with respect to some fixed 
orthonormal % is this too strong?
basis
and hence to
work with vectors and matrices instead of general Hilbert-space operators. % and spectral measures.

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:spectral-theorem-finite-dimensional}
%[Finite-Dimensional Spectral Theorem]
Let \(\matr{A} \in \C^{n\times n}\) be Hermitian. 
Then there exists a unitary matrix \(\matr U\) and a real diagonal matrix \(\matr\Lambda\) 
% of the form
% \[
% \matr\Lambda
% =
% \operatorname{diag}(\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_n)
% \]
such that
\begin{equation}\label{eq:spectral-theorem-finite-dimensional}
\matr{A}
=
\matr U \matr\Lambda \matr U^*
\end{equation}
where $\matr{\Lambda} = \operatorname{diag}(\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_n)$ is a diagonal matrix of eigenvalues listed with algebraic multiplicity.
Equivalently, \(\matr{A}\) admits an orthonormal basis of eigenvectors.
\end{theorem}


For $\psi \in \mathcal{H} \cong \C^n$, the corresponding column vector
\[
\ket{\psi}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
\psi_1 \\
% \psi_2 \\
\vdots \\
\psi_n
\end{bmatrix}
\]
is called a \emph{ket}.
Its adjoint (complex conjugated transpose) is the row vector
\[
\bra{\psi}
=
\ket{\psi}^*
=
\begin{bmatrix}
\psi_1^*,
% \psi_2^*  &
\ldots, 
\psi_n^* 
\end{bmatrix}
\]
called a \emph{bra}.
The inner product becomes
\[
\braket{\psi_{A} | \psi_{B}}
=
\bra{\psi_{A}}  \ket{\psi_{B}}
=
\sum_{i=1}^n \psi_{A,i}^* \psi_{B,i}
\in \C
\]
called a \emph{bra-ket}.


% which is the usual matrix multiplication of a row vector with a column vector.

% Projectors onto one-dimensional subspaces can be written as
% \[
% P_{\varphi} = \ket{\varphi}\bra{\varphi}
% \]
% because
% \[
% \ket{\varphi}\bra{\varphi}\ket{\psi}
% =
% \braket{\varphi|\psi}\ket{\varphi}
% \]

% Thus, if
% \[
% A = \sum_i \lambda_i P_{\varphi_i}
% \]
% then
% \[
% A = \sum_i \lambda_i \ket{\varphi_i}\bra{\varphi_i}
% \]




The notation is also useful for expressing Hermitian observables.
As seen in \autoref{ex:finite-dimensional-case}, if $A$ is self-adjoint, we have
\[
% \begin{aligned}
A
=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j P_{M_j} 
=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j \sum_{k=1}^{d_j} P_{\varphi_{j,k}}
% \end{aligned}
\]
and therefore
\[
\begin{aligned}
\ket{A \psi}
% &=
% \Ket{\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j \sum_{k=1}^{d_j} P_{\varphi_{j,k}} \psi} \\
&=
\sum_{\smash[b]{j}=1}^r \lambda_j \sum_{k=1}^{d_j} \Ket{P_{\varphi_{j,k}} \psi} \\
% &=
% \sum_{\smash[b]{j}=1}^r \lambda_j \sum_{k=1}^{d_j} \Ket{\braket{\varphi_{j,k}|\psi} \varphi_{j,k}} \\
&=
\sum_{\smash[b]{j}=1}^r \lambda_j \sum_{k=1}^{d_j} \braket{\varphi_{j,k}|\psi} \ket{\varphi_{j,k}} \\
&=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j \sum_{k=1}^{d_j} \ket{\varphi_{j,k}}\bra{\varphi_{j,k}} \ket{\psi}
\end{aligned}
\]
whereby we obtained the common way to represent projectors as
\[
% P_{\varphi_{\smash[b]{j,k}}} = \ket{\varphi_{j,k}}\bra{\varphi_{j,k}}
P_{\varphi} = \ket{\varphi}\bra{\varphi}
\]
and thus the observable $A$ itself as $A = \sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j \sum_{k=1}^{\smash{d_j}} \ket{\varphi_{j,k}}\bra{\varphi_{j,k}}$.

Fix an orthonormal basis $\{\ket{\varphi_i}\}_{i=1}^n$ of $\mathcal{H}$.
In the following, we abbreviate the basis vectors with their indices, i.e. $\ket{\varphi_{i}} \eqcolon \ket{i}$.

A common trick when working with an orthonormal basis in braket notation is to insert the identity operator
\begin{equation}\label{eq:orthonormal-basis-resolution-identity}
% \matr{I} 
\id
= 
\sum_{i=1}^n \ket{i}\bra{i}
\end{equation}
and then rearrange the terms.
Note that this is a discrete instance of the resolution of the identity from \autoref{def:resolution-of-identity}. 
For example, we can write a linear operator $A$ 
% on $\mathcal{H}$ 
as
% if $\{\ket{k}\}_k$ is an 
% orthonormal % too strong?
% basis,
\[
A 
= 
\left(\sum_{k=1}^n \ket{k}\bra{k}\right) A \left(\sum_{l=1}^n \ket{l}\bra{l}\right)
% =
% \sum_{k=1}^n \sum_{l=1}^n  \left(\ket{k}\bra{k}\right) A \left(\ket{l}\bra{l}\right)
=
\sum_{k=1}^n \sum_{l=1}^n  \ket{k} \braket{k|A|l} \bra{l}
=
\sum_{k=1}^n \sum_{l=1}^n  \underbrace{\braket{k|A|l}}_{[\matr{A}]_{kl}} \ket{k}\bra{l}
\]
hence, every linear operator $A$ can be represented as a matrix $\matr{A}$.
The entry in the $k^\text{th}$ row and $l^\text{th}$ column is $[\matr{A}]_{kl} = \braket{k|A|l}$.

The way in which it is written here is rather an expansion in a basis of the vector space of linear operators $\operatorname{End}(\mathcal{H})$, and $\braket{k|A|l}$ is the coefficient before the basis vector $\ket{k}\bra{l}$, i.e., a matrix with a one in the $k^\text{th}$ row and the $l^\text{th}$ column.

\subsection{The trace} \label{sec:trace}

\begin{definition}
For an operator $A \in \operatorname{End}(\mathcal{H})$ on a finite-dimensional Hilbert space, the trace is defined by
\begin{equation}\label{eq:trace-definition}
\Trace(A)
\coloneq
\sum_{i = 1}^n \braket{i|A|i}
\end{equation}
where $\{\ket{i}\}_{i=1}^n$ is any orthonormal basis.
\end{definition}

% In infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces, the same formula does \emph{not} define the trace for arbitrary operators. 
% One restricts to the class of \emph{trace-class operators}.
The trace can be generalized to infinite-dimensional spaces, at least for certain, so called \emph{trace-class}, operators:


\begin{definition}[Trace Class]
Let $\mathcal H$ be a separable Hilbert space with orthonormal basis $\{\ket{i}\}_{i=1}^{\infty}$.
A bounded operator $A \in B(\mathcal H)$ is called \emph{trace class} if
\[
\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \bra{i} |A| \ket{i} < \infty
\]
where $|A| \coloneq \sqrt{A^*A}$ denotes the positive-semidefinite Hermitian square root, i.e., the unique positive operator $B$ such that $B^2 = A^*A$.
In that case, the trace of $A$ is defined by
\[
\Trace(A)
\coloneq
\sum_{i=1}^{\infty} \braket{i|A|i}
\]
and this series converges absolutely and is independent of the choice of orthonormal basis.
\end{definition}

In particular, finite-rank operators are trace class. Hence rank-one operators such as
\[
\ket{\psi_1}\bra{\psi_2}
\]
are trace class, which is why the density-matrix formalism still works in infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces for pure states and, more generally, for trace-class density operators.



\begin{interlude}[Change of Basis] % here matrix makes sense
Fix an “old” orthonormal basis $B=(\vect{e}_1,\dots,\vect{e}_n)$ and a “new” orthonormal basis $\widetilde B=(\tilde{\vect{e}}_1,\dots,\tilde{\vect{e}}_n)$ of $\mathcal H$.
Let $\vect{x}$ be the coordinate column of a vector $v \allowbreak \in \allowbreak \mathcal{H}$ with respect to $B$, and let $\tilde{\vect{x}}$ be the coordinate column of the same vector with respect to $\widetilde B$.
If $\matr{U}$ denotes the change-of-basis matrix from $B$ to $\widetilde B$, then
\[
\tilde{\vect{x}} = \matr{U} \vect{x}
\]
where the columns of $\matr{U}$ are the old basis vectors written in the new basis.
Equivalently, the columns of $\matr{U}^{-1}$ are the new basis vectors written in the old basis.
If a linear operator $\hat{A} \allowbreak \colon \allowbreak \mathcal{H} \allowbreak \to \allowbreak \mathcal{H}$ has matrix $\matr{A} \in \mathbb{C}^{n\times n}$ in the old basis and matrix $\tilde{\matr{A}} \in \mathbb{C}^{n\times n}$ in the new basis, then
\[
% \boxed{
\tilde{\matr{A}} = \matr{U} \matr{A} \matr{U}^{-1}
% }
\]
where $\matr{U}$ is the change-of-basis matrix from the old basis $B$ to the new basis $\widetilde B$.
% \qquad \qquad \qquad
\tikzexternaldisable
\[
\begin{tikzcd}[column sep=4em, row sep=2.5em, font=\footnotesize, mycdstyle]
\mathbb{C}^n
  \arrow[r, "\matr{A}"] %\in\mathbb{C}^{n\times n}"]
  \arrow[dd, swap, "\matr{U}", shift right=2.0em]
&
\mathbb{C}^n
  \arrow[dd, "\matr{U}", shift left=2.0em]
\\
\mathcal{H}
  \arrow[u, "{[\cdot]_B}"] % coordinate isomorphism
  \arrow[r, "\hat{A}\ \text{linear}"]
  \arrow[d, swap, "{[\cdot]_{\widetilde B}}"] % coordinate isomorphism
&
\mathcal{H}
  \arrow[u, swap, "{[\cdot]_B}"] % coordinate isomorphism
  \arrow[d, "{[\cdot]_{\widetilde B}}"] % coordinate isomorphism
\\
\mathbb{C}^n
  \arrow[r, "\tilde{\matr{A}}"] %\in\mathbb{C}^{n\times n}"]
&
\mathbb{C}^n
\end{tikzcd}
\]
\tikzexternalenable
So matrices of the same operator in different bases are related by conjugation.
In the orthonormal case, $\matr{U}$ is unitary, hence
\(
\matr{U}^{-1}=\matr{U}^*
\).
\end{interlude}

\pagebreak[3]

The trace is basis-independent.
% A key identity is
To see this, recall that
\(
\Trace(A B) = \Trace(B A)
\).
Hence for a unitary change of basis $U$,
\begin{equation}\label{eq:trace-basis-independence}
\Trace(U A U^*)
=
\Trace(U^* U A)
=
\Trace(A)
\end{equation}


This can also be seen using the trick mentioned above:
\begingroup
\allowdisplaybreaks
\begin{align*}
\Trace(A)
&=
\sum_{i=1}^n \braket{i|A|i}
=
\sum_{i=1}^n \bra{i} \id A \id  \ket{i} \\
&=
\sum_{i=1}^n \bra{i} \left(\sum_{\tilde{u} = 1}^n \ket{\tilde{u}}\bra{\tilde{u}}\right) A \left(\sum_{\tilde{v} = 1}^n \ket{\tilde{v}}\bra{\tilde{v}}\right) \ket{i} \\
&=
\sum_{i=1}^n \sum_{\tilde{u} = 1}^n \sum_{\tilde{v} = 1}^n \braket{i|\tilde{u}} \braket{\tilde{u}|A|\tilde{v}} \braket{\tilde{v}|i} \\
&=
\sum_{\tilde{u} = 1}^n \sum_{\tilde{v} = 1}^n \sum_{i=1}^n  \braket{\tilde{v}|i} \braket{i|\tilde{u}} \braket{\tilde{u}|A|\tilde{v}}  \\
&=
\sum_{\tilde{u} = 1}^n \sum_{\tilde{v} = 1}^n   \bra{\tilde{v}} \underbrace{\left(\sum_{i=1}^n \ket{i} \bra{i} \right)}_{\id} \ket{\tilde{u}} \braket{\tilde{u}|A|\tilde{v}}  \\
&=
\sum_{\tilde{u} = 1}^n \sum_{\tilde{v} = 1}^n   \underbrace{\braket{\tilde{v} | \tilde{u}}}_{\delta_{\tilde{v}, \tilde{u}}} \braket{\tilde{u}|A|\tilde{v}}  \\
&=
\sum_{\tilde{u} = 1}^n \braket{\tilde{u}|A|\tilde{u}} 
\end{align*}
\endgroup


For a pure state $\ket{\psi}$ and an observable $A$, the \hl{expectation value} can be written as
\[
\begin{aligned}
\langle A \rangle_{\psi}
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:expectation-value-observable}}}{=}
\braket{\psi|A|\psi} \\
&=
\bra{\psi} A \left(\sum_{i=1}^n \ket{i}\bra{i}\right) \ket{\psi} \\
&=
\sum_{i=1}^n \braket{\psi | A | i} \braket{i | \psi} \\
&= 
\sum_{i=1}^n \braket{i | \left(\ket{\psi} \bra{\psi} A \right) | i} \\
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:trace-definition}}}{=}
\Trace\bigl(\ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}A\bigr)
\end{aligned}
\]
which is the form that extends naturally to mixed states.  % [oai_citation:7‡book_Invitation-to-Quantum-Informatics pages 52 - 59.pdf](sediment://file_00000000b410720abc2d96070c66ef5d)  % [oai_citation:8‡Note 17 Feb 2027.pdf](sediment://file_00000000ec74724390e9a998a2de5991)



\subsection{Density matrices}\label{sec:density-matrices}

So far, we have described quantum states by normalized vectors
\(
\psi \in \mathcal H
\),
i.e. by \emph{pure states}.
In practice, however, one often does not know the actual pure state exactly, but only a probability distribution over possible pure states.
Such a \emph{statistical mixture} is \textbf{not} the same thing as a superposition.
% It is represented by a \emph{density matrix}.
While the vector representation is sufficient for pure states, as soon as we want to describe statistical mixtures, it is more convenient to use an equivalent representation in terms of density matrices.

\begin{definition}[Density Matrix]\label{def:density-matrix}
A \hl[2]{density matrix} on a finite-dimensional Hilbert space \(\mathcal H\) is an operator
\(
\rho \in \operatorname{End}(\mathcal H)
\)
such that
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
\item 
\(\rho \ge 0\), i.e. \(\braket{\psi|\rho|\psi} \ge 0\) for every \(\psi \in \mathcal H\)
\item 
\(\Trace(\rho)=1\)
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{definition}

Since \(\rho\) is positive, it is in particular self-adjoint, and by the spectral theorem (\autoref{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded}; in the finite-dimensional case compare also \autoref{ex:finite-dimensional-case}) it admits a decomposition
\[
\rho
=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j P_{M_j}
=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j \sum_{k=1}^{\smash[t]{d}_j} \ket{\varphi_{j,k}}\bra{\varphi_{j,k}}
\]
where \(\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_r\ge 0\) are the distinct eigenvalues of \(\rho\),
\(M_j\) is the eigenspace corresponding to \(\lambda_j\),
\begin{equation}\label{eq:projector-eigenspace}
P_{M_j} = \sum_{k=1}^{\smash[t]{d}_j} P_{\varphi_{j,k}} = \sum_{k=1}^{\smash[t]{d}_j} \ket{\varphi_{j,k}}\bra{\varphi_{j,k}}
\end{equation}
is the orthogonal projection onto \(M_j\),
and
\(
\{\varphi_{j,1},\dots,\varphi_{j,d_j}\}
\)
is an orthonormal basis of \(M_j\), with
\(
d_j = \dim M_j
\).
Since
\[
\Trace(\rho)
=
\sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j  \Trace(P_{M_j})
=
\sum_{j=1}^r d_j \lambda_j
=
1
\]
the numbers \(d_j\lambda_j\) add up to one. 
Equivalently, if one lists eigenvalues with multiplicity, then
\[
\rho 
= 
\sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i \ket{\varphi_i}\bra{\varphi_i}
\]
with
\(
\lambda_i \ge 0
\)
and
\(
\sum_{i=1}^n \lambda_i = 1
\)
for some orthonormal basis of eigenvectors \(\{\varphi_i\}_{i=1}^n\) (compare also \autoref{ex:finite-dimensional-case}).
Thus the eigenvalues, counted with multiplicity, can be interpreted as probabilities of the system to be in the state of the corresponding eigenvector.

\begin{definition}[Mixed state]\label{def:mixed-state}
If the system is in the pure state \(\ket{\varphi_i}\) with probability \(p_i\), then the corresponding density matrix is exactly
\begin{equation}\label{eq:mixed-state-density-matrix}
\rho
=
\sum_i p_i  \ket{\varphi_i}\bra{\varphi_i}
\end{equation}
where \(p_i \ge 0\) and \(\sum_i p_i = 1\).
This is called a \emph{mixed state} or \emph{statistical mixture}.
\end{definition}

\begin{caution}
A normalized superposition
\(
\alpha \ket{\psi_1} + \beta \ket{\psi_2}
\)
with \(|\alpha|^2 + |\beta|^2 = 1\) 
is again a pure state.
By contrast,
\(
p \ket{\psi_1}\bra{\psi_1} + (1-p)\ket{\psi_2}\bra{\psi_2}
\)
is generally a mixed state.
So convex combinations of projectors are fundamentally different from linear combinations of vectors.
\end{caution}

The \hl{time evolution} of density matrices is induced by the unitary time evolution
\(
U(t)=\eu^{-\iu H t/\hbar}
\) 
of pure states (\autoref{sec:time-evolution}):
\begin{equation}\label{eq:density-matrix-time-evolution}
\rho(t)
=
U(t)\,\rho(0)\,U(t)^*
\end{equation}

For an observable \(A\), the \hl{probability} of obtaining a measurement outcome in a Borel set \(\omega \subseteq \sigma(A)\) is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:born-rule}
{\color{red}\boxed{\color{black}
\Prob_A^{\rho}(\omega)
=
\Trace\bigl(E_A(\omega)\rho\bigr)
}}
\end{equation}
where \(E_A\) is the spectral measure of \(A\) from \autoref{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded}.
In the finite-dimensional case, if
\[
A = \sum_{j=1}^r \lambda_j P_{M_j}
\]
with distinct eigenvalues \(\lambda_j\) and eigenspaces \(M_j\), then
\begin{equation}\label{eq:born-rule-density-matrix}
\Prob_A^{\rho}(\{\lambda_j\})
=
\Trace\bigl(P_{M_j}\rho\bigr)
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:projector-eigenspace}}}{=}
\Trace\left(\sum_{k=1}^{\smash[t]{d}_j} \ket{\varphi_{j,k}}\bra{\varphi_{j,k}} \rho\right)
\end{equation}
So in the degenerate case one must use the projection onto the whole eigenspace, not merely a (rank-one) projector onto a single eigenvector.



\begin{definition}[Pure State as a Density Matrix]\label{def:pure-state-density-matrix}
A density matrix \(\rho\) is called \emph{pure} if
\[
\rho = \ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}
\]
for some normalized \(\psi \in \mathcal H\).
\end{definition}

Equivalently, \(\rho\) is pure if and only if one eigenvalue equals \(1\) and all others are \(0\), or, equivalently, if and only if \(\rho\) has rank \(1\).
Thus pure states are precisely the rank-one orthogonal projections (compare also \autoref{eq:post-measurement-state} and \autoref{ex:finite-dimensional-case}).


\begin{overview}[pure vs. mixed states]\label{overview:pure-vs-mixed-states}
A density operator $\rho$ is
\begin{itemize}
\item pure iff $\rho^2 = \rho$  (i.e. $\rho$ is a rank-$1$ projection)
\item mixed iff $\rho^2 \neq \rho$
\end{itemize}
Equivalently,
\begin{itemize}
\item pure iff $\Trace(\rho^2) = 1$
\item mixed iff $\Trace(\rho^2) < 1$
\end{itemize}
In terms of the eigenvalues of $\rho$:
\begin{itemize}
\item pure iff $\rho$ has exactly one eigenvalue equal to $1$ and all others equal to $0$
\item mixed iff $\rho$ has more than one nonzero eigenvalue \qedhere
\end{itemize}
\end{overview}



For the density matrix of a pure state \(\rho = \ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}\), 
\eqref{eq:born-rule} becomes
% \[
% \begin{aligned}
% \Prob_A^{\rho}(\omega)
% &\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:born-rule}}}{=}
% \Trace\bigl(E_A(\omega)\ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}\bigr)
% \overset{\text{\eqref{eq:trace-definition}}}{=}
% \sum_{i=1}^n \bra{i} E_A(\omega) \ket{\psi}\bra{\psi} \ket{i} \\
% &=
% \sum_{i=1}^n \bra{\psi} \ket{i} \bra{i} E_A(\omega) \ket{\psi}
% =
% \bra{\psi} \left(\sum_{i=1}^n \ket{i}\bra{i}\right) E_A(\omega) \ket{\psi}
% =
% \bra{\psi} E_A(\omega) \ket{\psi}
% \end{aligned}
% \]
\[
% \begin{aligned}
\Prob_A^{\rho}(\omega)
% \overset{\text{\eqref{eq:born-rule}}}{=}
% \Trace\bigl(E_A(\omega)\ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}\bigr)
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:trace-definition}}}{=}
\sum_{i=1}^n \bra{i} E_A(\omega) \ket{\psi}\bra{\psi} \ket{i} 
=
% \sum_{i=1}^n \bra{\psi} \ket{i} \bra{i} E_A(\omega) \ket{\psi}
% =
\bra{\psi} \left(\sum_{i=1}^n \ket{i}\bra{i}\right) E_A(\omega) \ket{\psi}
=
\bra{\psi} E_A(\omega) \ket{\psi}
% \end{aligned}
\]
which is consistent with \eqref{eq:probability-measure-from-spectral-decomposition}.

For a pure state \(\ket{\psi}\) and an observable \(A\), the \hl{expectation value} can be written as
\[
\braket{A}_{\psi}
% =
% \braket{\psi|A|\psi}
=
\Trace\bigl(\ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}A\bigr)
\]
as seen in \autoref{sec:trace}.
This motivates the general formula (compare also \autoref{eq:expectation-value-observable})
\begin{equation}\label{eq:expectation-density-matrix}
{\color{red}\boxed{\color{black}
\braket{A}_{\rho}
=
\Trace(\rho A)
}}
\end{equation}
for arbitrary density matrices \(\rho\).

\begin{definition}[Separability and Entanglement]\label{def:separability-entanglement}
Let \(\mathcal H_A\) and \(\mathcal H_B\) be Hilbert spaces.
A pure state
\(
\psi \in \mathcal H_A \otimes \mathcal H_B
\)
is called \emph{separable} if it can be written as
\[
\psi = \varphi_A \otimes \varphi_B
\]
for some
\(
\varphi_A \in \mathcal H_A
\)
and
\(
\varphi_B \in \mathcal H_B
\).
Otherwise it is called \emph{entangled} (compare \autoref{sec:aspect-gisin-zeilinger-experiments}).
\end{definition}

\begin{example}\label{ex:singlet-state-entangled}
The singlet state $\ket{\Psi^-}$ from \autoref{sec:aspect-gisin-zeilinger-experiments}
% \[
% \ket{\Psi^-}
% =
% \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}
% \bigl(
% \ket{0}_A \otimes \ket{1}_B
% -
% \ket{1}_A \otimes \ket{0}_B
% \bigr)
% \]
is entangled.
It is a superposition (nor\-malized linear combination) of two pure states and hence a pure state itself.
\end{example}



\begin{digression}[Tensor products]\label{dig:tensor-products}
The tensor product combines two vector spaces \(V\) and \(W\) into a larger space
\[
V\otimes W
\]
whose elements are finite linear combinations of simple tensors
\[
\ket{v}\otimes\ket{w}
\]
with
\(
\ket{v}\in V,\ \ket{w}\in W
\).
If \(\dim V=m\) and \(\dim W=n\), then
\[
\dim(V\otimes W)=mn
\]
Moreover, if \(\{\ket{i}\}_i\) is a basis of \(V\) and \(\{\ket{j}\}_j\) is a basis of \(W\), then
\[
\{\ket{i}\otimes\ket{j}\}_{i,j}
\]
is a basis of \(V\otimes W\). In Dirac notation one often abbreviates
\[
\ket{v}\otimes\ket{w}
=
\ket{v}\ket{w}
=
\ket{v,w}
=
\ket{vw}
\]

The tensor product is bilinear, i.e.
\[
z(\ket{v}\otimes\ket{w})
=
(z\ket{v})\otimes\ket{w}
=
\ket{v}\otimes(z\ket{w})
\]
\[
(\ket{v_1}+\ket{v_2})\otimes\ket{w}
=
\ket{v_1}\otimes\ket{w}
+
\ket{v_2}\otimes\ket{w}
\]
\[
\ket{v}\otimes(\ket{w_1}+\ket{w_2})
=
\ket{v}\otimes\ket{w_1}
+
\ket{v}\otimes\ket{w_2}
\]

If \(A\colon V\to V'\) and \(B\colon W\to W'\) are linear maps, their tensor product
\[
A\otimes B \colon V\otimes W \to V'\otimes W'
\]
is defined by
\[
(A\otimes B)(\ket{v}\otimes\ket{w})
=
A\ket{v}\otimes B\ket{w}
\]
and extended linearly to all of \(V\otimes W\).
% In particular, for rank-one operators,
% \[
% (\ket{a}\bra{c})\otimes(\ket{b}\bra{d})
% =
% (\ket{a}\otimes\ket{b})(\bra{c}\otimes\bra{d}),
% \]
% so
% \[
% \ket{ij}\bra{kl}
% =
% \ket{i}\bra{k}\otimes\ket{j}\bra{l}.
% \]

If \(V\) and \(W\) are Hilbert spaces, then \(V\otimes W\) becomes a Hilbert space with inner product determined by
\begin{equation}\label{eq:inner-product-tensor-product}
{\color{red}\boxed{\color{black}
\braket{v_1\otimes w_1\,|\,v_2\otimes w_2}
=
\braket{v_1|v_2}\,\braket{w_1|w_2}
}}
\end{equation}
and extended sesquilinearly.
% Hence orthonormal bases tensor to orthonormal bases. % is that even a sentence?
Hence, if $\{\ket{i}\}_i$ and $\{\ket{j}\}_j$ are orthonormal bases of $V$ and $W$, then $\{\ket{i}\otimes\ket{j}\}_{i,j}$ is an orthonormal basis of $V\otimes W$.

For the trace of the tensor product of two operators \(A \in \operatorname{End}(V)\) and \(B \in \operatorname{End}(W)\) one has
\[
\begin{aligned}
\Trace(A\otimes B)
&=
\sum_{i,j} \braket{i \otimes j | A\otimes B | i \otimes j} \\
&=
\sum_{i,j} \braket{i|A|i}\,\braket{j|B|j} \\
&=
\left(\sum_i \braket{i|A|i}\right) \left(\sum_j \braket{j|B|j}\right) \\
&=
\Trace(A)\,\Trace(B)
\end{aligned}
\]

In matrix form, the tensor product is represented by the Kronecker product. For matrices
\(
\matr{A}=(a_{ij})
\),
\(
\matr{B}
\),
one has
\[
\matr{A} \otimes \matr{B}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
a_{11}\matr{B} & \cdots & a_{1n}\matr{B}\\
\vdots  & \ddots & \vdots \\
a_{m1}\matr{B} & \cdots & a_{mn}\matr{B}
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Useful identities include
\(
(\matr{A} \otimes \matr{B})^*  = \matr{A}^* \otimes \matr{B}^* 
\).
% \(
% (\matr{A}\otimes \matr{B})^\top = \matr{A}^\top\otimes \matr{B}^\top
% \),
% \(
% (\matr{A}\otimes \matr{B})^\dagger = \matr{A}^\dagger\otimes \matr{B}^\dagger
% \).
In particular, if \(\matr{A}\) and \(\matr{B}\) are unitary, Hermitian, positive, or projectors, then \(\matr{A}\otimes \matr{B}\) has the same property.
% Finally, for repeated tensor products one writes
% \[
% \ket{\psi}^{\otimes k}
% =
% \underbrace{\ket{\psi}\otimes\cdots\otimes\ket{\psi}}_{k\text{ times}},
% \qquad
% A^{\otimes k}
% =
% \underbrace{A\otimes\cdots\otimes A}_{k\text{ times}}.
% \]
\end{digression}

However, parts of an entangled state are mixed states.
To understand what Alice's part of the entangled system from \autoref{ex:singlet-state-entangled} looks like, we introduce the %\emph{partial trace}.
\begin{definition}[Partial Trace]\label{def:partial-trace}
To describe the state of only one subsystem, we use the linear map % which is the density-matrix analogue of the discussion of local measurements in bipartite systems. % what discussion?
\[
\Trace_B \colon \operatorname{End}(\mathcal H_A \otimes \mathcal H_B) \to \operatorname{End}(\mathcal H_A)
\]
defined on simple tensors by
\[
\Trace_B(S_A \otimes S_B) \coloneq \Trace(S_B)\,S_A
\]
and extended linearly.
Analogously one defines \(\Trace_A\).
\end{definition}

\begin{example}\label{ex:trace-out-one-subsystem}
The density matrix of the singlet state is
\[
\begin{aligned}
\rho
=
\ket{\Psi^-}\bra{\Psi^-}
&=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{01}-\ket{10}) \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\bra{01}-\bra{10})
\\
&=
\frac12\bigl(
\ket{01}\bra{01}
-
\ket{01}\bra{10}
-
\ket{10}\bra{01}
+
\ket{10}\bra{10}
\bigr)
\end{aligned}
\]
Using
% \[
% \Trace_B\bigl(\ket{i}\bra{k}\otimes \ket{j}\bra{l}\bigr)
% =
% \Trace(\ket{j}\bra{l})\,\ket{i}\bra{k}
% = % why?
% \braket{l|j}\,\ket{i}\bra{k}
% =
% \delta_{lj}\,\ket{i}\bra{k},
% \]
\[
\Trace(\ket{i}\bra{j})
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:trace-definition}}}{=}
\sum_k \bra{k}\ket{i}\bra{j}\ket{k}
% =
% \sum_k \braket{k|i}\braket{j|k}
=
\bra{j}\left(\sum_k \ket{k}\bra{k}\right)\ket{i}
=
\braket{j|i}
=
\delta_{ji}
\]
we get
\[
\begin{aligned}
&\Trace_B \bigl(\ket{\Psi^-}\bra{\Psi^-}\bigr)
\\
&=
\frac12\Bigl(
\Trace_B(\ket{01}\bra{01})
-
\Trace_B(\ket{01}\bra{10})
-
\Trace_B(\ket{10}\bra{01})
+
\Trace_B(\ket{10}\bra{10})
\Bigr)
\\
&= % this is not justified???
\frac12\Bigl(
\Trace_B(\ket{0}\bra{0}\otimes \ket{1}\bra{1})
-
\Trace_B(\ket{0}\bra{1}\otimes \ket{1}\bra{0})
% \\
% &\qquad\qquad
% -
% \Trace_B(\ket{1}\bra{0}\otimes \ket{0}\bra{1})
% +
% \Trace_B(\ket{1}\bra{1}\otimes \ket{0}\bra{0})
% \Bigr)
-
%\mathrlap%
{
\Trace_B(\ket{1}\bra{0}\otimes \ket{0}\bra{1})
+
\Trace_B(\ket{1}\bra{1}\otimes \ket{0}\bra{0})
\Bigr)
}
\\
&=
\frac12\Bigl(
\Trace(\ket{1}\bra{1})\,\ket{0}\bra{0}
-
\Trace(\ket{1}\bra{0})\,\ket{0}\bra{1}
% \\
% &\qquad\qquad
-
%\mathrlap%
{
\Trace(\ket{0}\bra{1})\,\ket{1}\bra{0}
+
\Trace(\ket{0}\bra{0})\,\ket{1}\bra{1}
\Bigr)
}
\\
% &=
% \frac12\bigl(
% \ket{0}\bra{0}
% -
% 0
% -
% 0
% +
% \ket{1}\bra{1}
% \bigr)
% \\
&=
\frac12\bigl(\ket{0}\bra{0}+\ket{1}\bra{1}\bigr)
=
\frac{\id_A}{2}
\end{aligned}
\]
and similarly
\(
\Trace_A \bigl(\ket{\Psi^-}\bra{\Psi^-}\bigr)
=
\frac12\bigl(\ket{0}\bra{0}+\ket{1}\bra{1}\bigr)
=
\frac{\id_B}{2}
\).
So each subsystem is in the \emph{maximally mixed state}.

Importantly, the partial trace contains all information about local measurements.
If Alice performs a measurement corresponding to an observable $A$ on her subsystem and \(E_A\) denotes its spectral measure from \autoref{thm:spectral-theorem-unbounded}, then the event that Alice's measurement outcome lies in a Borel set \(\omega \subseteq \sigma(A)\) is represented on the joint system by the projection
\(
E_A(\omega)\otimes \id_B
\)
since the measurement acts on Alice's subsystem and 
trivially (meaning as the identity, i.e. Bob's subsystem is left unchanged) 
on Bob's.
Therefore, by the Born rule \eqref{eq:born-rule},
\[
\Prob_A^{\rho}(\omega)
=
\Trace\bigl((E_A(\omega)\otimes \id_B)\rho\bigr)
\]
which can be split into a partial trace over Bob's subsystem and a subsequent trace over Alice's subsystem,
\[
\Prob_A^{\rho}(\omega)
=
\Trace\bigl(E_A(\omega)\Trace_B(\rho)\bigr)
\]
where we used \autoref{thm:partial-trace-local-observables}.
Hence Bob's subsystem influences Alice's local statistics only through the reduced density matrix \(\Trace_B(\rho)\).
In particular, local operations on Bob's side alone cannot be used to transmit information to Alice, saving us from problems with relativity.
\end{example}


\begin{theorem}[Partial trace and local observables]\label{thm:partial-trace-local-observables}
Let $\mathcal H_A$ and $\mathcal H_B$ be finite-dimensional Hilbert spaces.
Let
\(
\rho \in \operatorname{End}(\mathcal H_A \otimes \mathcal H_B)
\) and
\(
A \in \operatorname{End}(\mathcal H_A)
\).
Then
\begin{itemize}
\item
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:partial-trace-pull-through-left}
\(
\Trace_B\bigl((A\otimes \id_B)\rho\bigr)
=
A\,\Trace_B(\rho)
\)
% \end{equation}
\item
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:local-observable-partial-trace}
\(
\Trace\bigl((A\otimes \id_B)\rho\bigr)
=
\Trace_A\bigl(A\,\Trace_B(\rho)\bigr)
\)
% \end{equation}
\item
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:partial-trace-pull-through-right}
\(
\Trace_B\bigl(\rho(A\otimes \id_B)\bigr)
=
\Trace_B(\rho)\,A 
\)
\qedhere
% \end{equation}
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}


























 \clearpage

\section{Qbits}




We now consider finite-dimensional quantum systems.
Since quantum theory is linear, restricting to such spaces does not alter the underlying physics. 
The basic unit is the quantum bit, or Qbit, corresponding to a $2$-dimensional Hilbert space. 
Unlike classical bits, however, understanding a single Qbit is not enough to understand systems of several Qbits, 
because qualitatively new phenomena arise there, most importantly entanglement, which lies at the heart of the power of quantum information processing.

\subsection{One Qbit: $\mathcal{H} = \C^2$}
\label{sec:one-qbit}


A quantum bit, or Qbit, or {Qbit} is a quantum system whose state space is
\[
\mathcal H = \C^2
\]
We choose the standard orthonormal basis
\[
\ket{0}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
1\\0
\end{bmatrix}
\qquad
\ket{1}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
0\\1
\end{bmatrix}
\]
called the \hl{\emph{computational basis}}.

A general pure state of one Qbit is
\[
\ket{\psi}
=
\alpha \ket{0} + \beta \ket{1}
\]
with 
\(
\alpha,\beta \in \C
\),
\(
|\alpha|^2+|\beta|^2=1
\).

At first sight this seems to involve four real parameters minus one normalization condition, hence three real degrees of freedom.
But one more degree of freedom is physically irrelevant:
If $c \in \C$, \(|c|=1\), then
\[
% P_{c\ket{\psi}}
% =
  (c\ket{\psi})(c\ket{\psi})^*
  =
  c\ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}\overline{c}
  =
  \ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}
% =
% P_{\ket{\psi}}
\]
Thus \(\ket{\psi}\) and \(c\ket{\psi}\) represent the same physical pure state.
Global phase factors cannot be observed.

Mathematically, two vectors are said to be equivalent if and only of they diﬀer simply by a global phase factor. 
Quantum states then correspond to equivalence relations with respect to that relation, sometimes called ``unit rays''. 



So a pure one-Qbit state is really determined by only two real parameters.
A convenient para\-metrization is
\[
\begin{aligned}
\ket{\psi}
&=
\alpha \ket{0} + \beta \ket{1} \\
&=
\eu^{\iu \varphi_\alpha}|\alpha| \ket{0} + \eu^{\iu \varphi_\beta}|\beta| \ket{1} \\
&=
\eu^{\iu \varphi_\alpha}\left(|\alpha| \ket{0} + \eu^{\iu (\varphi_\beta-\varphi_\alpha)}|\beta| \ket{1}\right) \\
&\sim
|\alpha| \ket{0} + \eu^{\iu (\varphi_\beta-\varphi_\alpha)}|\beta| \ket{1} \\
&= 
\cos\frac{\Theta}{2}\ket{0}
+
\eu^{\iu\varphi}\sin\frac{\Theta}{2}\ket{1}
\end{aligned}
\]
with
\(
\Theta \in [0,\pi]
\)
and
\emph{relative phase}
\(
\varphi \in [0,2\pi)
\).
% This leads to the Bloch sphere representation of pure one-Qbit states.
% compare also the discussion of spin-$\tfrac{1}{2}$ systems in \autoref{sec:stern-gerlach-experiment}.
A representation of states which takes into account the irrelevance of global phases is the Bloch sphere. 
In this representation of the unit rays, or projectors, ``orthogonal'' becomes ``antipodal''.



This equals the coordinate system on earth (including the fact that the poles do not have a well-defined longitude).

Orientation on the Bloch sphere:
\begin{itemize}
%   \item Computational Basis: $\ket{0}$, $\ket{1}$ 
\item Standard / Computational / Z Basis: $\ket{0}$, $\ket{1}$ 
%   \item Diagonal States: $\ket{+} = \frac{\ket{0} + \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}$, $\ket{-} = \frac{\ket{0} - \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}$
\item Diagonal / Hadamard / X Basis: $\ket{+} = \frac{\ket{0} + \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}$, $\ket{-} = \frac{\ket{0} - \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}$
%   \item Circular States: $\ket{\iu} = \smash[t]{\frac{\ket{0} + \iu \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}}$, $\ket{-\iu} = \smash[t]{\frac{\ket{0} - \iu \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}}$
\item Circular / Y Basis: $\ket{\iu} = \smash[t]{\frac{\ket{0} + \iu \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}}$, $\ket{-\iu} = \smash[t]{\frac{\ket{0} - \iu \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}}$
\end{itemize}

% \tikzset{external/export next=false}
\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.75, line cap=round, line join=round, font=\footnotesize]
\def\R{6}
\def\thetaState{33}
\def\phiState{33}
\def\rTheta{3}
\def\rPhi{3}

% spherical outline (screen-space circle)
\draw[black!85, line width=0.9] (0,0) circle (\R);


% 3D projection basis (all key points/arcs are specified in 3D coordinates)
% \begin{scope}[x={(1cm,-0.00cm)}, y={(-0.0cm,-0.3cm)}, z={(0cm,1cm)}]
\def\planeRot{28}

\pgfmathsetmacro{\xx}{cos(\planeRot)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\xy}{-0.3*sin(\planeRot)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yx}{-sin(\planeRot)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\yy}{-0.3*cos(\planeRot)}

\begin{scope}[
  x={(\xx cm,\xy cm)},
  y={(\yx cm,\yy cm)},
  z={(0cm, 1cm)}
]
\coordinate (O) at (0,0,0);
\coordinate (Xp) at (\R,0,0);
\coordinate (Xm) at (-\R,0,0);
\coordinate (Yp) at (0,\R,0);
\coordinate (Ym) at (0,-\R,0);
\coordinate (Zp) at (0,0,\R);
\coordinate (Zm) at (0,0,-\R);

\pgfmathsetmacro{\sx}{\R*sin(\thetaState)*cos(\phiState)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\sy}{\R*sin(\thetaState)*sin(\phiState)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\sz}{\R*cos(\thetaState)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\projlength}{sqrt(\sx^2+\sy^2)}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\sprojx}{\R*\sx/\projlength}
\pgfmathsetmacro{\sprojy}{\R*\sy/\projlength}
\coordinate (Psi) at (\sx,\sy,\sz);
\coordinate (PsiProj) at (\sprojx,\sprojy,0);

% equator (xy-plane): back half dashed, front half solid
\draw[black!70, dashed] plot[variable=\t, domain=180-\planeRot:360-\planeRot, samples=90, smooth]
({\R*cos(\t)},{\R*sin(\t)},0);
\draw[black!85] plot[variable=\t, domain=0-\planeRot:180-\planeRot, samples=90, smooth]
({\R*cos(\t)},{\R*sin(\t)},0);

% axes and auxiliary directions
\draw[black!70, dashed] (Ym) -- (O);
\draw[black!70] (O) -- (Yp);
\draw[black!85, ] (O) -- (Zp);
\draw[black!85, , dashed] (O) -- (Zm);
\draw[black!75] (O) -- (Xp);
\draw[black!70, dashed] (O) -- (Xm);

% meridian in the plane spanned by z-axis and PsiProj
\draw[black!85!black, ] plot[variable=\a, domain=0:180, samples=140, smooth] ({\R*sin(\a)*cos(\phiState)},{\R*sin(\a)*sin(\phiState)},{\R*cos(\a)});


% Bloch vector and its xy-plane projection
\draw[red!85!black, line width=1.2, -{Latex[length=2.7mm]}] (O) -- (Psi) node [pos=0.95, above right] {$\ket{\psi}$};
\draw[black!85!black, ] (O) -- (PsiProj);

% theta arc in the meridian plane
\draw[black!85, ->] plot[variable=\a, domain=0:\thetaState, samples=80, smooth] ({\rTheta*sin(\a)*cos(\phiState)},{\rTheta*sin(\a)*sin(\phiState)},{\rTheta*cos(\a)});

% phi arc in the equatorial plane (from +y axis to PsiProj direction)
\draw[black!70!black,  ->] plot[variable=\a, domain=0:\phiState, samples=70, smooth] ({\rPhi*cos(\a)},{\rPhi*sin(\a)},0);

\coordinate (ThetaLabelPos) at
({1.18*\rTheta*sin(0.5*\thetaState)*cos(\phiState)},
 {1.18*\rTheta*sin(0.5*\thetaState)*sin(\phiState)},
 {1.18*\rTheta*cos(0.5*\thetaState)});
\coordinate (PhiLabelPos) at
({1.0*\rPhi*cos(0.5*\phiState)},
 {1.0*\rPhi*sin(0.5*\phiState)},
 0);
\end{scope}

\foreach \P in {Zp,Zm,Yp,Ym,Xp,Xm}
  \fill (\P) circle (1.8pt);

\node[black!85, above=3pt] at (Zp) {$\ket{0}$};
\node[black!85, below=3pt] at (Zm) {$\ket{1}$};

% \node[below left] at (Yp) {$\ket{\iu} = \frac{\ket{0} + \iu \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}$};
% \node[above right] at (Ym) {$\ket{-\iu} = \frac{\ket{0} - \iu \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}$};
% \node[below right] at (Xp) {$\ket{+} = \frac{\ket{0} + \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}$};
% \node[above left] at (Xm) {$\ket{-} = \frac{\ket{0} - \ket{1}}{\sqrt{2}}$};
\node[below left] at (Yp) {$\ket{\iu}$};
\node[above right] at (Ym) {$\ket{-\iu}$};
\node[below right, xshift=-0.5em] at (Xp) {$\ket{+}$};
\node[above left, xshift=0.5em] at (Xm) {$\ket{-}$};

\node[black!85] at (ThetaLabelPos) {$\Theta$};
\node[black!70!black, below right] at (PhiLabelPos) {$\varphi$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}





\medskip


% BEGIN: from Wikipedia; https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bloch_sphere

While the surface of the Bloch sphere represents pure states, the interior corresponds to mixed states \cite{wikipediaBlochSphere}.
To see this, recall that a mixed state is represented by a density operator $\rho$.
% For mixed states, one considers the density operator. 
Any two-dimensional density operator $\rho$ can be expanded using the identity $\matr{I}$ and the Hermitian, traceless Pauli matrices 
$
\vect{\sigma} = [\matr{\sigma}_x, \matr{\sigma}_y, \matr{\sigma}_z]
$,
\[
\begin{aligned}
\rho 
&=
\frac{1}{2}(\matr{I} + \vect{a} \cdot \vect{\sigma}) 
\\
&=
\frac{1}{2}
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0 \\
0 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
+
\frac{a_x}{2}
\begin{bmatrix}
0 & 1 \\
1 & 0
\end{bmatrix}
+
\frac{a_y}{2}
\begin{bmatrix}
0 & -\iu \\
\iu & 0
\end{bmatrix}
+
\frac{a_z}{2}
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0 \\
0 & -1
\end{bmatrix}
\\
&=\frac{1}{2}
\begin{bmatrix}
1+a_z & a_x-\iu a_y \\
a_x+\iu a_y & 1-a_z
\end{bmatrix}
\end{aligned}
\]
where 
$
\vect{a} = [a_x, a_y, a_z] \in \R^3
$ 
is called the Bloch vector.

% \begin{digression}
% This is because $\{\matr{I}, \matr{\sigma}_x, \matr{\sigma}_y, \matr{\sigma}_z\}$ is a basis of the vector space of $2\times 2$ Hermitian matrices.
% This space consists a priori of 4 real dimensions, since
% a general Hermitian $2\times2$ matrix can be written as
% $
% \rho
% =
% c \matr{I} + a_x \matr{\sigma}_x + a_y \matr{\sigma}_y + a_z \matr{\sigma}_z
% $.

% But density matrices satisfy
% $
% \operatorname{tr}(\rho)=1
% $
% and the Pauli matrices are traceless, hence
% $
% \operatorname{tr}(\rho)
% =
% \operatorname{tr}(cI)
% =
% 2c$, so
% $
% c=\frac12
% $.

% So the identity coefficient is not free. 
% It is fixed by normalization.

% That is why we write
% \[
% \rho
% =
% \frac12(\matr{I}+\vect{a} \cdot \vect{\sigma})
% \]

% The remaining freedom is exactly the 3 real coordinates
% $(a_x,a_y,a_z)$.


% However, the affine space of trace-one Hermitian matrices is 3-dimensional. 
% The Bloch ball lives in that 3-dimensional affine slice.
% \end{digression}
\begin{digression}
This is because $\{\matr{I}, \matr{\sigma}_x, \matr{\sigma}_y, \matr{\sigma}_z\}$ is a basis of the vector space of $2\times 2$ Hermitian matrices.
This space has 4 real dimensions, since a general Hermitian $2\times2$ matrix can be written as
$
\rho
=
c \matr{I} + a_x \matr{\sigma}_x + a_y \matr{\sigma}_y + a_z \matr{\sigma}_z
$.

For density matrices, however, the normalization condition
$
\operatorname{tr}(\rho)=1
$
removes one degree of freedom.
Since the Pauli matrices are traceless,
$
\operatorname{tr}(\rho)
=
\operatorname{tr}(c\matr{I})
=
2c
$,
so
$
c=\frac12
$.

Thus the identity coefficient is not free, but fixed by normalization.
The remaining freedom is exactly the 3 real coordinates
$(a_x,a_y,a_z)$.

Equivalently, the trace-one Hermitian matrices form a 3-dimensional affine slice of the 4-dimensional real vector space of Hermitian matrices.
The Bloch ball lives in that affine slice.
\end{digression}

It is this Bloch vector $\vect{a}$ that indicates the point within the sphere that corresponds to a given mixed state. 
% Specifically, as a basic feature of the Pauli vector, 
It can be shown that
the eigenvalues of $\rho$ are $\lambda_{\pm} = \frac{1}{2}(1 \pm \|\vect{a}\|)$. 
Density operators must be positive-semidefinite, so it follows that $\|\vect{a}\| \leq 1$.

We \rlap{have}
\[
% \begin{aligned}
\Trace(\rho^2)
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:spectral-theorem-finite-dimensional}}}{=}
\Trace(
\matr{U} \matr{\Lambda} \matr{U}^* \matr{U} \matr{\Lambda} \matr{U}^*
) 
% \\
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:trace-basis-independence}}}{=}
\Trace(\matr{\Lambda}^2)
% \\
=
\lambda_+^2+\lambda_-^2
% \\
% =
% \left(\frac{1+\|\vect a\|}{2}\right)^2
% +
% \left(\frac{1-\|\vect a\|}{2}\right)^2\\
% &=
% \frac{
% (1+\|\vect a\|)^2
% +
% (1-\|\vect a\|)^2
% }{4}\\
% &=
% \frac{2+2\|\vect a\|^2}{4}
% \\
=
\frac12(1+\|\vect a\|^2)
% \end{aligned}
\]
% \[
% \Trace(\rho^2)=\frac{1}{2}(1 + \|\vect{a}\|^2)=1 \quad \Leftrightarrow \quad\|\vect{a}\|=1
% \]
which, by \autoref{overview:pure-vs-mixed-states}, is equal to \(1\) if and only if \(\rho\) is pure.
Hence $\|\vect{a}\|=1$ if and only if $\rho$ is pure.
% confirming that
% the surface of the Bloch sphere represents all the pure states of a two-dimensional quantum system, whereas the interior corresponds to all the mixed states.


For a pure state
\(
\ket{\psi}
% =
% \cos\frac{\Theta}{2}\ket0
% +
% \eu^{\iu\varphi}\sin\frac{\Theta}{2}\ket1
=
[\cos\frac{\Theta}{2}, \eu^{\iu\varphi}\sin\frac{\Theta}{2}]^\top
\)
the corresponding density matrix is
\[
% \begin{aligned}
\rho
=
\ket{\psi}\bra{\psi}
=
% \begin{bmatrix}
% \cos\frac{\Theta}{2}\\
% \eu^{\iu\varphi}\sin\frac{\Theta}{2}
% \end{bmatrix}
% \begin{bmatrix}
% \cos\frac{\Theta}{2} &
% \eu^{-\iu\varphi}\sin\frac{\Theta}{2}
% \end{bmatrix}
% \\
% &=
% \begin{bmatrix}
% \cos^2\frac{\Theta}{2}
% &
% \eu^{-\iu\varphi}\sin\frac{\Theta}{2}\cos\frac{\Theta}{2}
% \\
% \eu^{\iu\varphi}\sin\frac{\Theta}{2}\cos\frac{\Theta}{2}
% &
% \sin^2\frac{\Theta}{2}
% \end{bmatrix}
% \\
% &=
% \frac12
% \begin{bmatrix}
% 1+\cos\Theta
% &
% \eu^{-\iu\varphi}\sin\Theta
% \\
% \eu^{\iu\varphi}\sin\Theta
% &
% 1-\cos\Theta
% \end{bmatrix}
% \\
\frac12
\begin{bmatrix}
1+\cos\Theta
&
\sin\Theta\cos\varphi-\iu\sin\Theta\sin\varphi
\\
\sin\Theta\cos\varphi+\iu\sin\Theta\sin\varphi
&
1-\cos\Theta
\end{bmatrix}
\]
Comparing this with
% \[
% \rho
% =
% \frac12
% \begin{bmatrix}
% 1+a_z & a_x-\iu a_y\\
% a_x+\iu a_y & 1-a_z
% \end{bmatrix}
% \]
the expansion of $\rho$ in the Pauli basis above, we obtain
\[
\vect a
=
\begin{bmatrix}
\sin\Theta\cos\varphi\\
\sin\Theta\sin\varphi\\
\cos\Theta
\end{bmatrix}
\]
which is exactly the point on the unit sphere introduced above.
Together with
% \[
% \Trace(\rho^2)=1
% \quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
% \|\vect a\|=1,
% \]
with the previous calculation
this shows that the surface of the Bloch sphere represents precisely the pure states, whereas its interior corresponds to mixed states.


% END: from Wikipedia



\medskip

Operations on one Qbit are unitary \(2\times2\)-matrices.
\begin{example}\label{ex:hadamard}
A central example is the Hadamard gate \(H\). Its coordinate matrix in the computational basis is
\(
\matr{H}
=
\smash[b]{
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}
}
\left[
\begin{smallmatrix}
1 & 1\\
1 & -1
\end{smallmatrix}
\right]
\), 
introduced in \autoref{sec:quantum-bit}.
It
satisfies
\(
H^2=\id
\),
and, hence, is not only unitary but also an involution (and, therefore, by \autoref{thm:unitary-selfadjoint-involution}, also self-adjoint).
For $b \in\{0,1\}$,
\[
% \matr{H}\ket{b}=\frac{\smash[t]{\ket{0}+(-1)^b\ket{1}}}{\smash[b]{\sqrt{2}}}
H\ket{b}=\frac{{\ket{0}+(-1)^b\ket{1}}}{{\sqrt{2}}}
\]
i.e. the Hadamard maps
\(
% \matr{H}
\ket{0}
% =
\mapsto
% \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{0}+\ket{1})
\ket{+}
\), 
\(
% \matr{H}
\ket{1}
% =
\mapsto
% \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{0}-\ket{1})
\ket{-}
\), so its action on the Bloch sphere includes a rotation of $\pi/2$ around the axis connecting the circular polarizations $\ket{\iu}, \ket{-\iu}$.
Hovever, it also maps $\ket{\iu} \mapsto \ket{-\iu}$ and $\ket{-\iu} \mapsto \ket{\iu}$, i.e. it exchanges the two circular basis states.
So the first rotation must be combined with a second rotation of $\pi$ around the axis connecting the diagonal states $\ket{+}, \ket{-}$.
\end{example}

Since arbitrary measurements in any orthonormal basis can be reduced to a unitary basis change followed by a standard-basis measurement, as discussed in \autoref{sec:observables}, it is enough to understand measurements in the basis \(\{\ket{0},\ket{1}\}\).
For instance, a measurement in the diagonal basis is a Hadamard transform followed by a standard measurement.





\subsection{Two Qbits: $\mathcal{H} = \C^4$}

% For two Qbits the state space is
% \[
% \mathcal{H} = \C^2 \otimes \C^2 \cong \C^4
% \]
% with computational basis
% \(
% \{\ket{00},\ket{01},\ket{10},\ket{11}\}
% \).

\begin{definition}[Product State]
A product state has the form
\(
\ket{\psi_1}\otimes\ket{\psi_2}
\).
\end{definition}
% \begin{example}
% \(\sqrt{2/3}\ket{00}+\sqrt{1/3}\ket{01}\) is a product state.
% \end{example}

% \begin{example}
% \(
% \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{00}+\ket{01})
% \) 
% and
% \(
% \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{00}+\ket{10})
% \)
% are product states.
% \end{example}

Not every state of two Qbits is of this form.
\begin{example}
\label{ex:bell-states}
The Bell states, which are the following maximally entangled two-Qbit states,
\[
\begin{aligned}
\ket{\Phi^+}
&=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{00}+\ket{11}) \\
\ket{\Phi^-}
&=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{00}-\ket{11}) \\
\ket{\Psi^+}
&=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{01}+\ket{10}) \\
\ket{\Psi^-}
&=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(\ket{01}-\ket{10})
\end{aligned}
\]
cannot be written as a product state,
%  and are therefore entangled,
see \autoref{sec:aspect-gisin-zeilinger-experiments}.
They are also called EPR (Einstein-Po\-dol\-sky-Rosen) pairs. 
They form an orthonormal basis of the two-Qbit Hilbert space, hence together they are often referred to as the Bell basis.
We will see how to translate between the computational basis and the Bell basis in \autoref{sec:cnot-gate}.
\end{example}


\begin{remark}
% For product states \(\psi_{1,A} \otimes \psi_{1,B}\) and \(\psi_{2,A} \otimes \psi_{2,B}\), the sum
% \[
% \psi_{1,A}\otimes \psi_{1,B} + \psi_{2,A}\otimes \psi_{2,B}
% \]
% is a product state if and only if either \(\psi_{1,A}\) and \(\psi_{2,A}\) are linearly dependent or \(\psi_{1,B}\) and \(\psi_{2,B}\) are linearly dependent.
% In particular, a 
A general two-Qbit pure state
\[
\ket{\psi}
=
\alpha\ket{00}+\beta\ket{01}+\gamma\ket{10}+\delta\ket{11}
\]
% is a product state if and only if
% \[
% \alpha\delta-\beta\gamma=0.
% \]
% Indeed, \(\ket{\psi}\) 
is a product state if and only if there exist \(a,b,c,d\in\C\) such that
\[
\ket{\psi}
=
(a\ket0+b\ket1)\otimes(c\ket0+d\ket1)
\]
and expanding this gives
% \[
% \alpha=ac \qquad \beta=ad \qquad \gamma=bc \qquad \delta=bd 
% \]
\(
\alpha=ac\),  \(\beta=ad\),  \(\gamma=bc\),  \(\delta=bd 
\). 
Thus the coefficient matrix
\[
\matr{A} \coloneq % matrix good
\begin{bmatrix}
\alpha & \beta\\
\gamma & \delta
\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
a\\ b
\end{bmatrix}
\begin{bmatrix}
c & d
\end{bmatrix}
\]
must have rank \(1\). 
Conversely, every rank-\(1\) matrix admits such a factorization, hence determines a product state. 
% Since \(\matr{A}\neq \matr{0}\) for a nonzero state vector, and since for a nonzero \(2\times 2\) matrix rank \(1\) is equivalent to vanishing determinant, this is equivalent to
% \(
% \det(\matr{A})=\alpha\delta-\beta\gamma=0
% \).
\end{remark}

The state space of a Qbit pair is the tensor product of the individual spaces, which is the span of the set of product states
\[
\Span{\{\ket{\psi_1 \psi_2}\}}
=
\Span\{\ket{00},\ket{01},\ket{10},\ket{11}\}
=
\C^2 \otimes \C^2
\cong
\C^4
\]

% \subsubsection{$n$ Qbits: $\mathcal{H} = \C^{2^n}$}
Analogously, 
for \(n\) Qbits, the state space is
\[
\Span(\{\ket{\vect{x}} : \vect{x} \in \{0,1\}^n\})
=
\left(\C^2\right)^{\otimes n}
% =
% \underbrace{\C^2 \otimes \cdots \otimes \C^2}_{n\text{ factors}}
\cong
\C^{2^n}
\]
with computational basis indexed by classical \(n\)-bit strings.
% where a basis is given by the classical \(n\)-bit strings, $\{\ket{\vect{x}} : \vect{x} \in \{0,1\}^n\}$.

On the level of coordinates, the tensor product is given by the Kronecker product:
\[
\begin{bmatrix}
a\\b
\end{bmatrix}
\otimes
