(-1)^{\vect{i}\cdot(\vect{x}\oplus\vect{y})}
\right)
\ket{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{y}}
\end{aligned}
\]
If \(\vect{a}=\vect{b}\), then \(a_{\vect{i}}\oplus b_{\vect{i}}=0\) for every \(\vect{i}\), so the state is
\[
\begin{aligned}
\frac{1}{\sqrt{N^3}}
\sum_{\vect{x}}
\sum_{\vect{y}}
\left(
\sum_{\vect{i}}
(-1)^{\vect{i}\cdot(\vect{x}\oplus\vect{y})}
\right)
\ket{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{y}}
&=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{N^3}}
\sum_{\vect{x}}
\sum_{\vect{y}}
% \begin{cases}
% N & \text{if }\vect{x}=\vect{y}\\
% 0 & \text{if }\vect{x}\neq\vect{y}
% \end{cases}
N \delta_{\vect{x},\vect{y}}
\ket{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{y}}
\\
&=
\frac{1}{\sqrt N}
\sum_{\vect{x}}
\ket{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{x}}
\end{aligned}
\]
Thus the outputs are always equal.

If \(d_{\text{Ham}}(\vect{a},\vect{b})=N/2\), then exactly half of the values
\(
(-1)^{a_{\vect{i}}\oplus b_{\vect{i}}}
\)
are \(+1\), and exactly half are \(-1\).
For equal outputs \(\vect{x}=\vect{y}\), the corresponding part of the state has coefficient
\[
\frac{1}{\sqrt{N^3}}
\sum_{\vect{i}}
(-1)^{a_{\vect{i}}\oplus b_{\vect{i}}}
(-1)^{\vect{i}\cdot(\vect{x}\oplus\vect{x})}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{N^3}}
\sum_{\vect{i}}
(-1)^{a_{\vect{i}}\oplus b_{\vect{i}}}
=
0
\]
because \(\vect{x}\oplus\vect{x}=\vect0\).
Thus equal outputs are impossible in the second promised case, and the entangled strategy wins with certainty.







\subsection{Kochen--Specker theorem}\label{sec:ernst-specker}
% \[
% \iu^{\iu} 
% = 
% \left(\eu^{\left(\frac{\pi}{2} + 2\pi \Z\right)\iu}\right)^{\iu} 
% =
% \eu^{-\frac{\pi}{2} - 2\pi \Z}
% \]

Ernst Specker argued that classical logic is inadequate for describing quantum mechanics
\cite{speckerLogikNichtGleichzeitigEntscheidbarerAussagen}.
Like Bell's theorem (\autoref{claim:bell}), discussed in \autoref{sec:aspect-gisin-zeilinger-experiments}, his argument rules out a classical deterministic explanation.
% The distinction is that Bell separates measurements in space, whereas Specker asks whether outcomes can be assigned consistently to all possible measurements of one quantum system.
The common structure is that the result should not depend on a choice that, classically, seems irrelevant.
In Bell's theorem, Alice's predetermined answer should not depend on Bob's distant measurement choice; this is the locality assumption.
In the Kochen--Specker theorem, the predetermined value of a ray should not depend on which other orthogonal rays are measured with it; this is the noncontextuality assumption.
Thus \autoref{claim:bell} rules out deterministic hidden variables plus \emph{locality}, whereas \autoref{thm:kochen-specker} rules out deterministic hidden variables plus \emph{noncontextuality}.
In Specker's words, ``quantum mechanics is bad for seers'': 
a prophet who claims to know all future measurement outcomes does not merely risk being contradicted by a later experiment; 
the full table of predictions is already internally inconsistent.

\subsubsection*{The parable of the overprotective seer}

Specker's 1960 article \cite{speckerLogikNichtGleichzeitigEntscheidbarerAussagen} begins with a parable.
A seer presents three boxes to each suitor of his daughter.
The suitor may choose and open any two boxes, and exactly one of those two contains a jewel, but the remaining box can never be opened:
\[
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  box/.style={draw,rounded corners=1.5pt,minimum width=1.5cm,minimum height=1.5cm, thick},
  pair/.style={draw,rounded corners,inner xsep=0.55em,inner ysep=0.28em},
  every node/.style={font=\small},
  >=Stealth
]
  \node[box] (b1) at (-2.1,0) {$?$};
  \node[box] (b2) at (0,0) {$?$};
  \node[box] (b3) at (2.1,0) {$?$};
  \node[above=0.0cm of b1] {box \(1\)};
  \node[above=0.0cm of b2] {box \(2\)};
  \node[above=0.0cm of b3] {box \(3\)};

  % \node[pair] (p12) at (-2.1,-1.55) {\(\{1,2\}\)};
  % \node[pair] (p23) at (0,-1.55) {\(\{2,3\}\)};
  % \node[pair] (p13) at (2.1,-1.55) {\(\{1,3\}\)};
  % \draw[->] (p12) -- (b1.south);
  % \draw[->] (p12) -- (b2.south west);
  % \draw[->] (p23) -- (b2.south);
  % \draw[->] (p23) -- (b3.south west);
  % \draw[->] (p13) -- (b1.south east);
  % \draw[->] (p13) -- (b3.south);

  \node[align=left,anchor=west] at (4.0,0.5) {open one pair\\exactly one jewel, the other empty};
  \node[align=left,anchor=west,text=gray!70!black] at (4.0,-0.3) {third box remains inaccessible};
\end{tikzpicture}
\]
A classical preassignment \(x_i\in\{0,1\}\), where \(x_i=1\) means that box \(i\) contains a jewel, would have to satisfy
\[
x_1+x_2
=
x_2+x_3
=
x_1+x_3
=
1
\]
Adding the three equations \rlap{gives}
\[
2(x_1+x_2+x_3)
=
3
\]
which is impossible.
The content of a box would therefore have to depend on which other box is opened: it would be \emph{contextual}.

This magic-box behaviour is not itself an ordinary quantum-mechanical phenomenon.
For sharp quantum yes/no measurements, pairwise joint measurability already implies joint measurability of all three questions.
Equivalently, 
if quantum mechanics lets us answer any two of three such questions jointly, 
then those answers can be embedded into a joint answer for all three.
The parable is therefore not the theorem itself, but a motivating foil for the genuinely quantum obstruction below.
% The parable is therefore not the theorem itself, but a smaller logical model of the same obstruction: each equation alone is satisfiable, but the equations together are not.
% The Kochen--Specker theorem is the qutrit version of this idea, using orthogonal triples of rays instead of pairs of boxes.



\subsubsection*{Noncontextual predictions}

As explained in \autoref{sec:observables}, a 
% projective
 measurement in an orthonormal basis
\[
B
=
\left\{\ket{v_1}, \allowbreak \ldots, \allowbreak\ket{v_d}\right\}
\]
has exactly one outcome.

Each ray in the basis also represents a yes/no property of the system.
The ray \(\ket v\) corresponds to the projector
\[
P_v
=
\ket v\bra v
\]
and hence to the question whether the measurement outcome lies in that ray.
In this sense, the proposition associated with \(P_v\) is:
``if we test for the ray \(v\), do we get yes?''
% A deterministic classical explanation would therefore preassign which ray in each basis will occur.
% Equivalently, it would assign a value
Quantum mechanics gives only the probabilities of these outcomes, but a hidden-variable explanation would say that the individual system carries an additional hidden state \(\lambda\).
Many systems may have the same quantum state \(\ket\psi\), while having different hidden states \(\lambda\).
The observed randomness would then be ignorance about \(\lambda\), and each hidden state would give definite answers.
% The Kochen--Specker question is whether such a single deterministic hidden answer sheet \(\lambda\) is even logically possible.
% Equivalently, for fixed \(\lambda\), one would assign a value
The Kochen--Specker question is whether every possible yes/no quantum property \(P_v\) can have such a pre-existing truth value.
Equivalently, it asks whether a single deterministic hidden answer sheet \(\lambda\) is even logically possible.
Such a sheet would have to be prepared before the measurement context is known: it must answer all possible basis choices at once.
For fixed \(\lambda\), one would assign a value
\[
c:\mathcal V\to\{0,1\} % mathcal V: set of rays/vectors that we are trying to color
\]
% to each relevant ray, where \(c(v)=1\) means that the outcome corresponding to \(\ket v\) occurs if a basis containing \(\ket v\) is measured.
to each relevant ray, where \(c(v)=1\) means that the outcome corresponding to \(\ket v\) is the answer selected by the hidden state \(\lambda\) if a basis containing \(\ket v\) is measured.

\pagebreak[2]

The assignment must satisfy:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item \label{item:kochen-specker-orthogonal}
if \(\braket{v|w}=0\), then
\(
c(v)+c(w)\leq 1
\),
because two orthogonal rays can occur in the same measurement basis and cannot both be the unique outcome
\item \label{item:kochen-specker-basis}
for every orthonormal basis \(B\subseteq\mathcal V\),
\(
\sum_{\ket v\in B}c(v)=1
\),
% because exactly one outcome occurs
because a complete basis measurement asks which one of the mutually exclusive alternatives occurs, and exactly one outcome occurs
\end{enumerate}
The value \(c(v)\) is required to depend only on the ray \(\ket v\), not on the choice of the other orthogonal rays used to complete it to a basis.
Indeed, if these yes/no properties were ordinary classical properties, then the truth value of \(P_v\) should not depend on which other compatible properties are measured alongside it.
This basis-independence is the \emph{noncontextuality} assumption.
 


For a Qbit 
\[
\ket\psi=\alpha\ket0+\beta\ket1
\]
such a coloring is possible: orthogonal pure states are antipodal points of the Bloch sphere, and one may select exactly one point of each antipodal pair.
For example, choose one half of the sphere, with an arbitrary boundary convention, and color every selected ray green and its antipodal ray red.
This does not mean that the quantum state \(\ket\psi\) determines the outcome with certainty.
It only means that there is no logical contradiction in imagining additional hidden variables \(\lambda\) that determine the outcome of each individual run.

For a Qtrit
\[
\ket\psi=\alpha\ket0+\beta\ket1+\gamma\ket2
\]
% already the real rays inside the Hilbert space would have to be colorable so that every orthonormal tripod contains exactly one selected ray.
even the real rays inside the Hilbert space cannot be colored so that every orthonormal tripod contains exactly one selected ray.
The direct analogue of the Qbit coloring would be a coloring of the real sphere in which every orthogonal triple contains exactly one green ray.
Kochen--Specker says that this apparently local requirement cannot be satisfied globally
\cite{kochenSpeckerHiddenVariables}:
% Thus the problem is not that the ray is itself the unknown state of the system; rather, the ray represents one possible yes/no measurement property, and the question is whether all such properties can be assigned classical truth values at once.
% \[
% \begin{tikzpicture}[
%   scale=0.82,
%   line cap=round,
%   line join=round,
%   every node/.style={font=\footnotesize},
%   basis/.style={draw,rounded corners,minimum height=0.82cm,align=center},
%   >=Stealth
% ]
%   % \coordinate (o) at (-4.25,0.2);
%   % \draw[thick] (o) circle (1.42);
%   % \draw[thick] ($(o)+(-1.42,0)$) arc[start angle=180,end angle=360,x radius=1.42,y radius=0.43];
%   % \draw[dashed,gray] ($(o)+(-1.42,0)$) arc[start angle=180,end angle=0,x radius=1.42,y radius=0.43];
%   % \draw[->,very thick,overviewgreen] (o) -- ++(0.20,1.23)
%   %   node[above] {\(\ket{v_1}\)};
%   % \draw[->,very thick,alizarincrimsonred] (o) -- ++(-1.06,-0.48)
%   %   node[below left] {\(\ket{v_2}\)};
%   % \draw[->,very thick,alizarincrimsonred] (o) -- ++(1.11,-0.39)
%   %   node[below right] {\(\ket{v_3}\)};
%   % \fill[overviewgreen] ($(o)+(0.20,1.23)$) circle (1.8pt);
%   % \fill[alizarincrimsonred] ($(o)+(-1.06,-0.48)$) circle (1.8pt);
%   % \fill[alizarincrimsonred] ($(o)+(1.11,-0.39)$) circle (1.8pt);
%   % \node at ($(o)+(0,-1.83)$) [align=center]
%   % {one selected ray\\per orthonormal tripod};

%   \node[basis,minimum width=2.20cm] (basis) at (-0.5,0.65)
%   {measurement basis\\\(B=\{\ket{v_1},\ket{v_2},\ket{v_3}\}\)};
%   \node[basis,minimum width=1.35cm] (system) at (3,0.65)
%   {Qtrit\\\(\ket\psi\)};
%   \node[basis,minimum width=1.85cm] (outcome) at (6,0.65)
%   {one outcome\\\(\ket{v_i}\in B\)};
%   \draw[->,thick] (basis) -- (system);
%   \draw[->,thick] (system) -- (outcome);
%   \node at (2.5,-0.25) [align=center,text=gray!70!black]
%   {the value of \(\ket v\) is fixed independently of the basis containing it};
% \end{tikzpicture}
% \]

\begin{theorem}[Kochen--Specker, 1967]\label{thm:kochen-specker}
There is a finite set \(\mathcal V\) of rays in \(\R^3\) that admits no noncontextual coloring satisfying the two conditions \ref{item:kochen-specker-orthogonal} and \ref{item:kochen-specker-basis} above.
\end{theorem}

Specker already announced the geometric obstruction in 1960, claiming that a finite set of rays on the sphere already suffices, but did not include the construction in that article
\cite{speckerLogikNichtGleichzeitigEntscheidbarerAussagen,rennerWolfSpeckerHiddenVariables}.
Kochen and Specker gave an explicit finite construction in 1967 using more than one hundred rays
\cite{kochenSpeckerHiddenVariables}.
% Thus a Qtrit cannot carry context-independent predetermined answers for all projective measurements.
Thus a Qtrit cannot carry context-independent predetermined answers for all projective measurements: 
one may choose an outcome separately for each basis, but not by assigning fixed values to the rays themselves.

\autoref{thm:kochen-specker} is the precise sense in which quantum mechanics is ``bad for seers/prophets'': 
even in principle, one cannot assign context-independent predetermined answers to all these yes/no questions.%
\footnote{This is similar in spirit to \autoref{claim:bell}: 
Alice and Bob cannot consistently prepare a table of answers for all possible measurement choices in advance. 
Bell rules out such tables under locality assumptions, 
while Kochen--Specker rules out context-independent value assignments already for a single system.}


\subsubsection*{A Kochen--Specker pseudo-telepathy game}

% Compared with the Deutsch--Jozsa game in \autoref{sec:deutsch-jozsa-game}, the Kochen--Specker construction needs only one maximally entangled pair of Qtrits.

% Let \(\mathcal B\) be a finite family of real orthonormal bases witnessing \autoref{thm:kochen-specker}.
Following Renner and Wolf's Kochen--Specker pseudo-telepathy formulation
\cite{rennerWolfPseudoTelepathy}, let \(\mathcal B\) be a finite family of real orthonormal bases whose union of rays is a Kochen--Specker set, i.e. it admits no noncontextual coloring as in \autoref{thm:kochen-specker}.
Alice receives \(B_{\mathrm{A}}\in\mathcal B\), Bob receives \(B_{\mathrm{B}} \in\mathcal B\), and each must output one vector from the received basis.
They win exactly when their vectors are not orthogonal:
% \(
% v_{\mathrm{A}}\in B_{\mathrm{A}}
% \),
% \(
% v_{\mathrm{B}}\in B_{\mathrm{B}} 
% \),
% \(
% \braket{v_{\mathrm{A}}|v_{\mathrm{B}}}\neq0
% \).
\[
\braket{v_{\mathrm{A}}|v_{\mathrm{B}}}\neq0
\]
where $v_{\mathrm{A}}\in B_{\mathrm{A}}$
and
$v_{\mathrm{B}}\in B_{\mathrm{B}}$ are the outputs of Alice and Bob, respectively.

A perfect deterministic classical strategy would select one vector from every basis while never selecting two orthogonal vectors.
% Coloring every selected vector by \(1\) and all others by \(0\) would give precisely the forbidden Kochen--Specker coloring.
If the same ray occurs in two bases, the strategy must select it in both or in neither, because any different vector in the same basis is orthogonal to it.
Thus coloring the selected rays by \(1\) and all other rays by \(0\) would give precisely the forbidden Kochen--Specker coloring.
Hence no perfect classical strategy exists.

For the quantum strategy, Alice and Bob share the maximally entangled pair of Qtrits
\[
\ket{\Psi}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt3}
\left(
\ket{00}+\ket{11}+\ket{22}
\right)
\]
If \(U_{\mathrm{A}}\) and \(U_{\mathrm{B}}\) are the unitaries mapping the standard basis to \(B_{\mathrm{A}}\) and \(B_{\mathrm{B}}\), each player measures in the received basis:
\[
\begin{quantikz}[column sep=0.62cm, row sep=0.22cm]
\lstick[wires=2]{$\ket{\Psi}$}
  & \gate{U_{\mathrm{A}}^*} & \meter{} \rstick{$v_{\mathrm{A}}\in B_{\mathrm{A}}$} \\
  & \gate{U_{\mathrm{B}}^*} & \meter{} \rstick{$v_{\mathrm{B}}\in B_{\mathrm{B}}$}
\end{quantikz}
\]
For real vectors
\(
\ket {v_{\mathrm{A}}},\ket {v_{\mathrm{B}}}\in\R^3
\),
the probability of the joint outcome \((v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}})\) is
\[
\begin{aligned}
\Prob(v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}})
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:projection-onto-eigenspace-born-rule}}}{=}
\left\|P_{\Span\{\ket{v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}}}\}}\ket{\Psi}\right\|^2
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:projector-eigenspace}}}{=}
\left\|\ket{v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}}}\bra{v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}}}\ket{\Psi}\right\|^2
=
\left\|\braket{v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}}|\Psi}\ket{v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}}}\right\|^2
\\
&=
\left|\braket{v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}}|\Psi}\right|^2
=
\left|\braket{\Psi|v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}}}\right|^2
\\
&\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:inner-product-tensor-product}}}{=}
\left|
\frac{1}{\sqrt3}
\left(
\braket{0|v_{\mathrm{A}}}\braket{0|v_{\mathrm{B}}}
+ 
\braket{1|v_{\mathrm{A}}}\braket{1|v_{\mathrm{B}}}
+ 
\braket{2|v_{\mathrm{A}}}\braket{2|v_{\mathrm{B}}}
\right)
\right|^2
\\
&=
\frac{1}{3}
\left|
(v_{\mathrm{A}})_0 (v_{\mathrm{B}})_0
+ 
(v_{\mathrm{A}})_1 (v_{\mathrm{B}})_1
+ 
(v_{\mathrm{A}})_2 (v_{\mathrm{B}})_2
\right|^2
\\
&=
\frac{1}{3}\left|\braket{v_{\mathrm{A}}|v_{\mathrm{B}}}\right|^2
\end{aligned}
\]
In particular, if
\(
\braket{v_{\mathrm{A}}|v_{\mathrm{B}}}
=
0
\) then \(
\Prob(v_{\mathrm{A}},v_{\mathrm{B}})
=
0
\),
so Alice and Bob never output orthogonal vectors and win with certainty.
When \(B_{\mathrm{A}} = B_{\mathrm{B}}\), all distinct vectors in the common basis are orthogonal, so both players necessarily obtain the same vector.
The entangled pair therefore behaves like two consistent measurements of the same Qtrit without communication.

The game uses local dimension \(3\times3\): Alice and Bob each hold one qtrit, so the shared state lives in \(\C^3\otimes\C^3\).
This is minimal for two-player pseudo-telepathy: local dimension \(2\times2\) cannot support a perfect quantum strategy without a perfect classical one \cite{rennerWolfPseudoTelepathy}.
% A pair of Qbits can violate Bell inequalities, but this is weaker than pseudo-telepathy: local dimension \(2\times2\) cannot support a perfect quantum strategy without a perfect classical one.
% Thus the maximally entangled Qtrit pair is the minimal two-player quantum resource for pseudo-telepathy.




\clearpage




\section{The Needle in the Haystack: Grover's algorithm}\label{sec:grover}

Grover's algorithm solves the unstructured search problem: given a Boolean function
\[
f:\{0,1\}^n\to\{0,1\}
\]
with a small number $M$ of marked inputs satisfying
\(
f(\vect{x})=1
\),
find such an input.
Classically this requires
\(
\Theta(N/M)
\)
queries on average, where
\(
N=2^n
\).
Grover reduces this to
\(
\Theta(\sqrt{N/M})
\)
queries,
which is a quadratic  
% rather than exponential 
speed-up.
% This is a query-complexity statement; the complexity-class viewpoint in \autoref{sec:quantum-complexity-theory} explains how such algorithmic speed-ups fit into classes such as \(\mathrm{BPP}\), \(\mathrm{BQP}\), and \(\mathrm{PSPACE}\).
% For example, exhaustive key search against a block cipher with a \(2n\)-bit key takes only about as many quantum queries as classical search against an \(n\)-bit key.
% The algorithm is nevertheless optimal in the black-box model: unstructured quantum search requires
% \(
% \Omega(\sqrt{N/M})
% \)
% oracle queries.

% \begin{remark}[Why the classical cost is linear]\label{rem:grover-classical-lower-bound}
% Suppose first that \(M=1\) and the marked input is chosen uniformly at random.
% Any deterministic algorithm making \(t\) distinct queries finds it with probability at most
% \[
% \frac{t}{N}
% \]
% Consequently a randomized classical algorithm needs
% \(
% \Omega(N)
% \)
% queries for constant success probability.
% For \(M\) marked inputs the same argument gives
% \(
% \Omega(N/M)
% \).
% \end{remark}

% The algorithm begins in the computational basis state
% \(
% \ket{\vect{0}}
% \)
% and applies Hadamards:
% \[
% \ket{\Psi}
% =
% H^{\otimes n}\ket{\vect{0}}
% =
% \frac{1}{\sqrt N}
% \sum_{\vect{x}\in\{0,1\}^n}
% \ket{\vect{x}}
% \]
% which is the equal superposition over all candidates.

Let $\ket{\Psi} = H^{\otimes n}\ket{\vect{0}}$ as always (see \autoref{ex:hadamard-n-qbits}, \autoref{eq:nfold-hadamard-zero}).
The two ingredients of \autoref{alg:grover} are:
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*)]
\item 
The \emph{oracle} $O_f$ that implements the function \(f\) in the usual way 
(%reversible gate plus phase kickback,% 
see \autoref{sec:phase-kickback}):
\[
O_f\ket{\vect{x}}
=
(-1)^{f(\vect{x})}\ket{\vect{x}}
\]
and flips the phase of marked states.

\item 
The \emph{diffusion operator} 
\[
D 
= 
H^{\otimes n}(2\ket{\vect{0}}\bra{\vect{0}}-\id)H^{\otimes n}
\]
% which is a reflection at the line $\Span(\ket{\Psi})$:
which reflects amplitudes about their average.
\end{enumerate}

The product
\[
G
=
DO_f
\]
is called the \emph{Grover iterate}.

Both operators really are reflections.
For any state
\(
\ket{\varphi}=a\ket{\alpha}+b\ket{\beta}
\)
in the plane introduced below,
\[
O_f\ket{\varphi}
=
a\ket{\alpha}-b\ket{\beta}
\]
so \(O_f\) reflects at the non-solution axis \(\Span(\ket{\alpha})\).
Moreover, 
if
$\ket{\phi_\parallel}$ denotes the part of $\ket{\phi}$ parallel to $\ket{\Psi}$ (i.e., the projection of $\ket{\phi}$ onto $\ket{\Psi}$) and $\ket{\phi_\perp}$ denotes the part of $\ket{\phi}$ perpendicular to $\ket{\Psi}$% 
%(i.e., the difference between $\ket{\phi}$ and its projection onto $\ket{\Psi}$)%
, so that
\(
% \ket{\eta}\perp\ket{\Psi}
\ket{\phi}=\ket{\phi_\parallel}+\ket{\phi_\perp}
\),
then
% \[
% \begin{aligned}
% D\ket{\Psi}
% &=
% \left(2\ket{\Psi}\bra{\Psi}-\id\right)\ket{\Psi}
% =
% \ket{\Psi}
% \\
% D\ket{\eta}
% &=
% \left(2\ket{\Psi}\bra{\Psi}-\id\right)\ket{\eta}
% =
% -\ket{\eta}
% \end{aligned}
% \]
% \[
% \begin{aligned}
% D \ket{\phi}
% &=
% \ket{\phi_\parallel} - \ket{\phi_\perp} 
% \\
% &=
% \ket{\phi_\parallel} - (\ket{\phi} - \ket{\phi_\parallel})
% \\
% &=
% 2\ket{\phi_\parallel} - \ket{\phi}
% \\
% &=
% 2\frac{\braket{\Psi|\phi}}{\braket{\Psi|\Psi}}\ket{\Psi} - \ket{\phi}
% \\
% &=
% 2\ket{\Psi}\bra{\Psi}\ket{\phi} - \ket{\phi}
% \\
% &=
% (2\ket{\Psi}\bra{\Psi} - \id)\ket{\phi}
% \\
% &=
% (2\ket{\Psi}\bra{\Psi}-\id)\ket{\phi}
% \\
% &=
% (2(H^{\otimes n}\ket{\vect{0}})(\bra{\vect{0}}H^{\otimes n})- H^{\otimes n}H^{\otimes n})\ket{\phi}
% \\
% &=
% (H^{\otimes n}(2\ket{\vect{0}}\bra{\vect{0}}-\id)H^{\otimes n})\ket{\phi}
% \end{aligned}
% \]
\[
\begin{aligned}
% D \ket{\phi}
\ket{\phi_{\text{reflected}}}
&=
\ket{\phi_\parallel} - \ket{\phi_\perp} 
\\
&=
\ket{\phi_\parallel} - (\ket{\phi} - \ket{\phi_\parallel})
\\
&=
2\ket{\phi_\parallel} - \ket{\phi}
\\
&=
2\frac{\braket{\Psi|\phi}}{\braket{\Psi|\Psi}}\ket{\Psi} - \ket{\phi}
\\
&=
2\ket{\Psi}\bra{\Psi}\ket{\phi} - \ket{\phi}
\\
&=
(2\ket{\Psi}\bra{\Psi} - \id)\ket{\phi}
\\
&=
(2(H^{\otimes n}\ket{\vect{0}})(\bra{\vect{0}}H^{\otimes n})- H^{\otimes n}H^{\otimes n})\ket{\phi}
\\
&=
\underbrace{H^{\otimes n}(2\ket{\vect{0}}\bra{\vect{0}}-\id)H^{\otimes n}}_{D}\ket{\phi}
\end{aligned}
\]
so \(D\) reflects at \(\Span(\ket{\Psi})\).

The name \emph{diffusion operator} comes from its action on amplitudes.
Writing
\(
\ket{\varphi}=\sum_{\vect{x}} a_{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{x}}
\)
and
\(
\bar a=\frac1N\sum_{\vect{x}}a_{\vect{x}}
\),
we obtain
\[
\begin{aligned}
D\ket{\varphi}
&=
\left(2\ket{\Psi}\bra{\Psi}-\id\right)\ket{\varphi}
\\
&=
\left(
2\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt N}\sum_{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{x}}\right)\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt N}\sum_{\vect{y}}\bra{\vect{y}}\right)-\id
\right)
\sum_{\vect{z}} a_{\vect{z}}\ket{\vect{z}}
\\
&=
2
\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt N}
\sum_{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{x}}
\right)
\left(
  \frac{1}{\sqrt N}\sum_{\vect{y}}
  \sum_{\vect{z}} a_{\vect{z}}\braket{\vect{y}|\vect{z}}
\right)
-
\sum_{\vect{z}} a_{\vect{z}}\ket{\vect{z}}
\\
&=
\left(
\sum_{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{x}}
\right)
\left(
  2\frac{1}{N}\sum_{\vect{y}} a_{\vect{y}}
\right)
-
\sum_{\vect{z}} a_{\vect{z}}\ket{\vect{z}}
\\
&=
\left(
\sum_{\vect{x}}\ket{\vect{x}}
\right)
2
\bar{a}
-
\sum_{\vect{z}} a_{\vect{z}}\ket{\vect{z}}
\\
% &=
% \left(
% \frac{2}{N}\sum_{\vect{x},\vect{y}}\ket{\vect{x}}\bra{\vect{y}}
% -
% \id
% \right)
% \sum_{\vect{z}} a_{\vect{z}}\ket{\vect{z}}
% \\
&=
\sum_{\vect{x}}
\left(
2\bar a-a_{\vect{x}}
\right)
\ket{\vect{x}}
\end{aligned}
\]
Thus every amplitude is reflected about the average amplitude.

\subsection{Geometric picture}

Let
\[
\ket{\alpha}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{N-M}}
\sum_{f(\vect{x})=0}\ket{\vect{x}}
\qquad
\ket{\beta}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt M}
\sum_{f(\vect{x})=1}\ket{\vect{x}}
\]
be the normalized superpositions of non-solutions and solutions.
Then
\[
\ket{\Psi}
=
\sqrt{\frac{N-M}{N}}\ket{\alpha}
+
\sqrt{\frac{M}{N}}\ket{\beta}
\]
so all dynamics take place in the two-dimensional plane
\(
\Span\{\ket{\alpha},\ket{\beta}\}
\).
Define
\begin{equation}\label{eq:grover-angle}
\sin\theta
=
\sqrt{\frac{M}{N}}
\qquad
\cos\theta
=
\sqrt{\frac{N-M}{N}}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}\label{eq:grover-initial-state-angle}
\ket{\Psi}
=
\cos\theta\ket{\alpha}
+
\sin\theta\ket{\beta}
\end{equation}

% \[
% \begin{tikzpicture}[
%   scale=0.95,
%   >=Stealth,
%   line cap=round,
%   line join=round,
%   every node/.style={font=\footnotesize}
% ]
%   \def\thetaplot{15}
%   \def\veclen{3.45}
%   \coordinate (o) at (0,0);

%   \draw[->,thick] (o) -- (4.4,0) node[right] {\(\ket{\alpha}\)};
%   \draw[->,thick] (o) -- (0,4.0) node[above] {\(\ket{\beta}\)};

%   \draw[->,very thick,funblue]
%     (o) -- ++(\thetaplot:\veclen)
%     node[pos=0.72,above] {\(\ket{\Psi}\)};

%   \draw[->,very thick,alizarincrimsonred]
%     (o) -- ++(-\thetaplot:\veclen)
%     node[right] {\(O_f\ket{\Psi}\)};

%   \draw[->,very thick,overviewgreen]
%     (o) -- ++({3*\thetaplot}:\veclen)
%     node[above] {\(G\ket{\Psi}\)};

%   \coordinate (oracle) at (-\thetaplot:\veclen);
%   \coordinate (psi) at (\thetaplot:\veclen);
%   \coordinate (grover) at ({3*\thetaplot}:\veclen);

%   \draw[densely dashed,alizarincrimsonred] (oracle) -- (grover);
%   \draw[densely dashed,overviewgreen] (grover) -- (psi);

%   \draw[->] (1.25,0) arc[start angle=0,end angle=\thetaplot,radius=1.25]
%     node[pos=0.55,right] {\(\theta\)};
%   \draw[->] (\thetaplot:1.68) arc[start angle=\thetaplot,end angle={3*\thetaplot},radius=1.68]
%     node[pos=0.58,right] {\(2\theta\)};

%   \node[align=left,anchor=west] at (4.85,2.15)
%   {\(O_f\): reflection at \(\ket{\alpha}\)\\
%    \(D\): reflection at \(\ket{\Psi}\)\\
%    \(G=DO_f\): rotation by \(2\theta\)};
% \end{tikzpicture}
% \]

\[
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  scale=0.95,
  >=Stealth,
  line cap=round,
  line join=round,
  every node/.style={font=\footnotesize}
]
  \def\thetaplot{15}
  \def\veclen{3.45}
  \coordinate (o) at (0,0);

  \draw[thin] (o) circle[radius=\veclen];

  \draw[] (-4.0,0) -- (4.0,0) node[right] {\(\ket{\alpha}\)-axis};
  \draw[] (0,-4.0) -- (0,4.0) node[above] {\(\ket{\beta}\)-axis};

  \draw[->,thick] (o) -- (0:\veclen); %node[pos=0.72,below] {\(\ket{\alpha}\)};
  \draw[->,thick] (o) -- (90:\veclen); %node[pos=0.72,left] {\(\ket{\beta}\)};

  \draw[->,very thick,black] (o) -- (\thetaplot:\veclen) node[pos=1.0,above right] {\(\ket{\Psi}\)};
  \draw[->,very thick,black] (o) -- (-\thetaplot:\veclen) node[below right] {\(O_f\ket{\Psi}\)};
  \draw[->,very thick,black] (o) -- ({3*\thetaplot}:\veclen) node[above right] {\(D O_f \ket{\Psi}\)};

  \coordinate (oracle) at (-\thetaplot:\veclen);
  \coordinate (psi) at (\thetaplot:\veclen);
  \coordinate (grover) at ({3*\thetaplot}:\veclen);

  \draw[densely dashed,black] (oracle) -- (grover);
  \draw[densely dashed,black] (grover) -- (psi);

  \draw[->] (1.68,0) arc[start angle=0,end angle=\thetaplot,radius=1.68] node[pos=0.58,right] {\(\theta\)};
  \draw[->] (\thetaplot:1.68) arc[start angle=\thetaplot,end angle={3*\thetaplot},radius=1.68] node[pos=0.58,right] {\(2\theta\)};

  % \node[align=left,anchor=west] at (4.85,2.15)
  % {\(O_f\): reflection at \(\ket{\alpha}\)\\
  %  \(D\): reflection at \(\ket{\Psi}\)\\
  %  \(G=DO_f\): rotation by \(2\theta\)};
\end{tikzpicture}
\]

The oracle reflects at
\(
\ket{\alpha}
\),
while the diffusion operator reflects at
\(
\ket{\Psi}
\).
Two reflections compose to a rotation, hence each Grover iteration rotates the state toward
\(
\ket{\beta}
\),
thereby amplifying the probability of observing a marked state.

After \(k\) iterations the exact state is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:grover-state-after-k}
G^k\ket{\Psi}
=
\cos\!\left((2k+1)\theta\right)\ket{\alpha}
+
\sin\!\left((2k+1)\theta\right)\ket{\beta}
\end{equation}
and hence the success probability is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:grover-success-probability}
\Prob[\text{marked after \(k\) iterations}]
=
\sin^2\!\left((2k+1)\theta\right)
\end{equation}
The optimal integer number of iterations is therefore
\begin{equation}\label{eq:grover-optimal-iterations}
k_{\mathrm{opt}}
=
\left\lfloor
\frac{\pi}{4\theta}
-
\frac12
\right\rfloor
\end{equation}
For \(M\ll N\), %\autoref{eq:grover-angle} gives
\[
\theta
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:grover-angle}}}{=}
\arcsin\sqrt{\frac{M}{N}}
\approx
\sqrt{\frac{M}{N}}
\]
and therefore
\[
k_{\mathrm{opt}}
\approx
\frac{\pi}{4}
\sqrt{\frac{N}{M}}
\]
After this many iterations a measurement returns a solution with high probability.  
% at least
% \(
% 1-O(M/N)
% \).
Continuing past this point rotates the state away from the solution axis again, which is known as the \emph{souffl\'e problem}:
If you do not take it out of the oven in time, it collapses again. 
% However, the situation is not all that bad, since it then increases again.




If \(M\) is unknown, choose the number \(T\) of iterations uniformly from
\(
\{0,\ldots,m-1\}
\).
% Using \autoref{eq:grover-success-probability},
% \[
% \Exp_T
% \left[
% \sin^2\!\left((2T+1)\theta\right)
% \right]
% =
% \frac1m
% \sum_{j=0}^{m-1}
% \sin^2\!\left((2j+1)\theta\right)
% =
% \frac12
% -
% \frac{\sin(4m\theta)}{4m\sin(2\theta)}
% \]
% implying that the success probability is at least \(1/4\) once
% \(
% m\geq1/\sin(2\theta)
% \).
Trying geometrically increasing values
\(
m%=1,2,4,\ldots
\)
retains the expected query complexity
\(
O(\sqrt{N/M})
\)
without knowing \(M\).



\subsection{Circuit}

\[
\begin{quantikz}[
  column sep=0.50cm,
  wire types={b},
  classical gap=0.07cm
]
\lstick{$\ket{\vect{0}}$}
  & \qwbundle{n}
  & \gate{H^{\otimes n}}
  & \gate{O_f}
    \gategroup[
      1,
      steps=4,
      style={dashed,rounded corners,inner xsep=3pt,inner ysep=5pt},
      label style={label position=below,anchor=north,yshift=-0.13cm}
    ]{repeated $k_{\mathrm{opt}}$ times}
  & \gate{H^{\otimes n}}
  & \gate{2\ket{\vect{0}}\bra{\vect{0}}-\id}
  & \gate{H^{\otimes n}}
  & \meter{}
  & \setwiretype{c}\rstick{$\vect{x}$}
\end{quantikz}
\]

The dashed block consists of
\(
O_f
\)
and the three gates implementing
\(
D=H^{\otimes n}(2\ket{\vect{0}}\bra{\vect{0}}-\id)H^{\otimes n}
\)
and it
is repeated
\(
k_{\mathrm{opt}}
\)
times.


% \subsubsection*{Amplitude amplification}

% Grover search is the special case of a more general amplification principle.
% Suppose a unitary \(A\) prepares
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:amplitude-amplification-preparation}
% A\ket{\vect{0}}
% =
% \sqrt p\ket{\mathrm{good}}
% +
% \sqrt{1-p}\ket{\mathrm{bad}}
% \end{equation}
% where the two states are orthogonal and an oracle \(O_{\mathrm{good}}\) flips precisely the good component.
% The reflection at \(A\ket{\vect{0}}\) is
% \[
% R_A
% =
% A
% \left(
% 2\ket{\vect{0}}\bra{\vect{0}}-\id
% \right)
% A^\dagger
% \]
% and
% \(
% R_AO_{\mathrm{good}}
% \)
% rotates the two-dimensional good--bad plane by
% \(
% 2\arcsin\sqrt p
% \).
% Consequently
% \(
% O(1/\sqrt p)
% \)
% uses of \(A\), \(A^\dagger\), and \(O_{\mathrm{good}}\) amplify success close to one, whereas independent classical repetition needs
% \(
% O(1/p)
% \)
% trials.

\tikzexternaldisable
\begin{algorithm}[h]
\caption{Grover algorithm}\label{alg:grover}
\begin{algorithmic}[1]
\Require Oracle $O_f\ket{\vect{x}}=(-1)^{f(\vect{x})}\ket{\vect{x}}$, number of solutions $M$, search space size $N=2^n$
\State $\ket{\psi}\gets\ket{\vect{0}}$
\State $\ket{\psi}\gets H^{\otimes n}\ket{\psi}$ \Comment{equal superposition over all candidates}
\State $\theta\gets\arcsin\sqrt{M/N}$
\State $k\gets \left\lfloor \frac{\pi}{4\theta}-\frac12\right\rfloor$
\For{$j=1,\ldots,k$}
  \State $\ket{\psi}\gets O_f\ket{\psi}$ \Comment{flip the phase of marked states}
  \State $\ket{\psi}\gets D\ket{\psi}$ \Comment{diffusion / reflection about the average}
\EndFor
\State Measure $\ket{\psi}$ in the computational basis
\State \Return measured bit string $\vect{x}$
\end{algorithmic}
\end{algorithm}
\tikzexternalenable

% The algorithm amplifies the amplitudes of marked states while suppressing the others. Unlike Shor's algorithm, the speedup is quadratic rather than exponential, but it applies to essentially any unstructured search problem.






\clearpage
\section{Shor's algorithm}\label{sec:shor}

\subsection{Quantum Fourier transform}\label{sec:quantum-fourier-transform}
Let $N = 2^n$.
The quantum Fourier transform (QFT) %, as presented for example in \cite{laneveQuantumAlgorithms},
maps
\[
\sum_{\vect{j}\in\{0,1\}^n} f_\vect{j}\ket{\vect{j}}
\quad
\xmapsto{\operatorname{QFT}}
\quad
\sum_{\vect{j}\in\{0,1\}^n} \hat{f}_\vect{j}\ket{\vect{j}}
\]
where
$\hat{\vect{f}} = [{\hat{f}}_0, \ldots, {\hat{f}}_{N-1}]$ is the discrete Fourier transform of $\vect{f} = [f_0, \ldots, f_{N-1}]$,
i.e.
\[
  \hat{\vect{f}} = \matr{F}_N \vect{f}
\]
where $\matr{F}_N$ is the $N\times N$ Fourier matrix with entries
\(
[\matr{F}_N]_{k l}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt N}\omega^{kl}
\)
where $\omega \coloneq \eu^{2\pi\iu/N}$ is the $N$-th root of unity.

Let
\(
\vect{x}=x_1\cdots x_n
\)
be an \(n\)-bit string and let
\(
x=[\vect{x}]_2
\)
denote the integer represented by that string.
The QFT acts on a computational basis state as
\begin{equation}\label{eq:qft-computational-basis}
\operatorname{QFT}\ket{\vect{x}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt N}
\sum_{y = 0}^{N-1}
\omega^{xy}
\ket{\vect{y}}
=
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\sum_{y=0}^{2^n-1}
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,xy/2^n}
\ket{\vect{y}}
\end{equation}
where \(\vect{y}\in\{0,1\}^n\) is the binary representation of \(y\).
% where we used
% \(
% \omega^{xy}
% =
% \eu^{2\pi\iu\,xy/2^n}
% \).

% The QFT is unitary because
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:qft-unitarity-computation}
% \bra{k}\operatorname{QFT}_N^\dagger\operatorname{QFT}_N\ket{\ell}
% =
% \frac1N
% \sum_{y=0}^{N-1}
% \omega^{y(\ell-k)}
% =
% \begin{cases}
% 1 & k=\ell\\
% 0 & k\neq\ell
% \end{cases}
% \end{equation}
% Indeed, for \(k\neq\ell\), the geometric sum vanishes:
% \[
% \sum_{y=0}^{N-1}q^y
% =
% \frac{q^N-1}{q-1}
% =
% 0
% \qquad
% q=\omega^{\ell-k}\neq1
% \]


% % This can be written as a product state
% The quantum Fourier transform (QFT) maps
% $\ket{\vect{j}} = \ket{j_1 \cdots j_n}$ to
% \[
% \frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
% \bigotimes_{k=n}^{1}
% \left(
% \ket{0}
% +
% \eu^{2\pi\iu\,\phi_k(j)}
% \ket{1}
% \right)
% \]
% where
% \[
% \phi_k(j)
% \coloneqq
% \sum_{\ell=k}^n \frac{j_\ell}{2^{\ell-k+1}}
% \]
% is the binary fraction corresponding to
% % the bits $j_k\cdots j_n$, also written as
% $0.j_k\cdots j_n$ in binary notation.


% Writing the output state in tensor-product form.
Writing
\[
% \vect{y}=y_1\cdots y_n
% \qquad
y=[\vect{y}]_2
=
\sum_{k=1}^n y_k 2^{n-k}
% \qquad
%  y_k\in\{0,1\},
\]
we obtain
\[
\begin{aligned}
\operatorname{QFT}\ket{\vect{x}}
&=
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\sum_{y=0}^{2^n-1}
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,xy/2^n}
\ket{\vect{y}}
\\
&=
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\sum_{y=0}^{2^n-1}
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,x\left(\sum_{k=1}^n y_k 2^{n-k}\right)/2^n}
\ket{\vect{y}}
\\
&=
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\sum_{y=0}^{2^n-1}
\prod_{k=1}^n
\left(
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,x/2^k}
\right)^{y_k}
\ket{\vect{y}}
\\
% &=
% \frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
% \sum_{y=0}^{2^n-1}
% \prod_{k=1}^n
% \left(
% \eu^{2\pi\iu\,x/2^k}
% \right)^{y_k}
% \bigotimes_{k=1}^n\ket{y_k}
% \\
&=
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\sum_{y=0}^{2^n-1}
\bigotimes_{k=1}^n
\left(
\left(
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,x/2^k}
\right)^{y_k}
\ket{y_k}
\right)
\\
&=
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\sum_{\vect{y} \in\{0,1\}^n}
\bigotimes_{k=1}^n
\left(
\left(
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,x/2^k}
\right)^{y_k}
\ket{y_k}
\right)
\\
&= % this step is not clear yet
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\bigotimes_{k=1}^n
\left(
\sum_{y_k\in\{0,1\}}
\left(
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,x/2^k}
\right)^{y_k}
\ket{y_k}
\right)
\\
&=
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\bigotimes_{k=1}^n
\left(
\ket0
+
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,x/2^k}\ket1
\right)
\end{aligned}
\]
% where we use the distributive law for tensor products:
% \[
% \sum_{\vect{y}\in\{0,1\}^n}
% \bigotimes_{k=1}^n a_k(y_k)
% =
% \bigotimes_{k=1}^n
% \left(
% \sum_{y_k\in\{0,1\}} a_k(y_k)
% \right),
% \]
% where the sum over \(\vect{y}\) means an independent sum over all bits
% \(y_1,\ldots,y_n\).
% In our case,
% \[
% a_k(y_k)
% =
% \left(
% \eu^{2\pi\iu x/2^k}
% \right)^{y_k}
% \ket{y_k}.
% \]
Since
\[
\frac{x}{2^k}
=
\text{integer}
+
0.x_{n-k+1}\cdots x_n
\]
in binary notation, the integer part contributes no phase because
\(
\eu^{2\pi\iu m}=1
\)
for every integer \(m\).
Hence
\[
\operatorname{QFT}\ket{\vect{x}}
=
\frac{1}{2^{n/2}}
\bigotimes_{k=1}^n
\left(
\ket0
+
\eu^{2\pi\iu\,0.x_{n-k+1}\cdots x_n}
\ket1
\right)
\]
This is the QFT output in the standard computational-basis order.
In circuit implementations, the tensor factors are often produced in the opposite wire order; 
one then adds final SWAP gates to reverse the order of the output bits.

\subsubsection*{Circuit}

Define the phase gates
\begin{equation}\label{eq:qft-phase-gates}
R_k
\coloneqq
\begin{bmatrix}
1&0\\
0&\eu^{2\pi\iu/2^k}
\end{bmatrix}
\end{equation}
A Hadamard on the $j$-th Qbit, followed by controlled phase gates from the less significant Qbits, prepares the factor with phase
\(
0.x_jx_{j+1}\cdots x_n
\).
Thus the circuit produces the tensor factors in the opposite order compared with the product representation above; the final SWAP gates restore the standard computational-basis order.
The general \(n\)-Qbit circuit is
\[
\resizebox{\textwidth}{!}{%
\begin{quantikz}[
  % font=\tiny,
  column sep=0.42cm,
  row sep=0.30cm,
  wire types={q,q,n,q,q}
]
% \lstick{$\ket{x_1}$}
  &
   \gate{H}
  & \gate{R_2}
  & \ \ldots \
  & \gate{R_{n-1}}
  & \gate{R_n}
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \swap{4}
  & \qw \\
% \lstick{$\ket{x_2}$}
  &
   \qw
  & \ctrl{-1}
  & \ \ldots \
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \gate{H}
  & \ \ldots \
  & \gate{R_{n-2}}
  & \gate{R_{n-1}}
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \swap{2}
  & \qw \\
  % \lstick{\makebox[\widthof{$\ket{x_{n}}$}][c]{$\vdots$}}
  &
   \vdots
  & 
  & \ddots
  & 
  & 
  &
  & \ddots
  & 
  & 
  & \ddots
  & 
  &
  & \vdots
  &
  & \\
% \lstick{$\ket{x_{n-1}}$}
  &
   \qw
  & \qw
  & \ \ldots \
  & \ctrl{-3}
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \ \ldots \
  & \ctrl{-2}
  & \qw
  & \ \ldots \
  & \gate{H}
  & \gate{R_2}
  & \qw
  & \targX{}
  & \qw \\
% \lstick{$\ket{x_n}$}
  &
   \qw
  & \qw
  & \ \ldots \
  & \qw
  & \ctrl{-4}
  & \qw
  & \ \ldots \
  & \qw
  & \ctrl{-3}
  & \ \ldots \
  & \qw
  & \ctrl{-1}
  & \gate{H}
  & \targX{}
  & \qw
\end{quantikz}
}
\]
% The final bit reversal is
% \[
% \operatorname{REV}_n
% =
% \underbrace{
% \prod_{j=1}^{\lfloor n/2\rfloor}
% \operatorname{SWAP}(j,n+1-j)
% }_{\lfloor n/2\rfloor\text{ swaps}}
% \]
There are
\(
n(n-1)/2
\)
controlled rotations, \(n\) Hadamards, and
\(
\lfloor n/2\rfloor
\)
swaps, hence the exact QFT uses
\(
O(n^2)
\)
gates \cite{wikipediaQFTGateCount}.
% Discarding rotations \(R_k\) with large \(k\), whose phases are close to \(1\), gives an approximate QFT using
% \(
% O(n\log n)
% \)
% gates with inverse-polynomial error.

\subsection{Phase estimation}\label{sec:phase-estimation}

Let \(U\) be unitary and suppose an eigenstate \(\ket u\) is available with
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-eigenvalue}
U\ket u
=
\eu^{2\pi\iu\varphi}\ket u
\qquad
\varphi\in[0,1)
\end{equation}
The goal is to determine the first \(t\) binary digits of \(\varphi\).
Controlled powers of \(U\) transfer the phase to a \(t\)-Qbit control register by phase kickback:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-kickback}
\ket j\ket u
\xmapsto{C_U}
\ket jU^j\ket u
=
\eu^{2\pi\iu j\varphi}\ket j\ket u
\end{equation}
Let \(n_u\) denote the number of Qbits in the register containing \(\ket u\).
In compact register-level notation, phase estimation is
\[
\begin{quantikz}[
  column sep=0.58cm,
  row sep=0.38cm,
  wire types={b,b},
  classical gap=0.07cm
]
\lstick{$\ket{\vect{0}}$}
  & \qwbundle{t}
  & \gate{H^{\otimes t}}
    \slice[
      style={densely dashed,gray,semithick},
      label style={pos=1,anchor=north,yshift=0.0cm}
    ]{$\ket{\psi_1}$}
  & &
    \ctrl{1}
    \gategroup[
      2,
      steps=1,
      style={ densely dotted,rounded corners,inner xsep=6pt,inner ysep=4pt},
      label style={label position=above,anchor=south,yshift=-0.2cm}
    ]{$C_U$}
    &
    \slice[
      style={densely dashed,gray,semithick},
      label style={pos=1,anchor=north,yshift=0.0cm}
    ]{$\ket{\psi_2}$}
  & \gate{\operatorname{QFT}_{2^t}^{-1}}
    \slice[
      style={densely dashed,gray,semithick},
      label style={pos=1,anchor=north,yshift=0.0cm}
    ]{$\ket{\psi_3}$}
  & \meter{}
  & \setwiretype{c}\rstick{$\vect{\widetilde{\ell}}$} \\
\lstick{$\ket u$}
  & \qwbundle{n_u}
  & \qw
  &
  & \gate{U^{2^{\kappa}}}
  &
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \rstick{$\ket u$}
\end{quantikz}
\]
% The corresponding expanded circuit is
% \[
% \begin{quantikz}[
%   column sep=0.38cm,
%   row sep=0.28cm,
%   wire types={q,q,n,q,q}
% ]
% \lstick{$\ket0$}
%   & \gate{H}
%   & \ctrl{4}
%     \gategroup[
%       5,
%       steps=4,
%       style={dashed,rounded corners,inner xsep=3pt,inner ysep=4pt},
%       label style={label position=below,anchor=north,yshift=-0.15cm}
%     ]{\(t\) controlled powers \(U^{2^j}\), \(0\leq j<t\)}
%   & \qw
%   & \ \ldots \
%   & \qw
%   & \gate[wires=4]{\operatorname{QFT}_{2^t}^{\dagger}}
%   & \meter{}
%   & \rstick[wires=4]{$\vect{\widetilde{\ell}}$} \\
% \lstick{$\ket0$}
%   & \gate{H}
%   & \qw
%   & \ctrl{3}
%   & \ \ldots \
%   & \qw
%   &
%   & \meter{}
%   & \\
% \vdots
%   &
%   & \vdots
%   & \vdots
%   & \ddots
%   & \vdots
%   &
%   & \vdots
%   & \\
% \lstick{$\ket0$}
%   & \gate{H}
%   & \qw
%   & \qw
%   & \ \ldots \
%   & \ctrl{1}
%   &
%   & \meter{}
%   & \\
% \lstick{$\ket u$}
%   & \qw
%   & \gate{U}
%   & \gate{U^2}
%   & \ \ldots \
%   & \gate{U^{2^{t-1}}}
%   & \qw
%   & \qw
%   & \rstick{$\ket u$}
% \end{quantikz}
% \]
The slices in the circuit mark the following joint states.
Initially,
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-state-zero}
\ket{\psi_0}
=
\ket{\vect{0}}\ket u
\end{equation}
After the Hadamards, the control register is the usual equal superposition:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-state-one}
\ket{\psi_1}
=
\left(
H^{\otimes t}\otimes\id
\right)
\ket{\psi_0}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2^t}}
\sum_{j=0}^{2^t-1}
\ket j\ket u
\end{equation}
% Here the bundled controlled operation $\operatorname{c}\text{-}U^{2^{\kappa}}$ represents the \(t\) gates
% \[
% \underbrace{
% \operatorname{c}\text{-}U,\,
% \operatorname{c}\text{-}U^2,\,
% \ldots,\,
% \operatorname{c}\text{-}U^{2^{t-1}}
% }_{t\text{ controlled powers }U^{2^{\kappa}},\ 0\leq \kappa<t}
% \]
The compact controlled block represents the unitary
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-controlled-block}
C_U
\coloneqq
\sum_{j=0}^{2^t-1}
\ket j\bra j
\otimes
U^j
\end{equation}
Indeed, the binary expansion
\(
j=\sum_{k=0}^{t-1}j_k2^k
\)
selects precisely the powers
\(
U^{2^k}
\)
whose control bits satisfy \(j_k=1\), and their product is
\[
\prod_{k=0}^{t-1}
\left(
U^{2^k}
\right)^{j_k}
=
U^{\sum_{k=0}^{t-1}j_k2^k}
=
U^j
\]
Applying this block to \(\ket{\psi_1}\) and using
\(
U^j\ket u=\eu^{2\pi\iu j\varphi}\ket u
\)
gives
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-state-two}
\ket{\psi_2}
=
C_U\ket{\psi_1}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2^t}}
\sum_{j=0}^{2^t-1}
\ket jU^j\ket u
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2^t}}
\sum_{j=0}^{2^t-1}
\eu^{2\pi\iu j\varphi}
\ket j\ket u
\end{equation}

Suppose first that the phase has an exact \(t\)-bit representation
\(
\varphi
=
\frac{\ell}{2^t}
\), \(
\ell\in\{0,\ldots,2^t-1\}
\) (ideal case).
Substitution into \autoref{eq:phase-estimation-state-two} gives
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-fourier-state}
\ket{\psi_2}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2^t}}
\sum_{j=0}^{2^t-1}
\eu^{2\pi\iu j\ell/2^t}
\ket j\ket u
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:qft-computational-basis}}}{=}
\left(
\operatorname{QFT}_{2^t}\ket\ell
\right)
\ket u
\end{equation}
where the second equality is exactly the definition of the QFT from
\autoref{sec:quantum-fourier-transform}.
Consequently, the inverse QFT \rlap{produces}
\begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-state-three}
\ket{\psi_3}
=
\left(
\operatorname{QFT}_{2^t}^{-1}\otimes\id
\right)
\ket{\psi_2}
=
\ket\ell\ket u
\end{equation}
and measurement returns \(\ell\) with certainty.
% In general, the measured string
% \(
% \vect{\widetilde{\ell}}\in\{0,1\}^t
% \)
% encodes the phase estimate
% \(
% [\vect{\widetilde{\ell}}]_2/2^t
% \).


% For a general phase, write
% \(
% 2^t\varphi=\ell+\delta
% \)
% with
% \(
% |\delta|\leq1/2
% \).
% The amplitude of measuring the nearest integer \(\ell\) is
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-approximate-amplitude}
% \braket{\ell|\psi}
% =
% \frac1{2^t}
% \sum_{j=0}^{2^t-1}
% \eu^{2\pi\iu j\delta/2^t}
% =
% \frac1{2^t}
% \frac{\eu^{2\pi\iu\delta}-1}
% {\eu^{2\pi\iu\delta/2^t}-1}
% \end{equation}
% and hence
% \begin{equation}\label{eq:phase-estimation-approximate-probability}
% \Prob[\ell]
% =
% \frac{1}{2^{2t}}
% \frac{\sin^2(\pi\delta)}
% {\sin^2(\pi\delta/2^t)}
% \geq
% \frac{4}{\pi^2}
% >
% 0.4
% \end{equation}
In the non-ideal case, we can extend the circuit:
Adding \(s\) extra Qbits and discarding their measured bits gives the desired \(t\)-bit approximation with probability at least
\(
1-2^{-s}
\).









\subsection{Number Theory}

\newlength{\propw}
\newlength{\propsep}
\settowidth{\propw}{{Identity element:}}
\setlength{\propsep}{\propw + 2em}

\newlength{\quantw}
\settowidth{\quantw}{$\forall a\in G\ \exists a^{-1}\in G$}
\newlength{\quantsep}
\setlength{\quantsep}{\quantw + 1.5em}

\newcommand{\propitem}[3]{%
  \item
  \makebox[\propsep][l]{#1}%
  \makebox[\quantsep][l]{$#2$}%
  $#3$%
}
\begin{definition}[Group]
A \emph{group} is a set $G$ together with a binary operation
\[
\circ : G \times G \to G
\]
such that:
\begin{enumerate}
  \propitem{Associativity:}
    {\forall a,b,c\in G}
    {(a\circ b)\circ c = a\circ(b\circ c)}

  \propitem{Identity element:}
    {\exists e\in G\ \forall a\in G}
    {e\circ a = a\circ e = a}

  \propitem{Inverse element:}
    {\forall a\in G\ \exists a^{-1}\in G}
    {a\circ a^{-1} = a^{-1}\circ a = e}
\qedhere
\end{enumerate}
\end{definition}
\begin{remark}
Uniqueness of the identity and uniqueness of inverse elements are not part of the axioms;
they are consequences of the three axioms.
\end{remark}




\begin{definition}[Cyclic group]
A group $G$ is called \emph{cyclic} if there exists an element $g\in G$ such that every element of $G$ can be written as a power of $g$, i.e.
\[
G = \{g^n : n\in\Z\}
\]
The element $g$ is called a \emph{generator} of $G$, and we write
\(
G = \langle g\rangle
\).
\end{definition}







\begin{definition}[Residue classes and units]\label{def:residue-classes-units}
The integers modulo \(N\) form
\[
\Z_N
\coloneqq
\{0,\ldots,N-1\}
\]
with addition and multiplication modulo \(N\).
Their multiplicative group of units is
\[
\Z_N^*
\coloneqq
\left\{
x\in\Z_N:\gcd(x,N)=1
\right\}
\]
which contains the numbers coprime to \(N\).
% The inverse of
% \(
% x\in\Z_N^*
% \)
% is computed efficiently from B\'ezout's identity
% \[
% sx+tN
% =
% \gcd(x,N)
% =
% 1
% \]
% because reduction modulo \(N\) gives
% \(
% sx\equiv1\pmod N
% \) and hence
% \(
% x^{-1}\equiv s\pmod N
% \).
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Order]\label{def:multiplicative-order}
The order of an element \(a\) in a finite group is
\[
\operatorname{ord}(a)
\coloneqq
\min
\left\{
r>0:a^r=e
\right\}
\]
For
\(
a\in\Z_N^*
\),
this means
\(
\operatorname{ord}_N(a)
\coloneqq
\min
\left\{
r>0:a^r\equiv1\pmod N
\right\}
\).
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}
If
\(
N=pq
\)
for distinct primes, then
\(
\Z_N^*
\)
consists of the residue classes modulo \(N\) that are not divisible by \(p\) or by \(q\):
\[
\Z_N^*
=
\{x\in\{0,\ldots,N-1\}:p\nmid x\text{ and }q\nmid x\}
\]
% Thus, it is not enough to remove only the two numbers \(p\) and \(q\);
Thus, to obtain $\Z_N^*$ from $\Z_N$, one has to remove all multiples of \(p\) and all multiples of \(q\).
There are \(q\) multiples of \(p\) modulo \(N\)
% ,namely
% \(
% 0,p,2p,\ldots,(q-1)p
% \),
and \(p\) multiples of \(q\).
% namely
% \(
% 0,q,2q,\ldots,(p-1)q
% \).
The residue \(0\) is counted twice, so the number of non-units is
\(
p+q-1
\).
Therefore
\[
|\Z_N^*|
=
pq-(p+q-1)
=
(p-1)(q-1)
\qedhere
\]
% The Chinese remainder theorem identifies
% \(
% \Z_N^*
% \)
% with
% \(
% \Z_p^*\times\Z_q^*
% \),
% so computations modulo \(N\) can be understood componentwise modulo its prime factors.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Order finding}\label{sec:order-finding}

The following order-finding routine is the quantum subroutine behind Shor's factoring algorithm \cite{shorAlgorithms,laneveQuantumAlgorithms}.

For
\(
a\in\Z_N^*
\),
define the modular multiplication unitary
\begin{equation}\label{eq:modular-multiplication-unitary}
{\color{red}\boxed{\color{black}
U_a\ket y
=
\ket{ay\bmod N}
}}
\end{equation}
on basis states \(0\leq y<N\), extending it as the identity on unused computational-basis states.
It is unitary because \(a\in\Z_N^*\), so multiplication by \(a\) is a permutation of \(\Z_N\).

A controlled $U^{2^\kappa}$ can be implemented efficiently by translating the classical ``repeated squaring'' method in time \(O(\log N)^3 = O(n^3)\), which is the asymptotic running time of \autoref{alg:shor-factoring}.
(This can be improved to essentially \(O(\log N)^2\) by using an asymptotically faster multiplication algorithm --- based also on the discrete Fourier transform.)



If
\(
r=\operatorname{ord}_N(a)
\),
then for
\(
0\leq s<r
\),
% define
\begin{equation}\label{eq:order-finding-eigenstates}
\ket{u_s}
\coloneqq
\frac1{\sqrt r}
\sum_{k=0}^{r-1}
\eu^{-2\pi\iu sk/r}
\ket{a^k\bmod N}
\end{equation}
% The eigenvalue computation is
is an eigenstate of \(U_a\) with eigenvalue \(\eu^{2\pi\iu s/r}\), because
\[
\begin{aligned}
U_a\ket{u_s}
&=
\frac1{\sqrt r}
\sum_{k=0}^{r-1}
\eu^{-2\pi\iu sk/r}
\ket{a^{k+1}\bmod N}
\\
&=
\eu^{2\pi\iu s/r}
\frac1{\sqrt r}
\sum_{k=0}^{r-1}
\eu^{-2\pi\iu sk/r}
\ket{a^k\bmod N}
\\
&=
\eu^{2\pi\iu s/r}\ket{u_s}
\end{aligned}
\]
where we used the $r$-periodicity of both functions. 
We conclude that phase estimation yields digits of the phase
$
\varphi=\frac{s}{r}
$, and
given that we know sufficiently many, i.e., $O(\log N)$, digits, we can determine the period and, hence, the rational number, in particular $r$, which is the unknown.

But how do we obtain one of the $\ket{u_s}$? 
We cannot: 
The definition of $\ket{u_s}$ depends on $r$. 
But luckily, 
% we do not have to;
% As in \autoref{eq:period-shift-initial-superposition},
% Consider the equal superposition of all eigenstates,
\[
\begin{aligned}
\frac1{\sqrt r}
\sum_{s=0}^{r-1}
\ket{u_s}
&=
\frac{1}{r}
\sum_{s=0}^{r-1}
\sum_{k=0}^{r-1}
\eu^{-\frac{2\pi\iu sk}{r}}
\ket{a^k\bmod N}
\\
&=
\frac{1}{r}
\sum_{k=0}^{r-1}
% \underbrace{
% \underbrace{
\left(
\sum_{s=0}^{r-1}
\eu^{-\frac{2\pi\iu sk}{r}}
\right)
% }_{r \ \delta_{k,0}}
% }_{\begin{cases}r&\text{if }k=0\\0&\text{otherwise}\end{cases}}
\ket{a^k\bmod N}
\\
&=
\frac{1}{r}
\sum_{k=0}^{r-1}
r \ \delta_{k,0}
\ket{a^k\bmod N}
\\
&=
\ket{1}
\end{aligned}
\]
so an eigenstate does not need to be prepared explicitly.
Let
\(
n=\lceil\log_2N\rceil
\).
The order-finding circuit is phase estimation for \(U_a\), written at register level with the same controlled-unitary convention as in \autoref{sec:phase-estimation}:
\[
\begin{quantikz}[
  column sep=0.58cm,
  row sep=0.38cm,
  wire types={b,b},
  classical gap=0.07cm
]
\lstick{$\ket{\vect{0}}$}
  & \qwbundle{t}
  & \gate{H^{\otimes t}}
  & &
    \ctrl{1}
    \gategroup[
      2,
      steps=1,
      style={densely dotted,rounded corners,inner xsep=6pt,inner ysep=4pt},
      label style={label position=above,anchor=south,yshift=-0.2cm}
    ]{$C_{U_a}$}
  &
  & \gate{\operatorname{QFT}_{2^t}^{-1}}
  & \meter{}
  & \setwiretype{c}\rstick{$\vect{z}$} \\
\lstick{$\ket{{1}}$}
  & \qwbundle{n}
  & \qw
  &
  & \gate{U_a^{2^{\kappa}}}
  &
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \rstick{$\ket{u_s}$}
\end{quantikz}
\]

% Conditioned on the measured value, the second register is projected onto the corresponding eigenstate \(\ket{u_s}\).

Here the dotted block denotes the controlled operation
\[
C_{U_a}
=
\sum_{j=0}^{2^t-1}
\ket j\bra j
\otimes
U_a^j
\]
from \eqref{eq:phase-estimation-controlled-block}.
In the expanded circuit, this is implemented by the controlled powers
\(
U_a^{2^\kappa}
\)
for \(0\leq\kappa<t\).
Equivalently, on basis states,
\[
C_{U_a}\ket j\ket y
=
\ket j\ket{a^jy\bmod N}
\]
which is the usual coherent modular exponentiation.
\pagebreak[3]

The measured bit string
\(
\vect{z}
\)
encodes an approximation
\(
[\vect{z}]_2/2^t\approx s/r
\).
Repeated squaring makes every controlled power \rlap{efficient:}
\[
U_a^{2^\kappa}\ket y
=
\ket{a^{2^\kappa}y\bmod N}
\]
and the constants
\(
a^{2^\kappa}\bmod N
\)
are precomputed classically.
Phase estimation (\autoref{sec:phase-estimation}) returns
\(
s/r
\)
approximately, and continued fractions recover \(r\).

\subsection{Integer Factoring}\label{sec:shor-factoring}

Shor's factoring algorithm combines the quantum order-finding subroutine from \autoref{sec:order-finding} with a classical reduction \cite{shorAlgorithms,laneveQuantumAlgorithms}.
% In complexity-class language, 
This puts factoring in the complexity class \(\mathrm{BQP}\), but as we will see in \autoref{sec:quantum-complexity-theory}, this by itself does not prove \(\mathrm{P}\subsetneq\mathrm{BQP}\).
Let \(N\) be an odd composite integer that is not a prime power and choose
\(
a\in\{2,\ldots,N-1\}
\)
uniformly.
If
\(
\gcd(a,N)>1
\),
a factor has already been found.
Otherwise compute
\(
r=\operatorname{ord}_N(a)
\).

If \(r\) is even, then
\begin{equation}\label{eq:shor-difference-of-squares}
\begin{aligned}
a^r
&\equiv
1
\pmod N
\\
a^r-1
&\equiv
0
\pmod N
\\
(a^{r/2}-1)
(a^{r/2}+1)
&\equiv
0
\pmod N
\end{aligned}
\end{equation}
Minimality of \(r\) guarantees
\(
a^{r/2}\not\equiv1\pmod N
\).
If also
\(
a^{r/2}\not\equiv-1\pmod N
\),
then neither factor in \autoref{eq:shor-difference-of-squares} is divisible by all of \(N\), while their product is.
Therefore
\begin{equation}\label{eq:shor-factor-gcds}
\gcd(a^{r/2}-1,N)
\qquad
\gcd(a^{r/2}+1,N)
\end{equation}
yield non-trivial factors.
For an admissible \(N\), a random
\(
a\in\Z_N^*
\)
has even order and avoids
\(
a^{r/2}\equiv-1\pmod N
\)
with probability at least \(1/2\).

% \begin{example}[Factoring \(15\)]\label{ex:shor-factor-15}
% Choose
% \(
% a=2
% \).
% Then
% \[
% 2^1
% \equiv
% 2
% \qquad
% 2^2
% \equiv
% 4
% \qquad
% 2^3
% \equiv
% 8
% \qquad
% 2^4
% \equiv
% 1
% \pmod{15}
% \]
% so
% \(
% r=4
% \).
% The classical post-processing gives
% \[
% \gcd(2^{4/2}-1,15)
% =
% \gcd(3,15)
% =
% 3
% \]
% and
% \[
% \gcd(2^{4/2}+1,15)
% =
% \gcd(5,15)
% =
% 5
% \]
% \end{example}

\tikzexternaldisable
\begin{algorithm}[ht]
\caption{Shor's factoring algorithm}\label{alg:shor-factoring}
\begin{algorithmic}[1]
\Require Composite integer $N$
\If{$N$ is even}
  \State \Return $2$ %\Comment{The even case is trivial}
\EndIf
\State Check classically whether $N$ is a prime power %\Comment{This case is excluded in \autoref{sec:shor-factoring}}
\State Choose $a$ uniformly from $\{2,\ldots,N-1\}$ \Comment{Random base for the reduction to order finding}
\State $d\gets\gcd(a,N)$ \Comment{Check whether $a$ is already not coprime to $N$}
\If{$d>1$} \Comment{$a\notin\Z_N^*$}
  \State \Return $d$ \Comment{The gcd already gives a non-trivial factor}
\EndIf
% \If{$d>1$} \Comment{$a$ is coprime to $N$ iff $d=1$}
%   \State \Return $d$ \Comment{Non-trivial factor found (i.e. $a$ is not coprime to $N$)}
% \EndIf
\State $r\gets\operatorname{ord}_N(a)$ using quantum order finding \Comment{See \autoref{def:multiplicative-order} and \autoref{sec:order-finding}}
\If{$r$ is odd or $a^{r/2}\equiv-1\pmod N$} \Comment{Then \eqref{eq:shor-difference-of-squares} does not yield useful gcds}
  \State Restart \Comment{For a random $a\in\Z_N^*$, success has constant probability}
\EndIf
\State \Return $\gcd(a^{r/2}-1,N)$ \Comment{A non-trivial factor from \eqref{eq:shor-factor-gcds}}
\end{algorithmic}
\end{algorithm}
\tikzexternalenable


% For an \(n\)-bit integer, order finding uses polynomially many gates in \(n=\lceil\log_2N\rceil\).
% The asymptotically dominant quantum task is coherent modular exponentiation.
% Using elementary reversible arithmetic gives a gate count of order
% \(
% O(n^3)
% \),
% already exponentially faster than the best known classical factoring algorithms.


\FloatBarrier


\subsection{Discrete Logarithms}\label{sec:shor-discrete-logarithm}

Shor's algorithm also solves the discrete logarithm problem \cite{shorAlgorithms,laneveQuantumAlgorithms}.
The factoring algorithm above is slightly indirect: the quantum part solves order finding, and the classical part reduces factoring to order finding.
Discrete logarithms are cleaner, because the relevant group order is part of the input, so the phase estimation can be made exact with a \(\operatorname{QFT}_q\).
This is also why the same idea applies not only to
\(
\Z_p^*
\),
but to any efficiently represented cyclic group, including elliptic-curve groups.

\begin{problem}[Discrete logarithm]\label{problem:discrete-logarithm}
Let
\[
G
=
\langle g\rangle
=
\left\{
1,g,g^2,\ldots,g^{q-1}
\right\}
\]
be a finite cyclic group of known order \(q\).
Given
\(
h=g^\ell
\),
determine
\(
\ell\in\Z_q
\).
\end{problem}

Assume that group multiplication and inversion are efficiently computable (i.e. in polynomial time in \(\log q\)).
Define the shift unitaries
\(
U\ket x
=
\ket{gx}
\),
\(
V\ket x
=
\ket{hx}
\)
on group-element basis states.
They are unitary because multiplication by a fixed group element is a permutation of \(G\).
Since
\(
h=g^\ell
\),
we have
\(
V
=
U^\ell
\).
Thus \(U\) and \(V\) have the same eigenvectors, and their eigenvalues differ by the unknown factor \(\ell\).

For \(s\in\Z_q\) (\autoref{def:residue-classes-units}), define
\[
\ket{u_s}
\coloneqq
\frac1{\sqrt q}
\sum_{k=0}^{q-1}
\eu^{-2\pi\iu sk/q}
\ket{g^k}
\]
Then
\[
\begin{aligned}
U\ket{u_s}
&=
\frac1{\sqrt q}
\sum_{k=0}^{q-1}
\eu^{-2\pi\iu sk/q}
\ket{g^{k+1}}
\\
&=
\eu^{2\pi\iu s/q}
\frac1{\sqrt q}
\sum_{k=0}^{q-1}
\eu^{-2\pi\iu sk/q}
\ket{g^k}
\\
&=
\eu^{2\pi\iu s/q}\ket{u_s}
\end{aligned}
\]
and therefore
\[
V\ket{u_s}
=
U^\ell\ket{u_s}
=
\eu^{2\pi\iu s\ell/q}\ket{u_s}
\]
As in \autoref{sec:order-finding}, the eigenstates need not be prepared directly.
Orthogonality of roots of unity gives
\(
% \begin{aligned}
\frac1{\sqrt q}
\sum_{s=0}^{q-1}
\ket{u_s}
% &=
% \frac1q
% \sum_{s=0}^{q-1}
% \sum_{k=0}^{q-1}
% \eu^{-2\pi\iu sk/q}
% \ket{g^k}
% \\
% &=
% \frac1q
% \sum_{k=0}^{q-1}
% q\delta_{k,0}
% \ket{g^k}
% \\
% &=
=
\ket1
% \end{aligned}
\).

For an eigenstate \(\ket{u_s}\), exact phase estimation (\autoref{sec:phase-estimation}) with \(\operatorname{QFT}_q\) gives
\[
\ket0\ket0\ket{u_s}
\xmapsto{\mathrm{PE}(U),\,\mathrm{PE}(V)}
\ket s\ket{s\ell\bmod q}\ket{u_s}
\]
Starting the lower register in \(\ket1\) therefore gives a uniformly random \(s\) (one out of \(q = |\Z_q|\) possible values):
\[
\ket0\ket0\ket1
\mapsto
\frac1{\sqrt q}
\sum_{s=0}^{q-1}
\ket s
\ket{s\ell\bmod q}
\ket{u_s}
\]
A register-level view of the two phase estimations is:
\tikzexternaldisable
\[
\begin{quantikz}[
  column sep=0.58cm,
  row sep=0.34cm,
  wire types={b,b,b},
  classical gap=0.07cm
]
\lstick{$\displaystyle\frac1{\sqrt q}\sum_{j=0}^{q-1}\ket j$}
  & \qwbundle{m}
  & 
  & \ctrl{2}
    \gategroup[
      3,
      steps=2,
      style={densely dotted,rounded corners,inner xsep=6pt,inner ysep=4pt},
      label style={label position=above,anchor=south,yshift=-0.2cm}
    ]{\(C_{\mathrm{dlog}}\)}
  & \qw
  &
  & \gate{\operatorname{QFT}_q^{-1}}
  & \meter{}
  & \setwiretype{c}\rstick{$s$} \\
\lstick{$\displaystyle\frac1{\sqrt q}\sum_{j=0}^{q-1}\ket j$}
  & \qwbundle{m}
  &
  & 
  & \ctrl{1}
  &
  & \gate{\operatorname{QFT}_q^{-1}}
  & \meter{}
  & \setwiretype{c}\rstick{$s\ell\bmod q$} \\
\lstick{$\ket1$}
  & \qwbundle{n_G}
  & 
  & \gate{U^j}
  & \gate{V^j}
  &
  & \qw
  & \qw
  & \rstick{$\ket{u_s}$}
\end{quantikz}
\]
\tikzexternalenable
where
\[
C_{\mathrm{dlog}}\ket{j_1} \ket{j_2} \ket z
=
\ket{j_1} \ket{j_2} \ket{g^{j_1} h^{j_2} z}
\]
and \(m=\lceil\log_2 q\rceil\) only indicates the number of Qbits needed to encode \(q\) basis states.
If \(q\) is not a power of two, one can either implement the exact \(\operatorname{QFT}_q\) on the \(q\)-dimensional subspace or use the usual high-precision power-of-two phase estimation as in \autoref{sec:phase-estimation}.
% Here one should not confuse the number \(m=\lceil\log_2 q\rceil\) of Qbits with the dimension \(q\) of the Fourier transform.
% If \(q\) is not a power of two, then an \(m\)-Qbit register has \(2^m\) computational basis states, not \(q\).
% Thus the state
% \[
% \frac1{\sqrt q}\sum_{j=0}^{q-1}\ket j
% \]
% is not obtained simply by applying \(H^{\otimes m}\), since
% \[
% H^{\otimes m}\ket0^{\otimes m}
% =
% \frac1{\sqrt{2^m}}
% \sum_{j=0}^{2^m-1}\ket j 
% \]
% One must either implement the exact \(\operatorname{QFT}_q\) on the \(q\)-dimensional subspace, 
% or first create a power-of-two superposition and then prepare the uniform superposition over the valid labels \(0,\ldots,q-1\) by an additional step, 
% for example by comparing with \(q\), measuring an ancilla, and postselecting on the outcome indicating \(j<q\). 
% Equivalently, one can avoid this exact preparation issue by using the usual high-precision power-of-two phase estimation from \autoref{sec:phase-estimation}.

After measurement we get
\[
(x,y)
=
(s,s\ell\bmod q)
\in
\Z_q^2
\]
If $s \in \Z_q^*$, i.e. \(s\) is invertible\footnote{%
If \(\gcd(s,q)=1\), then cancellation by \(s\) is valid modulo \(q\):
\(
sa\equiv sb\pmod q
\implies
a\equiv b\pmod q
\).
Hence multiplication by \(s\) modulo \(q\) is injective, and therefore, on the finite set \(\Z_q\), a permutation.%
} modulo \(q\) i.e. $s$ is coprime to $q$ i.e. $\gcd(s,q)=1$, the extended Eu\-cli\-dean algorithm gives \(s^{-1}\), and
\[
\ell
\equiv
ys^{-1}
\pmod q
\]
If \(s\notin\Z_q^*\), 
then \(s\) is not invertible modulo \(q\), 
so the congruence
\(
y\equiv s\ell \pmod q
\)
cannot be divided by \(s\), because it does not determine $\ell$ uniquely in that case. 
In fact, if \(d=\gcd(s,q)>1\), 
then this congruence determines \(\ell\) only modulo \(q/d\), 
and hence leaves \(d\) possible residue classes modulo \(q\). 
Thus this measurement gives only partial information about \(\ell\), 
not the unique discrete logarithm.
We therefore discard this run and repeat until \(s\in\Z_q^*\).
% repeat.
The success probability of one run is
\[
\Prob(s \perp q)
=
\Prob(s\in\Z_q^*)
=
\frac{|\Z_q^*|}{q}
% =
% \frac{\psi(q)}{q}
% =
% \Omega\!\left(\frac1{\log\log q}\right)
\]
where the numerator is the so-called  Euler totient function, which counts the number of integers coprime to \(q\).
It is known to grow with $\Omega(q/\log\log q)$, implying that the success probability is $\Omega(1/\log\log q)$ \cite{wikipediaTotientGrowth}.

Thus the expected number of repetitions is \(O(\log\log q)\).

% Hyperparameter for the plot below: change this to select how many integers are shown on the x-axis.
% The values of \(\varphi(q)\) are computed by a sieve in TeX and written to a generated data file.
\def\TotientPlotN{10000}
\ExplSyntaxOn
\intarray_new:Nn \g_qc_totient_phi_intarray { \TotientPlotN }
\iow_new:N \g_qc_totient_data_iow
\cs_new_protected:Npn \qc_totient_write_data:n #1
  {
    \int_step_inline:nn { #1 }
      { \intarray_gset:Nnn \g_qc_totient_phi_intarray { ##1 } { ##1 } }
    \int_step_inline:nnn { 2 } { #1 }
      {
        \int_compare:nNnT { \intarray_item:Nn \g_qc_totient_phi_intarray { ##1 } } = { ##1 }
          {
            \int_step_inline:nnnn { ##1 } { ##1 } { #1 }
              {
                \intarray_gset:Nnn \g_qc_totient_phi_intarray { ####1 }
                  {
                    \int_eval:n
                      {
                        \intarray_item:Nn \g_qc_totient_phi_intarray { ####1 }
                        - \int_div_truncate:nn
                            { \intarray_item:Nn \g_qc_totient_phi_intarray { ####1 } }
                            { ##1 }
                      }
                  }
              }
          }
      }
    \iow_open:Nn \g_qc_totient_data_iow { totient-values-sieve.dat }
    \iow_now:Nn \g_qc_totient_data_iow { q ~ phi }
    \int_step_inline:nn { #1 }
      {
        \iow_now:Nx \g_qc_totient_data_iow
          { ##1 ~ \intarray_item:Nn \g_qc_totient_phi_intarray { ##1 } }
      }
    \iow_close:N \g_qc_totient_data_iow
  }
\qc_totient_write_data:n { \TotientPlotN }
\ExplSyntaxOff
\tikzsetnextfilename{totient-plot-liminf-sieve}
\[
\begin{tikzpicture}[font=\footnotesize]
  \begin{axis}[
    scale only axis,
    width=7.5cm,
    height=7.5cm,

    xmin=0,

    ymin=0,
    ymax=\TotientPlotN,
    axis lines=left,
    axis line style={thick},
    tick style={black,thick},
    ticklabel style={font=\scriptsize},
    scaled ticks=false,
    xtick={0,1000,...,\TotientPlotN},
    ytick={0,1000,...,\TotientPlotN},
    xticklabel={\pgfmathprintnumber[fixed,precision=0]{\tick}},
    yticklabel={\pgfmathprintnumber[fixed,precision=0]{\tick}},
    xlabel={$q$},
    ylabel={$|\Z_q^*|$},
    xlabel style={font=\footnotesize},
    ylabel style={font=\footnotesize},

      at={(0.03,0.97)},
      anchor=north west,
      draw=none,
      fill=white,
      fill opacity=0.86,
      text opacity=1,

    },
    legend cell align=left,
    grid=both,

    minor grid style={black!5},
    minor tick num=1,
    clip=true,
    set layers=standard,
