  ]
    \addplot+[
      only marks,
      overviewgreen!75!black,
      mark=square*,
      mark size=0.28pt,
      mark options={
        draw=overviewgreen!75!black,
        fill=overviewgreen!75!black,
        line width=0pt
      },
      on layer=main,
      forget plot
    ] table[
      x=q,
      y=phi,
      restrict x to domain=1:\TotientPlotN
    ] {totient-values-sieve.dat};

    \addplot[
      alizarincrimsonred!85!black,
      solid,
      mark=none,
      line width=0.75pt,
      on layer=axis foreground,
      domain=16:\TotientPlotN,
      samples=160
    ] {totientomega(x)};
    \addlegendentry{$\eu^{-\gamma}q/\log\log q$}

    \node[
      anchor=south east,
      font=\scriptsize,
      fill=white,
      fill opacity=0.86,
      text opacity=1,
      inner sep=2pt
    ] at (rel axis cs:0.98,0.04) {$\gamma\simeq0.577$};
  \end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\]



% Equivalently, one may use \(V^\dagger\) and the two-dimensional period-finding function
% \[
% f(x,y)
% =
% g^x h^{-y}
% \]
% whose hidden period direction is generated by
% \(
% (\ell,1)
% \).
% Then the measured pair is \((s,-s\ell)\), and the post-processing is
% \(
% \ell\equiv -x^{-1}y\pmod q
% \).


% Let
% \(
% n=\lceil\log_2 q\rceil
% \)
% be the input length of the group order.
% One iteration uses two phase-estimation registers and controlled group exponentiation by precomputed powers of \(g\) and \(h\).
% If one group operation costs \(T_G\), one quantum run costs for instance \(O(T_G\log q+\log^2q)\) in the abstract group-operation model, hence polynomially many operations in \(n\).
% The repetition overhead above is only \(O(\log\log q)\), so the whole algorithm is still polynomial in \(n\).
% By contrast, generic classical discrete logarithm algorithms require
% \(
% \Omega(\sqrt q)
% =
% \Omega(2^{n/2})
% \)
% group operations, and the best known attacks on general elliptic-curve groups have this square-root behavior.
% This is the sense in which Shor's discrete logarithm algorithm gives an exponential speed-up in the generic-group setting.

% Sources for the estimates below:
% - Local qalg notes, PDF p. 87 / printed p. 89, Theorem 6.27:
%   \(O(T\log q+\log^2q)\) quantum gates, where \(T\) is the time for one group operation.
% - Local qalg notes, PDF p. 87 / printed p. 89, paragraph before Theorem 6.27:
%   \(O(\log\log q)\) trials if one repeats until the sampled \(s\) is invertible.
% - Local qalg notes, PDF pp. 87--88 / printed pp. 89--90, Theorem 6.27 and Algorithm 6.5:
%   the two-sample post-processing gives constant success probability without the \(O(\log\log q)\) overhead.
% - Local qalg notes, PDF p. 61 / printed p. 63, Section 5.3, Theorem 5.15 and Algorithm 5.1:
%   exact QFT over \(2^n\) has \(O(n^2)\) gates, giving the \(\log^2q\) contribution.
% - Local qalg notes, PDF p. 75 / printed p. 77, totient paragraph and footnote 3:
%   \(\varphi(r)/r=\Omega(1/\log\log r)\), hence \(O(\log\log q)\) expected repetitions.
% - Shor original paper, arXiv PDF p. 1 abstract and Section 6, PDF pp. 19--24:
%   polynomial-time quantum discrete logarithms, with the Section 6 construction in eqs. (6.1)--(6.20).
%   https://arxiv.org/pdf/quant-ph/9508027
% - Shor original paper, PDF p. 20, Section 6:
%   the algorithm uses two modular exponentiations and two quantum Fourier transforms.
%   https://arxiv.org/pdf/quant-ph/9508027
% - QFT gate count web reference, section "Circuit implementation", final count \(n(n+1)/2=O(n^2)\):
%   https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_Fourier_transform#Circuit_implementation
% - Totient growth web reference, section "Growth rate":
%   \(\liminf_{n\to\infty}\varphi(n)\log\log n/n=\eu^{-\gamma}\), motivating the plotted comparison \(\eu^{-\gamma}n/\log\log n\).
%   https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_totient_function#Growth_rate
% - Totient growth web reference, section "Growth rate", Rosser--Schoenfeld bound / Theorem 15:
%   \(\varphi(n)>n/(e^\gamma\log\log n+3/\log\log n)\) for \(n>2\).
%   https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_totient_function#Growth_rate
% - Generic-group lower bound, Shoup original source, EUROCRYPT 1997, LNCS 1233, pp. 256--266:
%   abstract on printed p. 256 and summary on printed p. 257 state the \(\Omega(\sqrt p)\) lower bound,
%   where \(p\) is the largest prime divisor of the group order; Theorem 1 in Section 2 proves it.
%   https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/3-540-69053-0_18
% - Generic-group lower bound, local qalg notes, PDF p. 88 / printed p. 90, Theorem 6.28 (Shoup):
%   states the prime-order/simplified \(\Omega(\sqrt q)\) form used in the lecture notes.
% - Modern generic discrete-log reference, arXiv PDF p. 14, Theorem 3.1:
%   for prime-order cyclic \(G\), constant-advantage classical generic DL/CDH/DDH algorithms require
%   \(\Omega(\sqrt{|G|})\) queries.
%   https://arxiv.org/pdf/2307.03065
Let
\(
n=\lceil\log_2q\rceil
\) be the input length of the group order, and let \(p_{\max}\) be the largest prime divisor of \(q\).
% Source: \(n=\lceil\log_2q\rceil\) is the input-length convention; Shoup, EUROCRYPT 1997, LNCS 1233, printed pp. 256--257, uses \(p\) for the largest prime divisor of the group order.
Let \(T_G\) denote the number of gates needed to implement one group operation coherently.
% Source: local qalg notes, PDF p. 87 / printed p. 89, Theorem 6.27, where \(T\) is the time for one group operation.
For the repeat-until-\(s\in\Z_q^*\) version above,
the expected gate count is
\[
O\!\left((T_G\log q+\log^2q)\log\log q\right)
\]
because one run uses \(O(T_G\log q+\log^2q)\) quantum gates and \(O(\log\log q)\) expected repetitions
\cite{laneveQuantumAlgorithms,wikipediaQFTGateCount,wikipediaTotientGrowth}.
% Source: one-run gate count from local qalg notes, PDF p. 87 / printed p. 89, Theorem 6.27; repetition count from local qalg notes, PDF p. 75 / printed p. 77, totient paragraph and footnote 3, and PDF p. 87 / printed p. 89, paragraph before Theorem 6.27.
% In the qalg notes this \(\log\log q\) overhead is removed by a constant-success two-sample post-processing.

\pagebreak[2]

Generic classical algorithms, by contrast, need
\[
\Omega(\sqrt{p_{\max}})
\]
group operations in Shoup's generic-group model \cite{shoupGenericDLP}.
% Source: Shoup, EUROCRYPT 1997, LNCS 1233, printed p. 256 abstract and printed p. 257 summary of Theorem 1; \(p_{\max}\) is the largest prime divisor of the group order.
In the prime-order case \(p_{\max}=q\), and this becomes
\[
\Omega(\sqrt q)
=
\Omega(2^{n/2})
\]
which is exponential in the input length \(n\) \cite{shoupGenericDLP,hhanYamakawaYunDiscreteLog}.
% Source: prime-order specialization from Shoup's \(p_{\max}=q\) lower bound; \(q=2^{\Theta(n)}\) follows from \(n=\lceil\log_2q\rceil\).



\clearpage



\section{Quantum Complexity Theory}\label{sec:quantum-complexity-theory}

% Main sources for this section:
% - Nielsen and Chuang, Quantum Computation and Quantum Information, Section 3.2.4,
%   local PDF pp. 184--187 / printed pp. 150--153: L, P, NP, PSPACE, EXP,
%   hierarchy theorems, and the optimistic / pessimistic interpretations.
% - Nielsen and Chuang, Quantum Computation and Quantum Information, Section 4.5.5,
%   local PDF pp. 234--236 / printed pp. 200--202: BQP, uniform circuit families,
%   BPP subset BQP subset PSPACE, and the polynomial-space simulation argument.
% - Algorithms & Complexity summary, public source
%   https://github.com/fabianbosshard/usi-informatics-course-summaries/tree/main/summaries/usi-algorithms-and-complexity,
%   chapters/06_complexity.tex: decision-problem/language viewpoint and notation for P, NP, EXP.

% The complexity-theory viewpoint is the same as in the Algorithms \& Complexity summary
A decision problem is represented by a language
\[
L
\subseteq
\{0,1\}^{*} 
\coloneq
\bigcup_{n=0}^{\infty}\{0,1\}^{n}
\]
and solving the decision problem means deciding membership \(x\in L\).
A complexity class is then a collection of languages that can be decided with a prescribed computational model and prescribed resource bounds.

The three knobs are:
\begin{itemize}
\item
resource: time, space, queries, circuit size
\item
kind of task: decision, search, optimization, approximation
\item
computational model: deterministic, randomized, quantum
\end{itemize}

% For comparing quantum computation with the classical theory learned last semester, we mostly stay with decision problems and polynomial resources.

\subsection{Classical complexity classes}

% Source: Nielsen--Chuang Section 3.2.4, PDF pp. 184--185 / printed pp. 150--151.
\begin{definition}[Some classical complexity classes]\label{def:classical-complexity-reference-classes}
Following \cite{nielsenChuangQCQI}, for languages \(L\subseteq\{0,1\}^{*}\):
\begin{itemize}
\item
\(\mathrm{L}\) is the class decidable using \(O(\log n)\) working space.
The input tape is read-only; the logarithmic bound applies to the work tape.
\item
\(\mathrm{P}\) is the class decidable in polynomial time.
\item
\(\mathrm{NP}\) is the class with polynomial-size certificates verifiable in polynomial time.
\item
\(\mathrm{PSPACE}\) is the class decidable using polynomial working space, with no polynomial bound on time.
\item
\(\mathrm{EXP}\) is the class decidable in exponential time \(O(2^{n^{k}})\) for some constant \(k\).
\qedhere
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}


\begin{claim}\label{claim:classical-complexity-chain}
\leavevmode\setlength{\abovedisplayskip}{0pt}\vspace{-\baselineskip}
\[
\mathrm{L}
\subseteq
\mathrm{P}
\subseteq
\mathrm{NP}
\subseteq
\mathrm{PSPACE}
\subseteq
\mathrm{EXP}
\qedhere
\]
\end{claim}


\begin{proof}
The inclusions have simple resource-counting proofs.

\(\mathrm{L}\subseteq\mathrm{P}\):
a deterministic \(O(\log n)\)-space machine has only polynomially many configurations:
polynomially many input-head positions, polynomially many work-tape contents, polynomially many work-head positions, and constantly many internal states.
A decider cannot visit the same configuration twice without looping, so it halts in polynomial time\footnote{%
An L-machine is a decider, it is not allowed to loop. 
Therefore it must halt before any repetition happens.%
}.

\(\mathrm{P}\subseteq\mathrm{NP}\):
use the empty certificate and run the polynomial-time decision algorithm.

\(\mathrm{NP}\subseteq\mathrm{PSPACE}\):
if the certificate length is bounded by \(p(n)\), enumerate all \(2^{p(n)}\) certificates one after another.
Each verification uses polynomial space, and the work tape can be erased between trials.

\(\mathrm{PSPACE}\subseteq\mathrm{EXP}\):
a polynomial-space machine has at most exponentially many configurations.
If it is a decider, it must halt before repeating a configuration.
\end{proof}

The time hierarchy theorem implies
\(
\mathrm{P}
\subsetneq
\mathrm{EXP}
\)
and the space hierarchy theorem implies
\(
\mathrm{L}
\subsetneq
\mathrm{PSPACE}
\).
Thus at least one inclusion in the chain of \autoref{claim:classical-complexity-chain} must be strict,
but it is not known which one(s).
% However, none of the consecutive inclusions
% \(
% \mathrm{L}\subseteq\mathrm{P}
% \), 
% \(
% \mathrm{P}\subseteq\mathrm{NP}
% \), 
% \(
% \mathrm{NP}\subseteq\mathrm{PSPACE}
% \), 
% \(
% \mathrm{PSPACE}\subseteq\mathrm{EXP}
% \)
% is known to be strict.

\subsection{Bounded-error computation}\label{sec:bounded-error-computation}

Randomized and quantum algorithms are usually allowed a small probability of error.
The constant is not important as long as it is bounded away from \(1/2\),
because independent repetitions and majority vote amplify the success probability\footnote{$1/2$ is the ``random guessing'' threshold for a yes/no answer.}.
For example, success probability \(3/4\) can be amplified to \(1-\varepsilon\) with \(O(\log(1/\varepsilon))\) repetitions.


% Source: Nielsen--Chuang Section 4.5.5, PDF p. 235 / printed p. 201.
\begin{definition}[BPP]\label{def:bpp}
\(\mathrm{BPP}\) (Bounded-error Probabilistic Polynomial-time) is the class of languages decidable by a randomized polynomial-time classical algorithm with bounded two-sided error \cite{nielsenChuangQCQI}.
\end{definition}

\pagebreak[3]

\begin{definition}[BQP]\label{def:bqp}
\(\mathrm{BQP}\) (Bounded-error Quantum Polynomial-time) is the quantum analogue of \autoref{def:bpp} \cite{nielsenChuangQCQI}.
A language \(L\) is in \(\mathrm{BQP}\) if there exists a \emph{uniform} family of quantum circuits
\[
\{C_n\}_{n\in\N}
\]
such that:
\begin{itemize}
\item
\(C_n\) has size polynomial in \(n\)
\item
on input \(x\in\{0,1\}^{n}\), the circuit \(C_n\) decides whether \(x\in L\) by measuring a designated output Qbit
\item
if \(x\in L\), the circuit accepts with probability at least \(3/4\)
\item
if \(x\notin L\), the circuit rejects with probability at least \(3/4\)
\end{itemize}
Uniform means that there is a classical Turing machine which, on input \(1^n\), outputs a description of \(C_n\) in time polynomial in \(n\).
\end{definition}

The family \(\{C_n\}\) is needed because one fixed circuit only has finitely many input wires.
Uniformity rules out hiding an arbitrarily hard language in the choice of the circuits themselves:
there must be an efficient classical recipe for constructing the circuit for length \(n\).

% % Source: classification follows the decision/search distinction used in the Algorithms & Complexity summary,
% % together with the algorithmic results proved in the earlier sections of this summary.
% \begin{remark}[Where the algorithms above fit]\label{rem:algorithm-complexity-classes}
% Strictly speaking, \(\mathrm{BQP}\) is a decision-problem class.
% Several algorithms above are therefore better classified first as promise, oracle, search, or function problems.
% The algorithms in \autoref{sec:simple-algorithms} are query algorithms:
% \autoref{sec:deutsch-algorithm} and \autoref{sec:deutsch-jozsa-algorithm} are exact quantum algorithms for promise decision problems, while \autoref{sec:bernstein-vazirani-algorithm} and \autoref{sec:simons-algorithm} are naturally search problems in the oracle model.
% The latter belong to the function analogue of \(\mathrm{BQP}\), often denoted \(\mathrm{FBQP}\), where the output is a string satisfying a relation rather than a single yes/no bit.
% \autoref{sec:grover} is also a search/query algorithm: the OR decision version is solvable with \(O(\sqrt N)\) quantum queries, but for \(N=2^n\) this is still exponential in the input length \(n\), so Grover's algorithm does not put \(\mathrm{NP}\) inside \(\mathrm{BQP}\).
% By contrast, Shor's algorithms in \autoref{sec:shor-factoring} and \autoref{sec:shor-discrete-logarithm} run in time polynomial in the input length.
% Factoring and discrete logarithms are function problems, so the precise statement is
% \[
% \text{factoring},\ \text{discrete logarithm}
% \in
% \mathrm{FBQP}.
% \]
% Corresponding decision versions are in \(\mathrm{BQP}\).
% \end{remark}

\begin{fact}\label{fact:bpp-bqp-pspace}
\leavevmode\setlength{\abovedisplayskip}{0pt}\vspace{-\baselineskip}
\[
\mathrm{P}
\subseteq
\mathrm{BPP}
\subseteq
\mathrm{BQP}
\subseteq
\mathrm{PSPACE}
\qedhere
\]
\end{fact}

\autoref{claim:classical-complexity-chain} and \autoref{fact:bpp-bqp-pspace} are summarized in the following diagram:
\[
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  scale=0.82,
  >=Stealth,
  class/.style={
    draw,
    rounded corners=2pt,
    minimum width=1.65cm,
    minimum height=0.54cm,
    align=center,
    inner sep=2pt,
    font=\footnotesize
  },
  inc/.style={-{Stealth[length=1.45mm]}, thick},
  note/.style={font=\scriptsize, align=center}
]
  \node[class] (L) at (0,1.35) {$\mathrm{L}$};
  \node[class] (P) at (3,1.35) {$\mathrm{P}$};
  \node[class] (NP) at (6,1.35) {$\mathrm{NP}$};
  \node[class] (PSPACE) at (9,1.35) {$\mathrm{PSPACE}$};
  \node[class] (EXP) at (12,1.35) {$\mathrm{EXP}$};

  \node[class] (BPP) at (4.5,0) {$\mathrm{BPP}$};
  \node[class] (BQP) at (7.5,0) {$\mathrm{BQP}$};

  \draw[inc] (L) -- (P);
  \draw[inc] (P) -- (NP);
  \draw[inc] (NP) -- (PSPACE);
  \draw[inc] (PSPACE) -- (EXP);
  \draw[inc] (P) -- (BPP);
  \draw[inc] (BPP) -- (BQP);
  \draw[inc] (BQP) -- (PSPACE);

  % \node[note] at (4.55,-0.72)
  %   {only proven inclusions are shown;\\no inclusion between \(\mathrm{NP}\) and \(\mathrm{BQP}\) is known};
\end{tikzpicture}
\]


The inclusion \(\mathrm{BPP}\subseteq\mathrm{BQP}\) is the easy one:
a quantum circuit can simulate a randomized classical polynomial-time computation.

% Random bits are generated by preparing \(\ket0\), applying a Hadamard, and measuring in the computational basis.
% Indeed, by \autoref{ex:hadamard},
% \[
% H\ket0
% =
% \frac{\ket0+\ket1}{\sqrt2},
% \]
% so the Born rule from \autoref{sec:observables} gives outcome \(0\) and outcome \(1\) with probability \(1/2\) each.
% Equivalently, by \eqref{eq:nfold-hadamard-zero}, measuring
% \(
% H^{\otimes k}\ket{\vect0}
% \)
% gives a uniformly random \(k\)-bit string.
Random bits are generated directly in parallel.
To obtain \(k\) independent random bits, prepare
\(
\ket{\vect 0}
=
\ket{0}^{\otimes k}
\),
apply Hadamards to all \(k\) Qbits, and measure in the computational basis.
% By \eqref{eq:nfold-hadamard-zero},
% \[
% H^{\otimes k}\ket{\vect 0}
% =
% \frac{1}{\sqrt{2^k}}
% \sum_{r\in\{0,1\}^k}
% \ket r
% \]
% so the Born rule from \autoref{sec:observables} says that the outcome
% \(
% r\in\{0,1\}^k
% \)
% is obtained with probability
% \(
% \left|
% \frac{1}{\sqrt{2^k}}
% \right|^2
% =
% \frac{1}{2^k}
% \).
% Thus the measurement produces a uniformly random \(k\)-bit string.
As seen in \autoref{ex:hadamard-n-qbits},
\[
H^{\otimes k}\ket{\vect 0}
\overset{\text{\eqref{eq:nfold-hadamard-zero}}}{=}
\frac{1}{\sqrt{2^k}}
\sum_{\vect r\in\{0,1\}^k}
\ket{\vect r}
\]
so the Born rule from \autoref{sec:observables} gives every outcome \(\vect r\in\{0,1\}^k\) probability \(1/2^k\).
Thus the measurement produces a uniformly random \(k\)-bit string.

The deterministic part of the classical computation can then be embedded into a reversible computation as in \autoref{sec:reversible-computing}.
At the gate level, \autoref{sec:toffoli-gate} explains why Toffoli gates are enough for reversible classical computation, and the \hyperref[sec:cnot-classical-universality]{classical-universality part of \autoref{sec:cnot-gate}} explains how CNOT plus one-Qbit gates gives Toffoli.

\medskip



The nontrivial and important inclusion is %\(\mathrm{BQP}\subseteq\mathrm{PSPACE}\).

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\(\mathrm{BQP}\subseteq\mathrm{PSPACE}\)}{BQP subset PSPACE}}\label{sec:bqp-subset-pspace}

% Source: Nielsen--Chuang Section 4.5.5, PDF pp. 235--236 / printed pp. 201--202.
The proof below follows the standard polynomial-space simulation argument in \cite{nielsenChuangQCQI}.
% Let a polynomial-size quantum circuit on \(m=\operatorname{poly}(n)\) Qbits be % what is $n$?
Let \(n\) be the input length.
After adding polynomially many ancilla Qbits, the circuit acts on \(m(n)=\operatorname{poly}(n)\) Qbits and has \(T(n)=\operatorname{poly}(n)\) elementary gates.
We assume a fixed finite universal gate set, or more generally elementary gates whose matrix entries can be computed to polynomially many bits using polynomial space.

By \autoref{fact:deferred-measurement-principle}, we may assume that the computation is unitary until a final measurement of one designated output Qbit.
Write the unitary part as
\[
C
=
% U_T U_{T-1} \cdots U_2 U_1
% \prod_{t=T}^{1} U_t
% =
U_T \cdots U_1
\]
% where \(T=\operatorname{poly}(n)\), and the initial state is the computational-basis state \(\ket{\eta_x}\).
% what is the meaning of $T$? what is the meaning of $U_t$? what is the dimension of $U_t$ (m times m)? what if the quantum algorithm is not of that simple form?
where each \(U_t\) denotes the full \(2^m\times2^m\) unitary on
\(
\mathcal H_m=(\C^2)^{\otimes m}
\)
obtained by applying one elementary one- or two-Qbit gate to its wires and the identity to all other wires. % why only one or two qbits? because that allows to build any unitary on \(m\) Qbits? 

The point is not that every \(m\)-Qbit unitary is counted as one allowed operation.
Rather, the circuit model counts local elementary gates.
One- and two-Qbit gate sets are universal in the sense discussed around \autoref{sec:cnot-gate}, but a generic \(m\)-Qbit unitary may require exponentially many local gates to implement.
Allowing such a unitary as a single \(U_t\) would hide the exponential complexity inside one gate.

\pagebreak[3]

Let the initial computational-basis state be
\[
\ket{\eta_x}
=
\ket{x}\ket{0}^{\otimes a(n)}
\]
with $a(n)=m(n)-n$,
where the second register contains the ancilla Qbits.


First fix a final basis state \(\ket{\vect{y}}\).
% To compute the amplitude of a final basis state 
% \(\ket{\vect{y}}\),  % so we calculate the amplitudes of all possible $2^m$ final basis states \(\ket{\vect{y}}\)?
To compute its amplitude, insert the computational-basis resolution of the identity from \eqref{eq:computational-basis-resolution-identity}, with \(n\) replaced by \(m\),
\[
\sum_{\vect{z}\in\{0,1\}^{m}}\ket{\vect{z}}\bra{\vect{z}}
=
\id_{\mathcal H_m}
% \id % id of what space? what dimenison?
\]
between every pair of gates:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:history-sum}
\braket{\vect{y}|U_T\cdots U_1|\eta_x}
=
\sum_{\vect{z}_1,\ldots,\vect{z}_{T-1}}
\braket{\vect{y}|U_T|\vect{z}_{T-1}}
% \prod_{t=T-1}^{2} \braket{\vect{z}_t|U_t|\vect{z}_{t-1}}
\braket{\vect{z}_{T-1}|U_{T-1}|\vect{z}_{T-2}}
\cdots
\braket{\vect{z}_2|U_2|\vect{z}_1}
\braket{\vect{z}_1|U_1|\eta_x}
\end{equation}
% This is a sum over exponentially many computational histories.
% It may take exponential time to evaluate, but it only needs polynomial space:
% one stores the current tuple
This is a sum over many computational histories:
each intermediate \(\vect{z}_t\) ranges over \(2^m\) basis strings, and there are \(T-1\) such intermediate times.
Thus the number of summands is
\(
2^{m(T-1)}
=
2^{\operatorname{poly}(n)}
\),
because \(m=\operatorname{poly}(n)\) and \(T=\operatorname{poly}(n)\).
This may take exponential time, but only polynomial space:
the classical simulator can run nested loops over
\(
\vect{z}_1,\ldots,\vect{z}_{T-1}
\),
% the current product, and the running sum, each to polynomial precision.
% After a term has been added, its workspace can be reused.
store the current \(m\)-bit strings, the current product, and the running sum, and then reuse this workspace after each summand has been added.
Although exponentially many summands are added, accuracy \(2^{-\operatorname{poly}(n)}\) still requires only polynomially many bits, and is enough to make the final additive error smaller than any fixed constant.



% The acceptance probability is obtained by summing \(|\alpha_{\vect{y}}|^2\) over accepting basis states \(\vect{y}\). % what? 
Now use that \(\mathrm{BQP}\) is a decision class.
The simulator does not need to find the most likely output string, nor sample from the whole final distribution.
It only needs the probability that the designated output Qbit is \(1\).
If
\[
A
\coloneq
\left\{
\vect{y}\in\{0,1\}^{m}
:
y_{\mathrm{out}}=1
\right\}
\]
is the set of accepting basis states, then
\begin{equation}\label{eq:acceptance-probability}
\Prob(\text{accept})
=
\sum_{\vect{y}\in A}
\left|
\braket{\vect{y}|U_T\cdots U_1|\eta_x}
\right|^2
\end{equation}
% This outer sum is also exponential, % exponential in what? and why?
% but it can again be evaluated term by term:
% store the current \(\vect{y}\), 
% compute its amplitude using the history sum above, 
% add the squared modulus to a running total, and erase the temporary amplitude.
% Because the \(\mathrm{BQP}\) promise gap is constant, % what is a promise gap?
% it is enough to approximate \(\Prob(\text{accept})\) to error, say, \(1/10\), % why is it enough to be that inaccurate? where does the magic number 1/10 come from?
% and then distinguish the cases \(\Prob(\text{accept})\ge 3/4\) and \(\Prob(\text{accept})\le 1/4\).
This outer sum is exponential in the number \(m\) of Qbits:
if one output Qbit is designated, then roughly half of the \(2^m\) basis states have \(y_{\mathrm{out}}=1\), so
\[
|A|
=
2^{m-1}
=
2^{\operatorname{poly}(n)} 
\]
Again this is allowed in \(\mathrm{PSPACE}\), because only space is restricted.
The simulator enumerates the accepting \(\vect{y}\)'s one after another, 
computes the amplitude for the current \(\vect{y}\) using the history sum \eqref{eq:history-sum}, 
adds the squared modulus to a running total, and erases the temporary amplitude.

Finally use the bounded-error gap in the definition of \(\mathrm{BQP}\).
For every input \(x\), the circuit is guaranteed to be in one of the two separated cases
\[
x\in L
\implies
\Prob(\text{accept})\ge \frac34,
\qquad
x\notin L
\implies
\Prob(\text{accept})\le \frac14
\]
The gap between the two thresholds is \(1/2\).
Hence the classical simulator does not need the exact acceptance probability.

\begin{remark}
Mathematically, the acceptance probability \eqref{eq:acceptance-probability} is exact.
The error below is not quantum error; it is only numerical approximation error from storing amplitudes and partial sums with finite precision on the classical work tape.
\end{remark}

Any additive error strictly smaller than half of this gap is enough.
Fix any constant
\(
0<\varepsilon<1/4
\).
If the simulator computes an estimate \(\widetilde p\) with
\[
\left|\widetilde p-\Prob(\text{accept})\right|
\le
\varepsilon
\]
then
\[
x\in L
\implies
\widetilde p\ge \frac34-\varepsilon>\frac12,
\qquad
x\notin L
\implies
\widetilde p\le \frac14+\varepsilon<\frac12
\]
Comparing \(\widetilde p\) with \(1/2\) therefore decides whether \(x\in L\).
Thus a classical Turing machine can simulate the bounded-error quantum circuit using polynomial space, proving
\(
\mathrm{BQP}
\subseteq
\mathrm{PSPACE}
\).


\paragraph{Consequences}
Quantum computers do not enlarge the class of computable functions:
their computations can be simulated classically if no efficiency requirement is imposed.
What is at stake is efficiency.

\pagebreak[3]


% \begin{remark}[Consequences]\label{rem:quantum-complexity-consequences}
\subsection{Extended Church--Turing thesis}

% Quantum computation challenges the strong Church--Turing thesis,
%  which informally says that every physically reasonable computation can be simulated efficiently by a probabilistic classical machine.
% The strong Church--Turing thesis informally says that every physically reasonable computing device can be simulated efficiently by a probabilistic classical machine.
While the original Church--Turing thesis is about computability, the \emph{extended} (or \emph{strong}) Church--Turing thesis is about efficient computability.
It says that every ``{reasonable}'' or ``{physically realizable}'' model of computation can be simulated by a probabilistic classical machine with at most polynomial overhead.
It is less known and less widely accepted than the original Church--Turing thesis.

% Quantum computation challenges this because there are natural problems, such as factoring and discrete logarithms in \autoref{sec:shor}, that have polynomial-time quantum algorithms, while no polynomial-time randomized classical algorithms are known for them.
% Thus quantum computers are not believed to compute uncomputable functions; rather, they may compute some ordinary computable functions much more efficiently.
% \end{remark}

Thanks to Shor's \autoref{alg:shor-factoring} from \autoref{sec:shor}, we know that one of the following statements must be false \cite{gharibianQuantumComplexityTheory}:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item The extended Church--Turing thesis is true.
  \item FACTOR cannot be solved efficiently by a classical randomized algorithm.\footnote{i.e., suitable decision versions of FACTOR are not in \(\mathrm{BPP}\)}
  \item large-scale universal quantum computers can be built.
\end{enumerate}


Any two of the three statements together imply that the remaining one is false.\footnote{%
This comparison needs some care: the first two claims are asymptotic complexity statements, while the third concerns finite physical circuits where one does not necessarily care about very large input sizes.%
}




\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The Sword in the Stone: Quantum Analogues of \(\mathrm{NP}\)}{The Sword in the Stone: Quantum Analogues of NP}}\label{sec:qma}

% Sources:
% - QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 5.1, PDF pp. 50--52 / printed pp. 47--49: QMA definition, promise-problem viewpoint, and pure quantum witnesses.
% - QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 5.3.1, PDF pp. 58--59 / printed pp. 55--56: QCMA/QMA variants and basic containments.
% - QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 6, PDF pp. 62--69 / printed pp. 59--66: Local Hamiltonian and the quantum Cook--Levin theorem.
% Source for the NP-verification comparison: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 5 introduction, PDF p. 50 / printed p. 47.
% \autoref{def:classical-complexity-reference-classes} described
% \(
% \mathrm{NP}
% \)
% as polynomial-time verification with a polynomial-size classical certificate.
% Source for the non-uniqueness of quantum analogues and the de facto role of QMA: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 5 introduction, PDF p. 50 / printed p. 47.
There is not a single unique quantum analogue of
\(
\mathrm{NP}
\) (\autoref{def:classical-complexity-reference-classes}) \cite{gharibianQuantumComplexityTheory}.
The verifier can be quantum, and the certificate can be classical or quantum.
Merlin--Arthur terminology is usually used here:
Merlin sends a witness, and Arthur verifies it.


% % Source for the MA definition: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Definition 5.1, PDF p. 50 / printed p. 47, defines PromiseMA with constants \(2/3\) and \(1/3\).
% % Exercise 5.2 on the same page explicitly asks for the no-promise version, i.e. MA; the definition below is obtained by dropping the invalid-input case.
% \begin{definition}[\(\mathrm{MA}\)]\label{def:ma}
% A language
% \(
% L\subseteq\{0,1\}^*
% \)
% is in
% \(
% \mathrm{MA}
% \)
% if there is a deterministic Turing machine
% \(
% M
% \)
% and polynomials
% \(
% p,s,r
% \)
% such that, for every input
% \(
% x\in\{0,1\}^n
% \),
% proof
% \(
% y\in\{0,1\}^{p(n)}
% \),
% and random string
% \(
% z\in\{0,1\}^{s(n)}
% \),
% \(
% M(x,y,z)
% \)
% halts in
% \(
% O(r(n))
% \)
% steps and:
% \begin{itemize}
% \item
% if
% \(
% x\in L
% \),
% then there exists a proof
% \(
% y
% \)
% such that
% \(
% M(x,y,z)
% \)
% accepts for at least
% \(
% 2/3
% \)
% of all random strings
% \(
% z
% \);
% \item
% if
% \(
% x\notin L
% \),
% then for every proof
% \(
% y
% \),
% \(
% M(x,y,z)
% \)
% accepts for at most
% \(
% 1/3
% \)
% of all random strings
% \(
% z
% \).
% \end{itemize}
% \end{definition}

% Source for the promise-problem convention: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Definition 5.1 and Definition 5.4, PDF pp. 50--51 / printed pp. 47--48.
Strictly speaking, these classes are usually defined for \emph{promise problems} \cite{gharibianQuantumComplexityTheory}.
This means that there are two disjoint sets
\(
L_{\mathrm{yes}}
\)
and
\(
L_{\mathrm{no}}
\),
and the verifier only has to behave correctly on inputs promised to lie in
\(
L_{\mathrm{yes}}\cup L_{\mathrm{no}}
\).
On inputs outside this promise set, no correctness requirement is imposed.
% This is the same kind of gap as in the bounded-error definition of
% \(
% \mathrm{BQP}
% \)
% from \autoref{def:bqp}. 

% Source for QMA definition: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Definition 5.4, PDF p. 51 / printed p. 48.
% The source writes the promise problem as \(A=(A_{\mathrm{yes}},A_{\mathrm{no}},A_{\mathrm{inv}})\), uses a P-uniform circuit family \(\{Q_n\}\), and uses thresholds \(2/3\) and \(1/3\).
% The version below uses the equivalent two-set promise notation \(L=(L_{\mathrm{yes}},L_{\mathrm{no}})\), renames the verifier to \(V_n\), and uses the amplified constant gap \(3/4\) versus \(1/4\).
% Source for changing constant completeness/soundness parameters: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, weak error reduction discussion after Definition 5.4, PDF p. 51 / printed p. 48, and strong error reduction in Section 5.2.
\begin{definition}[\(\mathrm{QMA}\)]\label{def:qma}
A promise problem
\(
L=(L_{\mathrm{yes}},L_{\mathrm{no}})
\)
is in
\(
\mathrm{QMA}
\)
if there is a polynomial-size uniform quantum circuit family
\(
\{V_n\}_{n\in\N}
\)
and polynomials
\(
p,q
\)
such that, on an input
\(
x\in\{0,1\}^n
\),
the verifier receives
\(
x
\),
a
\(
p(n)
\)-Qbit witness
\(
\ket{\psi}
\),
and
\(
q(n)
\)
ancilla Qbits initialized to
\(
\ket{0}^{\otimes q(n)}
\).
After applying
\(
V_n
\),
one designated output Qbit is measured in the computational basis.
The requirements are:
\begin{itemize}
\item
Completeness (YES-case): if
\(
x\in L_{\mathrm{yes}}
\),
then there exists a quantum witness
\(
\ket{\psi}
\)
that makes the verifier accept with probability at least
\(
3/4
\)
\item
Soundness (NO-case): if
\(
x\in L_{\mathrm{no}}
\),
then every quantum witness
\(
\ket{\psi}
\)
makes the verifier accept with probability at most
\(
1/4
\)
\qedhere
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

% Source for constant-gap robustness: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Exercise 5.3 for PromiseMA and weak/strong error reduction for QMA after Definition 5.4, PDF pp. 51--60 / printed pp. 48--57.
The constants
\(
3/4
\)
and
\(
1/4
\)
are not important.
As in
\(
\mathrm{BPP}
\) (\autoref{def:bpp})
and
\(
\mathrm{BQP}
\) (\autoref{def:bqp}),
the precise constants are not part of the class definition.
For \(\mathrm{QMA}\), this robustness follows from standard QMA error-reduction results.
% Source for pure witnesses and the linear/eigenvalue formulation of the verifier acceptance probability: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, "Pure versus mixed proofs", PDF p. 52 / printed p. 49, especially Equations (5.1)--(5.3) and Exercise 5.9.
It is also enough to quantify over pure witnesses:
a mixed witness is a convex combination of pure states, and the verifier's acceptance probability is linear in the density matrix.
Therefore the best mixed witness cannot do better than some pure witness.

% Source for QCMA: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 5.3.1, PDF p. 58 / printed p. 55.
\begin{definition}[\(\mathrm{QCMA}\)]\label{def:qcma}
\(
\mathrm{QCMA}
\)
is defined like
\(
\mathrm{QMA}
\),
except that Merlin's witness is an ordinary polynomial-size classical bit string (i.e. a classical ``certificate'').
% Thus the verifier is quantum, but the certificate is classical.
\end{definition}


% Source for MA: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Definition 5.1, PDF p. 50 / printed p. 47, defines PromiseMA; Exercise 5.2 asks for the no-promise MA version.
% Source for NP subset MA: direct from the definition of MA, by ignoring randomness.
% Source for MA subset QCMA: direct from \autoref{def:qcma}, because a quantum verifier can simulate the classical randomized verifier and generate random bits as in the proof of \(\mathrm{BPP}\subseteq\mathrm{BQP}\).
\(
\mathrm{MA}
\)
is the classical Merlin--Arthur class:
Merlin sends a classical witness, and Arthur checks it using a randomized polynomial-time classical verifier.
MA fits between
\[
\mathrm{NP}
\subseteq
\mathrm{MA}
\subseteq
\mathrm{QCMA}
\]
The first inclusion holds because an MA verifier is allowed but not forced to use randomness, so it can simply run the deterministic NP verifier.
The second inclusion holds because a quantum verifier can simulate the classical randomized verifier and generate random bits (same story as in the proof of \(\mathrm{BPP}\subseteq\mathrm{BQP}\) in \autoref{sec:bounded-error-computation}).

\pagebreak[3]


% Source for BQP subset QCMA subset QMA: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 5.3.1, PDF p. 58 / printed p. 55.
% Source for P subset NP: Nielsen--Chuang Section 3.2.4 and \autoref{claim:classical-complexity-chain}.
% Source for QMA subset PSPACE: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 5.3.1--5.3.2, PDF pp. 59--60 / printed pp. 56--57, which states QMA subset PP and recalls PP subset PSPACE.
% The inclusion NP subset QCMA follows directly from the definitions: a quantum verifier can run the deterministic NP verifier and check the classical certificate.
We have \cite{gharibianQuantumComplexityTheory}
% \begin{gather}
% % \mathrm{P}
% % \subseteq
% \mathrm{NP}
% \subseteq
% \mathrm{QCMA}
% % \subseteq
% % \mathrm{QMA}
% \label{eq:qma-containments-classical-witness-chain}
% \\
\[
\mathrm{BQP}
\subseteq
\mathrm{QCMA}
\subseteq
\mathrm{QMA}
\subseteq
\mathrm{PSPACE}
\]
% \label{eq:qma-containments-quantum-upper-bound-chain}
% \end{gather}
% % Source/justification: this is the direct simulation argument for \(\mathrm{NP}\subseteq\mathrm{QCMA}\) from \autoref{def:qcma} and the classical definition of NP in \autoref{def:classical-complexity-reference-classes}.
% \eqref{eq:qma-containments-classical-witness-chain} says that a quantum verifier with a classical witness generalizes ordinary deterministic verification with classical certificates.
% Indeed, the quantum verifier can simulate the deterministic
% \(
% \mathrm{NP}
% \)
% verifier and check the same classical certificate.
% Source/justification for BQP subset QCMA: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 5.3.1, PDF p. 58 / printed p. 55; the empty-witness proof is the standard trivial-inclusion proof.
% In \eqref{eq:qma-containments-quantum-upper-bound-chain}, 
The first inclusion
% \(
% \mathrm{BQP}\subseteq\mathrm{QCMA}
% \)
uses an empty classical witness:
Arthur ignores the witness register and simply runs the
\(
\mathrm{BQP}
\)
verifier from \autoref{def:bqp} on the input $x$ (same story as for \(
\mathrm{P}\subseteq\mathrm{NP}
\)).

% The second inclusion
% % \(
% % \mathrm{QCMA}\subseteq\mathrm{QMA}
% % \)
% holds because a classical witness is a special case of a quantum witness in the computational basis.
% % Source/justification for QMA subset PSPACE in this sentence: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, PDF p. 52 / printed p. 49 gives the largest-eigenvalue formulation, and PDF pp. 59--60 / printed pp. 56--57 give the upper bound QMA subset PP with PP subset PSPACE.

% The second inclusion
% % \(
% % \mathrm{QCMA}\subseteq\mathrm{QMA}
% % \)
% holds because the \(\mathrm{QMA}\) verifier can first measure Merlin's witness in the computational basis and then run the \(\mathrm{QCMA}\) verifier on the resulting classical string.
% Thus honest classical witnesses are allowed, while arbitrary quantum witnesses give only convex combinations of classical witnesses.
% % Source/justification for QMA subset PSPACE in this sentence: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, PDF p. 52 / printed p. 49 gives the largest-eigenvalue formulation, and PDF pp. 59--60 / printed pp. 56--57 give the upper bound QMA subset PP with PP subset PSPACE.

The second inclusion holds because every classical certificate \(w\) can be encoded as the computational-basis quantum state \(\ket w\). 
The QMA verifier first measures the witness register in the computational basis, obtaining a classical string, and then runs the QCMA verifier on that string. 
Hence honest QCMA witnesses (satisfying the completeness condition in a YES case) are valid QMA witnesses after this encoding. 
If Merlin sends an arbitrary quantum state instead, the first measurement only produces a probability distribution over classical strings, so the acceptance probability is a convex combination of the QCMA acceptance probabilities.

% The second inclusion holds because a classical witness
% \(
% w %\in\{0,1\}^{p(n)}
% \)
% is the computational-basis quantum state
% \(
% \ket{w}
% \).
% The \(\mathrm{QMA}\) verifier can first measure Merlin's witness in the computational basis and then run the \(\mathrm{QCMA}\) verifier on the resulting classical string.
% Thus honest classical witnesses are allowed as computational-basis quantum states.
% If Merlin instead sends an arbitrary quantum witness, the initial computational-basis measurement merely induces a probability distribution over classical strings, so the acceptance probability is a convex combination of the acceptance probabilities for classical witnesses.

The last inclusion is %a standard upper bound, 
analogous in spirit to \autoref{sec:bqp-subset-pspace}.
% For each fixed input \(x\), the verifier defines an acceptance operator acting on Merlin's witness register.
% The maximum acceptance probability over all witnesses is the largest eigenvalue of this operator.
% Although the operator is exponentially large, its entries can be computed from the verifier circuit, and the largest eigenvalue can be approximated using polynomial space.
For each fixed input \(x\), the verifier circuit \(V_x\) defines an acceptance operator \(A_x\) acting on Merlin's witness register.
For every pure witness \(\ket\psi\), the acceptance probability has the form
\(
p_{\mathrm{acc}}(\psi) \allowbreak
= \allowbreak
\braket{\psi | A_x | \psi}
\).
Hence the best possible witness is obtained by 
% maximizing this quadratic form:
\(
\max_{\ket\psi}\braket{\psi | A_x | \psi}
=
\lambda_{\max}(A_x)
\),
where \(\lambda_{\max}(A_x)\) is the largest eigenvalue of \(A_x\).
Although \(A_x\) is exponentially large, its entries can be computed on demand from the verifier circuit \(V_x\), and \(\lambda_{\max}(A_x)\) can therefore be approximated using polynomial space.

% The analogy with
% \(
% \mathrm{NP}
% \)
% should not be overread.
% \(
% \mathrm{QMA}
% \)
% does not mean that Arthur can find the witness efficiently; it only means that Arthur can verify it efficiently if Merlin supplies it.
% Similarly, the query speed-up in \autoref{sec:grover} does not prove
% \(
% \mathrm{NP}\subseteq\mathrm{BQP}
% \):
% Grover's algorithm gives a quadratic black-box search improvement, while
% \(
% \mathrm{BQP}
% \)
% has no extra witness input.

\medskip
% Source for Local Hamiltonian as the quantum Cook--Levin/SAT analogue: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 6 introduction, PDF p. 62 / printed p. 59.
The quantum analogue of
\(
\mathrm{SAT}
\)
is the \emph{Local Hamiltonian} problem \cite{gharibianQuantumComplexityTheory}.
% Source for Hamiltonians, locality, energy levels, and ground-state energy: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Definition 6.10, PDF p. 65 / printed p. 62.
Recall from \eqref{eq:hamiltonian} and \autoref{sec:observables} that a Hamiltonian is a Hermitian operator whose eigenvalues are interpreted as energy levels.
For a Hamiltonian
\(
H
\),
write
\(
\lambda_{\min}(H)
\)
for its smallest eigenvalue, i.e. its ground-state energy.
%
% Source for the witness analogy: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Lemma 6.23 and its proof sketch, PDF pp. 69--70 / printed pp. 66--67.
The analogy is that a satisfying assignment is a classical witness for
\(
\mathrm{SAT}
\),
whereas a low-energy quantum state is a quantum witness for $\mathrm{LH}$.
Arthur's task is to check whether the supplied state has low expected energy 
$\braket{\psi|H|\psi}$ 
with respect to
\(
H
\).

% Source for the k-$\mathrm{LH}$ promise problem: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Definition 6.20, PDF p. 68 / printed p. 65.
% The notation below uses the standard convention \(a<b\), with YES case \(\lambda_{\min}(H)\le a\), NO case \(\lambda_{\min}(H)\ge b\), and promise gap \(b-a\ge1/\operatorname{poly}(n)\).
\begin{problem}[\(k\)-$\mathrm{LH}$]\label{problem:k-local-hamiltonian}
Given a Hamiltonian
\(
H=\sum_j H_j
\)
on
\(
n
\)
Qbits, where each term
\(
H_j
\)
acts nontrivially on at most
\(
k
\)
Qbits, and two thresholds
\(
a<b
\)
with
\(
b-a\ge 1/\operatorname{poly}(n)
\).
Here the number of terms is polynomial in \(n\), each \(H_j\) is specified by polynomially many bits, and the operator norms are bounded by \(\operatorname{poly}(n)\).
Decide whether
\[
\lambda_{\min}(H)\le a
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
\lambda_{\min}(H)\ge b
\]
promised that one of the two cases holds.
\end{problem}

% Source for the theorem: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Theorem 6.22, PDF p. 69 / printed p. 66.
% Source for the \(k=2\) refinement comment: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, paragraph immediately after Theorem 6.22, PDF p. 69 / printed p. 66.
Kitaev's quantum Cook--Levin theorem says that
\(
k\text{-}\mathrm{LH}
\) is
\(
\mathrm{QMA}
\)-complete for $k \ge 5$.
% With stronger results, even
% \(
% 2
% \)-Local Hamiltonian is
% \(
% \mathrm{QMA}
% \)-complete.
% Source for "QMA-complete" meaning: QCT_Masterfile.pdf, Section 6.3, PDF p. 69 / printed p. 66, which says containment in QMA is shown in Section 6.3.1 and QMA-hardness in Section 6.3.2.
\(\mathrm{QMA}\)-complete means that the problem is in
\(
\mathrm{QMA}
\),
and every problem in
\(
\mathrm{QMA}
\)
can be reduced to it by a polynomial-time classical reduction.
Thus $\mathrm{LH}$ plays for
\(
\mathrm{QMA}
\)
a similare role as
\(
\mathrm{SAT}
\)
plays for
\(
\mathrm{NP}
\) \cite{gharibianQuantumComplexityTheory}.

\subsection{How optimistic should we be?}

% Source: Nielsen--Chuang Section 3.2.4, PDF p. 185 / printed p. 151,
% and Section 4.5.5, PDF p. 235 / printed p. 201.
% The previous paragraphs should be read together with the algorithms from the earlier sections.
\autoref{sec:grover} gives a provable query-complexity advantage, while \autoref{sec:shor} gives polynomial-time quantum algorithms for important arithmetic problems \cite{nielsenChuangQCQI}.
These results suggest that quantum computation can be more efficient than classical randomized computation for some natural problems.

However, they are still not unconditional complexity-class separations:
the Shor-type algorithms from \autoref{sec:shor} put suitable decision versions of factoring and discrete logarithms in \(\mathrm{BQP}\), but they do not prove
\(
\mathrm{P}\subsetneq\mathrm{BQP}
\)
or
\(
\mathrm{BPP}\subsetneq\mathrm{BQP}
\).

The inclusions in \autoref{fact:bpp-bqp-pspace} explain why such a proof would be a major breakthrough:
% \begin{itemize}
% \item
% Since
% \(
% \mathrm{BQP}
% \subseteq
% \mathrm{PSPACE}
% \),
% an unconditional proof that
% \(
% \mathrm{P}\subsetneq\mathrm{BQP}
% \)
% would imply
% \(
% \mathrm{P}
% \neq
% \mathrm{PSPACE}
% \).
% % Indeed, if \(\mathrm{P}=\mathrm{PSPACE}\), then
% % \(
% % \mathrm{BQP}\subseteq\mathrm{PSPACE}=\mathrm{P}
% % \).
% Hence, proving a genuine deterministic classical lower bound for a \(\mathrm{BQP}\) problem would separate two classical classes whose equality is an open problem.
% \item
% Similarly, an unconditional proof that quantum polynomial time is stronger than randomized classical polynomial time,
% \(
% \mathrm{BPP}
% \subsetneq
% \mathrm{BQP}
% \)
% would imply
% \(
% \mathrm{BPP}
% \neq
% \mathrm{PSPACE}
% \)
% which would also solve a major open problem in classical complexity theory.
% \end{itemize}
\begin{itemize}
\item
\(
\mathrm{P}\subsetneq\mathrm{BQP}
\implies
\mathrm{P}
\neq
\mathrm{PSPACE}
\)
\item
\(
\mathrm{BPP}
\subsetneq
\mathrm{BQP}
\implies
\mathrm{BPP}
\neq
\mathrm{PSPACE}
\)
\end{itemize}


There are different ways to look at this situation \cite{nielsenChuangQCQI}.

% \begin{remark}[Three views]\label{rem:three-views-quantum-advantage}
\textcolor{funblue}{Optimistic}.
Quantum algorithms might provide new structural ideas for proving separations such as
\(\mathrm{P}\subsetneq\mathrm{PSPACE}\),  
or a natural separation such as 
\(\mathrm{BPP}\subsetneq\mathrm{BQP}\). 


\textcolor{funblue}{Pessimistic}.
Because such separations imply major open lower bounds in classical complexity theory, 
% proving an unconditional quantum speed-up over classical polynomial-time algorithms may be very difficult.  % i mean we know that there are speed ups, but the point is they are not speedups in the sense intractiblae->tractable... so maybei
% proving that a natural decision problem is efficiently solvable quantumly but not by any randomized classical polynomial-time algorithm might be very difficult.
proving 
% rigorously 
that quantum computers can efficiently solve natural problems that are intractable for randomized classical polynomial-time algorithms might be very difficult.

\textcolor{funblue}{More pessimistic}.
If
\(
\mathrm{P}=\mathrm{PSPACE}
\),
then
\(
\mathrm{BQP}=\mathrm{P}
\)
as decision-problem classes, so polynomial-time quantum computers would offer no asymptotic decision-problem advantage over deterministic classical computers.
However, this equality is considered very unlikely.
% but it shows why the inclusion
% \(
% \mathrm{BQP}\subseteq\mathrm{PSPACE}
% \)
% makes unconditional separations hard to prove.
% \end{remark}







\clearpage




\end{document}
